The following <strong>list of cat breeds</strong> includes domestic cat <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felid_hybrid#Domestic_Cat_.26_hybridization">domestic/wild hybrids</a>. The list includes established breeds recognized by various <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cat_registry">cat registries</a>, new and experimental breeds, distinct domestic populations not being actively developed, and lapsed breeds.
Inconsistency in breed classification among registries means that an individual animal may be considered different breeds by different registries. For example, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_International_Cat_Association">The International Cat Association</a>s Himalayan is considered a colorpoint version of the Persian by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cat_Fanciers_Association">Cat Fanciers Association</a> while the CFA&apos;s Javanese is considered a color variation of the Balinese by TICA.
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_short-haired">domestic short-haired</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_long-haired_cat">domestic long-haired cat</a> are not breeds but terms used in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cat_fancy">cat fancy</a> to describe cats of a general type that do not belong to a particular breed.
There are hundreds of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_pig">domestic pig</a> <em>(Sus scrofa domesticus)</em>.
<strong>Dog breeds</strong> are dogs that have relatively uniform physical characteristics developed under controlled conditions by humans, with breeding animals selected for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/phenotype">phenotypic</a> traits such as size, coat color, structure, and behavior.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="Irion, D" data-year="2003" data-journal="Journal of Heredity" data-volume="94" data-issue="1" data-pages="81&#x2013;7" data-doi="10.1093/jhered/esg004" data-pmid="12692167">Analysis of Genetic Variation in 28 Dog Breed Populations With 100 Microsatellite Markers</span></ref> The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/F&#xE9;d&#xE9;ration_Cynologique_Internationale">F&#xE9;d&#xE9;ration Cynologique Internationale</a> recognizes over 400 pure dog breeds.
Other uses of the term breed when referring to dogs may include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/purebred_dog">pure breed</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog_crossbreed">cross-breeds</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mixed_breed_dog">mixed breeds</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Landrace#dogs">natural breeds</a>.
This is an alphabetical <strong>list of pigeon breeds</strong>. Pigeons and doves are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbidae">Columbidae</a>. Doves tend to be smaller and pigeons larger, although this distinction is not consistently applied.
The birds listed here are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breeds">breeds</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_pigeon">domestic pigeon</a> (<em>Columba livia domestica</em>). Other Columbidae <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> (e.g., the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barbary_dove">Barbary dove</a>, <em>Streptopelia risoria</em>) have been developed into breeds, but these are generally simple color variations. See also <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Columbidae_species">List of Columbidae species</a>.
<strong>Albatrosses</strong>, of the biological <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Diomedeidae</strong>, are large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seabird">seabird</a>s allied to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/procellariidae">procellariids</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/storm_petrel">storm petrel</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/diving_petrel">diving petrel</a>s in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Procellariiformes">Procellariiformes</a> (the tubenoses). They range widely in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> and the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific</a>. They are absent from the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic</a>, although <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fossil">fossil</a> remains show they once occurred there and occasional <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vagrancy_(biology)">vagrants</a> are found. Albatrosses are among the largest of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_flight">flying</a> birds, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/great_albatross">great albatross</a>es (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Diomedea</em>) have the largest wingspans of any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> birds, reaching up to [12ftm1]. The albatrosses are usually regarded as falling into four <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a>, but there is disagreement over the number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.
Albatrosses are highly efficient in the air, using <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dynamic_soaring">dynamic soaring</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/slope_soaring">slope soaring</a> to cover great distances with little exertion. They feed on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/squid">squid</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/krill">krill</a> by either scavenging, surface seizing or diving. Albatrosses are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_colony">colonial</a>, nesting for the most part on remote oceanic islands, often with several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> nesting together. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pair_bond">Pair bond</a>s between males and females form over several years, with the use of &quot;ritualised dances&quot;, and will last for the life of the pair. A <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breeding_season">breeding season</a> can take over a year from laying to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fledge">fledging</a>, with a single <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_egg">egg</a> laid in each breeding attempt. A <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laysan_albatross">Laysan albatross</a>, named <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wisdom_(albatross)">Wisdom</a>, on Midway Island is recognised as the oldest wild bird in the world; she was first banded in 1956 by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandler_Robbins">Chandler Robbins</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-last="Pattison" data-first="Darcy" data-authorlink="Darcy Pattison" data-url="http://albatross.darcypattison.com " data-="" publisher="Darcypattison.com (website of Darcy Pattison, author of book about Wisdom) " accessdate="23 April 2014">The oldest bird in the world</span></ref>
Of the 22 species of albatrosses recognised by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN">IUCN</a>, all are listed as at some level of concern; 3 species are Critically Endangered, 5 species are Endangered, 7 species are Near Threatened, and 7 species are Vulnerable.  Numbers of albatrosses have declined in the past due to harvesting for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/feather">feather</a>s, but today the albatrosses are threatened by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/introduced_species">introduced species</a>, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rat">rat</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/feral_cat">feral cat</a>s that attack eggs, chicks and nesting adults; by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollution">pollution</a>; by a serious decline in fish stocks in many regions largely due to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/overfishing">overfishing</a>; and by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/longline_fishing">longline fishing</a>. Longline fisheries pose the greatest threat, as feeding birds are attracted to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bait_(luring_substance)">bait</a>, become hooked on the lines, and drown. Identified <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_stakeholder">stakeholders</a> such as governments, conservation organisations and people in the fishing industry are all working toward reducing this <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bycatch">bycatch</a>.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image:Meyers_b14_s0378a.jpg">right</a>]]
This is a <strong>list of domestic breeds of sheep</strong>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestic_sheep">Domestic sheep</a> (<em>Ovis aries</em>) are partially derived from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouflon">mouflon</a> (<em>Ovis orientalis</em>) stock, and have diverged sufficiently to be considered a different species.
Over 800 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breeds">breeds</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle">cattle</a> are recognized worldwide, some of which <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/adaptation">adapted</a> to the local <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/climate">climate</a>, others which were bred by humans for specialized uses.<ref><a href="http://www.ecoshelters.com.au/bulletins/cattlebreeds.html">Cattle Breeds of the World</a></ref>
Breeds fall into two main types, regarded as either two closely related <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, or two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> of one species.  <em>Bos indicus</em> (or <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bos_taurus_indicus">Bos taurus indicus</a></em>) cattle, also called zebu, are adapted to hot climates.  <em>Bos taurus</em> (or <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bos_taurus_taurus">Bos taurus taurus</a></em>) are the typical cattle of Europe, north-eastern Asia, and parts of Africa &#x2013; they are referred to in this list as &quot;taurine&quot; cattle, and many are adapted to cooler climates.  <em>Taurus/indicus</em> hybrids are widely bred in many warmer regions, combining characteristics of both the ancestral types (such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sanga_cattle">Sanga cattle</a> of Africa).
In some parts of the world further species of cattle are found (both as wild and domesticated animals), and some of these are related so closely to taurine and indicus cattle that inter<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybrids</a> have been bred.  Examples include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dwarf_Lulu">Dwarf Lulu</a> cattle of the mountains of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nepal">Nepal</a> with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/yak">yak</a> blood,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-url="http://www.ingentaconnect.com/content/bsc/asj/2004/00000075/00000002/art00003" data-last="Takeda" data-first="K." data-author2="Satoh M. " data-author3="Neopane S.P. " data-author4="Kuwar B.S. " data-author5="Joshi H.D. " data-author6="Shrestha N.P. " data-author7="Fujise H. " data-author8="Tasai M. " data-author9="Tagami T. " data-author10=" Hanada H. " data-journal="Animal Science Journal" data-volume="75" data-issue="2" data-date="April 2004" data-pages="103&#x2013;110(8)" data-doi="10.1111/j.1740-0929.2004.00163.x">Mitochondrial DNA analysis of Nepalese domestic dwarf cattle Lulu</span></ref> the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beefalo">Beefalo</a> of North America with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bison">bison</a> genes, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selembu">Selembu</a> breed of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India">India</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bhutan">Bhutan</a> with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gayal">gayal</a> genes. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madura_cattle">Madura</a> breed of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia">Indonesia</a> may have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/banteng">banteng</a> in its parentage.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-url="http://www.cabi.org/bk_bookdisplay.asp?PID" data-first="J.A. " data-last="Lenstra" data-author2="D.G. Bradley " data-date="June 1999" data-isbn="978-0-85199-258-7">Systematics and Phylogeny of Cattle</span></ref>  In addition to these fertile hybrids, there are infertile crossings such as the  male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dzo">Dzo</a> of Nepal, a cattle-yak crossing which is bred for agricultural work - like the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mule">mule</a> they have to be continually bred from the parent species.
The <strong>African buffalo</strong> or <strong>Cape buffalo</strong> (<em>Syncerus caffer</em>), is a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>n bovine.(ref: MSW3) It is not closely related to the slightly larger <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wild_Asian_water_buffalo">wild Asian water buffalo</a>, and its ancestry remains unclear. <em>Syncerus caffer caffer</em>, the Cape buffalo, is the typical subspecies, and the largest one, found in South and East Africa.  <em>S. c. nanus</em> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_Forest_Buffalo">forest buffalo</a>) is the smallest subspecies, common in forest areas of Central and West Africa while  <em>S. c. brachyceros</em> is in West Africa and <em>S. c.  aequinoctialis</em> is in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savannas">savannas</a> of Central Africa. The adult buffalo&apos;s horns are its characteristic feature; they have fused bases, forming a continuous bone shield referred to as a &quot;boss&quot;.  It is widely regarded as a very dangerous animal, as it gores and kills over 200 people every year. 
The African buffalo is not an ancestor of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cattle">domestic cattle</a>, and is only distantly related to other larger bovines. Owing to its unpredictable nature, which makes it highly dangerous to humans, the African buffalo has never been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> unlike its Asian counterpart, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/water_buffalo">water buffalo</a>. Other than humans, African Cape buffaloes have few <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s aside from lions and are capable of defending themselves. Being a member of the &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_five_game">big five&quot; game</a> family, the Cape buffalo is a sought-after trophy in hunting.

<strong>Blackbird</strong>, <strong>blackbirds</strong>, <strong>black bird</strong> or <strong>black birds</strong> may refer to:
&#x2020;<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_antiquus">B. antiquus</a></em><br><em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Bison">B. bison</a></em><br><em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wisent">B. bonasus</a></em><br>&#x2020;<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_latifrons">B. latifrons</a></em><br>&#x2020;<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_occidentalis">B. occidentalis</a></em><br>&#x2020;<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_palaeosinensis">B. palaeosinensis</a></em><br>&#x2020;<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steppe_Wisent">B. priscus</a></em>}}
<strong>Bison</strong> or <strong>buffalo</strong> are large, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a>s in the genus <em>Bison</em> within the subfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovinae">Bovinae</a>.
Two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> and four <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extinction">extinct</a> species are recognized. Of the four extinct species, three were North American: <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_antiquus">Bison antiquus</a></em>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_latifrons">B. latifrons</a></em>, and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bison_occidentalis">B. occidentalis</a></em>. The fourth, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steppe_Wisent">B. priscus</a></em>, ranged across <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/steppe">steppe</a> environments from Western Europe, through Central Asia, and onto North America.
Of the two surviving species, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_bison">American bison</a>, <em>B. bison</em>, found only in North America, is the more numerous. Although sometimes referred to historically as a &quot;buffalo&quot;, it is only distantly related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_buffalo">true buffalo</a>. The North American species is composed of two subspecies, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plains_bison">plains bison</a>, <em>B. b. bison</em>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wood_bison">wood bison</a>, <em>B. b. athabascae</em>, which is the namesake of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wood_Buffalo_National_Park">Wood Buffalo National Park</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada">Canada</a>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_bison">European bison</a> <em>B. bonasus</em>, or wisent, is found in Europe and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, reintroduced after being extinct in the wild.
While all bison species are classified in their own <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a>, they are sometimes bred with domestic cattle (genus <em>Bos</em>) and produce fertile offspring called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beefalo">beefalo</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zubron">zubron</a>.

The <strong>bluebirds</strong> are a group of medium-sized, mostly insectivorous or omnivorous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a> in the order of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Passerines">Passerines</a> in the genus <em>Sialia</em> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thrush_(bird)">thrush</a> family (Turdidae). Bluebirds are one of the few thrush genera in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>. They have blue, or blue and rose beige, plumage. Female birds are less brightly colored than males, although color patterns are similar and there is no noticeable difference in size between the two sexes. 
Species:
<strong>Chameleons</strong> or <strong>chamaeleons</strong> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Chamaeleonidae</strong>) are a distinctive and highly specialized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/old_world">old world</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lizard">lizard</a>s with 202 species described as of June 2015.(ref: Glaw 2015)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" title=" Taxonomic checklist of chameleons (Squamata: Chamaeleonidae) " author1=" Glaw, F." journal="Vertebrate Zoology " year="2015 " volume="65 " issue=" 2 " pages="167&#x2013;246 "></span> These species come in a range of colours, and many species have the ability to change colours. Chameleons are distinguished by their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zygodactyl">zygodactyl</a>ous feet; their very long, highly modified, rapidly extrudable tongues; their swaying gait; and crests or horns on their distinctively shaped heads. Most species, the larger ones in particular, also have a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prehensile">prehensile</a> tail. Chameleons&apos; eyes are independently mobile, but in aiming at a prey item, they focus forward in coordination, affording the animal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stereoscopic_vision">stereoscopic vision</a>. Chameleons are adapted for climbing and visual hunting. They are found in warm habitats that range from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rain_forest">rain forest</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a> conditions, various species occurring in Africa, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, southern Europe, and across southern Asia as far as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>. They also have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introduced_species">introduced</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/California">California</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Florida">Florida</a>, and often are kept as household pets.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image:Monarch_Butterfly_Danaus_plexippus_Vertical_Caterpillar_2000px.jpg">thumb</a> (<em>Danaus plexippus</em>) caterpillar feeding on a leaf of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swamp_Milkweed">Swamp Milkweed</a> (Asclepias incarnata) in a private garden in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania">Lancaster, Pennsylvania</a>.]]
<strong>Caterpillar</strong> is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> for the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e of members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a> (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a> order comprising <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/butterfly">butterflies</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moth">moth</a>s).
As with most common names, the application of the word is arbitrary and the larvae of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sawfly">sawflies</a> commonly are called caterpillars as well.(ref: Ormerod1892)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Eleanor Anne Ormerod" data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-year="1892" data-publisher="Simpkin, Marshall, Hamilton, Kent &amp; Co.">A text-book of agricultural entomology: being a guide to methods of insect life and means of prevention of insect ravage for the use of agriculturists and agricultural students</span>(ref: Anderson1960)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Roger Fabian Anderson" data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-date="January 1960" data-publisher="Wiley" data-isbn="978-0-471-02739-3">Forest and Shade Tree Entomology</span>
Caterpillars of most species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivore">herbivorous</a>, but not all; some are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insectivore">insectivorous</a>, even cannibalistic. Some feed on other animal products; for example clothes moths feed on wool, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceratophaga_vastella">horn moths</a> feed on the hooves and horns of dead <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ungulate">ungulate</a>s.
Caterpillars as a rule are voracious feeders and many of them are among the most serious of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agriculture">agricultural</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism)">pests</a>. In fact many moth <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> are best known in their caterpillar stages because of the damage they cause to fruits and other agricultural produce, whereas the moths are obscure and do no direct harm. Conversely, various species of caterpillar are valued as sources of silk, as human or animal food, or for biological control of pest plants.
The <strong>Bovidae</strong> are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">biological family</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cloven-hoof">cloven-hoof</a>ed, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ruminant">ruminant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s that includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bison">bison</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_buffalo">African buffalo</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/water_buffalo">water buffalo</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antelope">antelope</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gazelle">gazelle</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goat">goat</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muskox">muskox</a>en, and domestic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle">cattle</a>. A member of this family is called a <strong>bovid</strong>. Consisting of 143 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">extant</a> species and 300 known extinct species, the family Bovidae consists of eight major <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamilies">subfamilies</a> apart from the disputed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peleinae">Peleinae</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tibetan_antelope">Pantholopinae</a>. The family evolved 20 million years ago, in the early <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miocene">Miocene</a>.
The bovids show great variation in size and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coat_(animal)">pelage</a> colouration. Excepting some <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polled_livestock">domesticated forms</a>, all male bovids have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horn_(anatomy)">horns</a>, and in many species females possess horns, too. The size and shape of the horns vary greatly, but the basic structure is always one or more pairs of simple bony protrusions without branches, often having a spiral, twisted or fluted form, each covered in a permanent sheath of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/keratin">keratin</a>. Most bovids bear 30 to 32 teeth.
Most bovids are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/diurnality">diurnal</a>. Social activity and feeding usually peak during dawn and dusk. Bovids usually rest before dawn, during midday, and after dark. They have various methods of social organization and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/social_behaviour">social behaviour</a>, which are classified into solitary and gregarious behaviour. Bovids use different forms of vocal, olfactory, and tangible communication. Most species alternately feed and ruminate throughout the day. While small bovids forage in dense and closed habitat, larger species feed upon high-fiber vegetation in open grasslands. Most bovids are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygynous_mating_systems">polygynous</a>.  Mature bovids mate at least once a year, and smaller species may even mate twice.  In some species,neonate bovids remain hidden for a week to two months, regularly nursed by their mothers; in other species, neonates are followers, accompanying their dams, rather than tending to remain hidden.
Most of the diverse bovid species occur in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>. The maximum concentration is in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savanna">savanna</a>s of eastern Africa. A few bovid species also occur in Europe, Asia, and North America. The Bovidae include three of the five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication">domesticated</a> mammals whose use has spread outside their original ranges, namely cattle, sheep, and goats. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dairy_products">Dairy products</a> such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/milk">milk</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/butter">butter</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cheese">cheese</a> are manufactured largely from domestic cattle. Bovids also provide <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leather">leather</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/meat">meat</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wool">wool</a>.
<strong>Chinchillas</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crepuscular">crepuscular</a> (most active around dawn and dusk) <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s, slightly larger and more robust than <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ground_squirrel">ground squirrel</a>s. They are native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andes">Andes</a> mountains in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a> and live in colonies called &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herd">herd</a>s&quot; at high elevations up to [4270mftabbr=onsigfig=3]. Historically, chinchillas lived in the Andes of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peru">Peru</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chile">Chile</a>, but today colonies in the wild remain only in Peru and Chile at elevations of over [5000mftabbr=onsigfig=2].<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-journal="Columbia Electronic Encyclopedia, 6th Edition" data-year="2011"></span></ref>(ref: :0)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-title=" The Encylopedia of World Wildlife" data-last=" Briggs" data-first=" Mike" data-publisher=" Parragon" data-year=" 2005" data-isbn=" 1405456809" data-location=" " data-pages=" 78" data-last2=" Briggs" data-first2=" Peggy"></span>  Along with their relatives, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/viscacha">viscacha</a>s, they make up the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinchillidae">Chinchillidae</a>.
The chinchilla (whose name literally means &quot;little chincha&quot;) is named after the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chincha_Culture">Chincha</a> people of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andes">Andes</a>, who once wore its dense, velvet-like fur.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" url="http://www.davidson-chinchillas.co.uk/pages/what_is_a_chinchilla_.php " title="What Is A Chinchilla? " publisher="Davidson Chinchillas " accessdate="2008-02-01 "></span></ref> By the end of the 19th century, chinchillas had become quite rare due to hunting for their ultra-soft <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fur">fur</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" url="http://chinchilla.co/chinchilla-facts/ " title="Chinchilla Facts &#x2013; The Top 10 Interesting Facts About Chinchillas " publisher="Chinchilla.co " accessdate="2013-12-01 "></span></ref> Most chinchillas currently used by the fur industry for clothing and other accessories are farm-raised.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-url="http://www.chincare.com/HealthLifestyle/JEJ/JEJExtirpation1995.pdf" data-doi="10.1016/0006-3207(95)00116-6" data-year="1996" data-last1="Jim&#xE9;nez" data-first1="Jaime E." data-journal="Biological Conservation" data-volume="77" data-page="1">The extirpation and current status of wild chinchillas Chinchilla lanigera and C. Brevicaudata</span></ref> Chinchilla fur is considered one of the most valuable furs in the world.(ref: :0)
Chinchillas are currently listed as a critically endangered species by the IUCN Red List of Threatened Species due to a severe population loss approximated at a 90% global population loss over the last 15 years. The severe population decline has been caused by chinchilla hunting by humans.(ref: iucn)
  }}
The <strong>chicken</strong> (<em>Gallus gallus domesticus</em>) is a  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fowl">fowl</a>, a subspecies of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red_junglefowl">red junglefowl</a>. As one of the most common and widespread <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_animals">domestic animals</a>, with a population of more than 24 billion in 2003, there are more chickens in the world than any other species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>. Humans keep chickens primarily as a source of food, consuming both <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chicken_(food)">their meat</a> and their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/egg_(food)">eggs</a>.
The earliest known domestication occurred in Northern China 8,000 BCE determined from archaeological chicken bones.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.pnas.org/content/111/49/17564" data-publisher="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America PNAS, Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences">Early Holocene chicken domestication in northern China</span></ref> Previously genetic studies have pointed to multiple maternal origins in Southeast, East, and South Asia, but with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> found in the Americas, Europe, the Middle East and Africa originating in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.   From India, the domesticated chicken was imported to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lydia">Lydia</a> in western <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>, and to Greece by the fifth century BC.<ref>Maguelonne Toussaint-Samat, (Anthea Bell, translator) <em>The History of Food</em>, Ch. 11 &quot;The History of Poultry&quot;, revised ed. 2009, p. 306.</ref> Fowl had been known in Egypt since the mid-15th century BC, with the &quot;bird that gives birth every day&quot; having come to Egypt from the land between <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syria">Syria</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shinar">Shinar</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babylonia">Babylonia</a>, according to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/annals_of_Thutmose_III">annals of Thutmose III</a>.<ref>Howard Carter, &quot;An Ostracon Depicting a Red Jungle-Fowl (The Earliest Known Drawing of the Domestic Cock)&quot; <em>The Journal of Egyptian Archaeology</em>, <strong>9</strong>.1/2 (April 1923), pp. 1-4.</ref><ref>Pritchard, &quot;The Asiatic Campaigns of Thutmose III&quot; <em>Ancient Near East Texts related to the Old Testament</em>, p240.</ref>(ref: RoehrigDreyfus2005)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last1="Roehrig" data-first1="Catharine H. " data-last2="Dreyfus" data-first2="Ren&#xE9;e " data-last3="Keller" data-first3="Cathleen A. " data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-year="2005" data-publisher="Metropolitan Museum of Art" data-location="New York" data-isbn="978-1-58839-173-5" data-page="268">Hatshepsut: From Queen to Pharaoh</span>
The <strong>wild boar</strong> (<em>Sus scrofa</em>), also known as the <strong>wild swine</strong><ref name="heptner1988">Heptner, V. G. ; Nasimovich, A. A. ; Bannikov, A. G. ; Hoffman, R. S. (1988) https://archive.org/stream/mammalsofsovietu11988gept#page/18/mode/2up <em>Mammals of the Soviet Union</em>, Volume I, Washington, D.C. : Smithsonian Institution Libraries and National Science Foundation, pp. 19-82</ref> or <strong>Eurasian wild pig</strong>(ref: oliver1993)<em>Sus scrofa</em>). In Oliver, W. L. R., ed., <em>Pigs, Peccaries, and Hippos &#x2013; 1993 Status Survey and Conservation Action Plan</em>, 112-121. IUCN/SSC Pigs and Peccaries Specialist Group, ISBN 2-8317-0141-4 is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suidae">suid</a> native to much of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Africa">North Africa</a>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greater_Sunda_Islands">Greater Sunda Islands</a>. Human intervention has spread its range further, making the species one of the widest-ranging mammals in the world, as well as the most widely spread <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suina">suiform</a>.(ref: oliver1993)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/least_concern">least concern</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN">IUCN</a>. The animal probably originated in Southeast Asia during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Pleistocene">Early Pleistocene</a>, and outcompeted other suid species as it spread throughout the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a>.
As of 2005, up to 16 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> are recognised, which are divided into four regional groupings based on skull height and lacrimal bone length. The species lives in matriarchal societies consisting of interrelated females and their young (both male and female). Fully grown males are usually solitary outside of the breeding season.(ref: marsan75)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grey_wolf">grey wolf</a> is the wild boars main predator throughout most of its range except in the Far East, where it is replaced by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tiger">tiger</a>. It has a long history of association with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>s, having been the ancestor of most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_pig">domestic pig</a> breeds and a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big-game_hunting">big-game animal</a> for millennia.
<strong>Carp</strong> are various species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/oily_fish">oily</a><ref><span class="reference" data-type="news" data-url="http://www.food.gov.uk/news/newsarchive/2004/jun/oilyfishdefinition" data-date="2004-06-24" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" food_standards_agency"="">Food Standards Agency&quot;&gt;What&apos;s an oily fish?</span></ref> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/freshwater_fish">freshwater fish</a> of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyprinidae">Cyprinidae</a>, a very large group of fish native to Europe and Asia.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
<strong>Catsharks</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carcharhiniformes">ground shark</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Scyliorhinidae</strong>, with over 150 known <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>. Although they are generally known as <em>catsharks</em>, many species are commonly called <strong>dogfish</strong> or <strong>gato</strong>.  Catsharks are found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/temperate">temperate</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical">tropical</a> seas worldwide, ranging from very shallow <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/intertidal">intertidal</a> waters to depths of [2000mftabbr=on] or more, depending on species.
An <strong>anaconda</strong> is a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snake">snake</a> found in tropical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>. Although the name applies to a group of snakes, it is often used to refer only to one species in particular, the common or green anaconda, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eunectes_murinus">Eunectes murinus</a></em>, which is the largest snake in the world by weight, and the second longest.
<strong>Anaconda</strong> may refer to:
Extant species are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/giant_anteater">giant anteater</a> <em>Myrmecophaga tridactyla</em>, about [1.8mabbr=on] long including the tail; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/silky_anteater">silky anteater</a> <em>Cyclopes didactylus</em>, about [35cmabbr=on] long; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southern_tamandua">southern tamandua</a> or collared anteater <em>Tamandua tetradactyla</em>, about [1.2mabbr=on] long; and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_tamandua">northern tamandua</a> <em>Tamandua mexicana</em> of similar dimensions.
The <strong>bobcat</strong> (<em>Lynx rufus</em>) is a North American <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a> of the cat family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felidae">Felidae</a> that appeared during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irvingtonian">Irvingtonian</a> stage of around 1.8 million years ago (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appearance_Event_Ordination">AEO</a>).<ref><a href="http://paleodb.org/cgi-bin/bridge.pl?action=checkTaxonInfo&amp;taxon_no=46528&amp;is_real_user=1">Paleobiology Database, collection 20397</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Do&#xF1;a_Ana_County,_New_Mexico">Do&#xF1;a Ana County, New Mexico</a>. Authorized and entered by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Alroy">Dr. John Alroy</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macquarie_University">Macquarie University</a>, April 30, 1994.</ref> Containing 12 recognized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a>, it ranges from southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada">Canada</a> to central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a>, including most of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continental_United_States">continental United States</a>. The bobcat is an adaptable <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a> that inhabits wooded areas, as well as semidesert, urban edge, forest edges, and swampland environments. It remains in some of its original range, but local populations are vulnerable to extirpation by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coyote">coyote</a>s and domestic animals. The bobcat is vital for controlling <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pest_(organism)">pest</a> populations. 
With a gray to brown coat, whiskered face, and black-tufted ears, the bobcat resembles the other species of the mid-sized <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lynx">Lynx</a></em> genus. It is smaller on average than the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada_lynx">Canada lynx</a>, with which it shares parts of its range, but is about twice as large as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cat">domestic cat</a>. It has distinctive black bars on its forelegs and a black-tipped, stubby tail, from which it derives its name.
Though the bobcat prefers <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rabbit">rabbit</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hare">hare</a>s, it will hunt anything from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s, chickens, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goose">geese</a> and other birds and small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/deer">deer</a>. Prey selection depends on location and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat">habitat</a>, season, and abundance. Like most cats, the bobcat is territorial and largely solitary, although with some overlap in home ranges. It uses several methods to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/territorial_marking">mark its territorial boundaries</a>, including claw marks and deposits of urine or feces. The bobcat breeds from winter into spring and has a gestation period of about two months.
Although bobcats have been hunted extensively by humans, both for sport and fur, their population has proven resilient though declining in some areas. The elusive predator features in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Native_American_mythology">Native American mythology</a> and the folklore of European settlers.
A <strong>laboratory rat</strong> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rat">rat</a> of the species <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_rat">Rattus norvegicus</a></em> (brown rat) which is bred and kept for scientific research. Laboratory rats have served as an important <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/animal_model">animal model</a> for research in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/psychology">psychology</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/medicine">medicine</a>, and other fields.
<strong>Cattle</strong>&#x2014;colloquially <strong>cows</strong>[group=noteThe noun <em>cattle</em> (which is treated as a plural and has no singular) encompasses both sexes. The singular, <em>cow</em>, unambiguously means the female, the male being <em>bull</em>. The plural feminine form <em>cows</em> is sometimes used colloquially to refer to both sexes collectively, as <em>e.g.</em> in a herd, but that usage can be misleading as the speakers intent may indeed be just the females. The bovine species per se<strong>is clearly dimorphic.]&#x2014;are the most common type of large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ungulates">ungulates</a>.  They are a prominent modern member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovinae">Bovinae</a>, are the most widespread species of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> </strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bos">Bos</a><em>, and are most commonly classified collectively as </em>Bos taurus.  Cattle are raised as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/livestock">livestock</a> for meat (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beef">beef</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/veal">veal</a>), as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy_cattle">dairy animals</a> for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/milk">milk</a> and other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy_product">dairy product</a>s, and as draft animals (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ox">ox</a>en or bullocks that pull <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cart">cart</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plow">plow</a>s and other implements). Other products include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leather">leather</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cow_dung">dung</a> for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/manure">manure</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fuel">fuel</a>. In some regions, such as parts of India, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cattle_in_religion">cattle have significant religious meaning</a>.  From as few as 80 progenitors domesticated in southeast Turkey about 10,500 years ago,(ref: HowNowCow)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Bollongino " data-first1="R. There are two more groups in the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic</a>, and at least two in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. As of 2014, the Californian blue whale population has rebounded to nearly its pre-hunting population.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.iflscience.com/plants-and-animals/california-blue-whales-bounce-back-whaling" data-publisher="">California Blue Whales Bounce Back From Whaling</span></ref>
The <strong>bobolink</strong> (<em>Dolichonyx oryzivorus</em>) is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/icterid">New World blackbird</a> and the only member of the genus <strong><em>Dolichonyx</em></strong>.
