<strong>Dung beetles</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beetle">beetle</a>s that <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coprophagia">feed partly or exclusively</a> on dung. A dung beetle can bury dung 250 times heavier than itself in one night.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://news.biharprabha.com/2014/04/some-less-known-fascinating-facts-about-dung-beetle/" data-work="IANS" data-publisher="news.biharprabha.com" data-accessdate="21 April 2014">Some Less Known Fascinating Facts about Dung Beetle</span></ref>
Many dung beetles, known as <em>rollers</em>, roll dung into round balls, which are used as a food source or brooding chambers. Others, known as <em>tunnelers</em>, bury the dung wherever they find it. A third group, the <em>dwellers</em>, neither roll nor burrow: they simply live in manure. They are often attracted by the dung collected by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/burrowing_owl">burrowing owl</a>s. Dung Beetles can grow to 3&#xA0;cm long and 2&#xA0;cm wide.
All the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> belong to the superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scarabaeoidea">Scarabaeoidea</a>; most of them to the subfamilies <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scarabaeinae">Scarabaeinae</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphodiinae">Aphodiinae</a> of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scarabaeidae">Scarabaeidae</a> (scarab beetles). As most species of Scarabaeinae feed exclusively on feces, that subfamily is often dubbed <em>true dung beetles</em>. There are dung-feeding beetles which belong to other families, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geotrupidae">Geotrupidae</a> (the <em>earth-boring dung beetle</em>). The Scarabaeinae alone comprises more than 5,000 species.<ref>Frolov, A.V. <a href="http://www.zin.ru/animalia/coleoptera/eng/afrsc_ru.htm">&quot;Subfamily Scarabaeinae: atlas of representatives of the tribes (Scarabaeidae)&quot;</a>. Retrieved on 2007-08-02.</ref>
Dung beetles are currently the only known non-human animal to navigate and orient themselves using the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way">Milky Way</a>.(ref: scienceDaily)<span class="reference" data-type="news" data-last="Wits University " data-url="http://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2013/01/130124123203.htm " data-work="ScienceDaily" data-date="24 January 2013 " data-accessdate="25 January 2013">Dung Beetles Follow the Milky Way: Insects Found to Use Stars for Orientation</span><ref name="NatGeo-Milkyway"><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-last="Dell&apos;Amore " data-first="Christine " data-url="http://newswatch.nationalgeographic.com/2013/01/24/dung-beetles-navigate-via-the-milky-way-an-animal-kingdom-first/ " data-work="News Watch" data-publisher="National Geographic Society" data-accessdate="25 January 2013">Dung Beetles Navigate Via the Milky Way, First Known in Animal Kingdom </span></ref>
<strong>Chimpanzees</strong>, colloquially called <strong>chimps</strong>, are two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">extant</a> hominid <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ape">ape</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Pan</em></strong>.  The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_River">Congo River</a> divides the native habitats of the two species:<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://animaldiversity.ummz.umich.edu/site/accounts/information/Pan_troglodytes.html " data-accessdate="2007-08-11 " data-work="Animal Diversity Web (University of Michigan Museum of Zoology)" data-author="Shefferly, N. " data-year="2005"> Pan troglodytes</span></ref>
Chimpanzees are members of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominidae">Hominidae</a>, along with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gorilla">gorilla</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/orangutan">orangutan</a>s. They split from the human line about four to six million years ago, see <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee-human_last_common_ancestor">Chimpanzee-human last common ancestor</a>. Chimpanzees (subtribe <strong>Panina</strong>) are the closest living relatives to humans (subtribe <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominina">Hominina</a>); both are members of the tribe <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominini">Hominini</a>. Chimpanzees are the only known members of the subtribe <strong>Panina</strong>.
The two <em>Pan</em> species, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bonobo">bonobo</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_chimpanzee">common chimpanzee</a>, split about one million years ago. This article uses the individual name when distinguishing one species or the other, and uses <em>Pan</em> or chimpanzee (or chimp) when both or either one is implied.
<strong>Butterflies</strong> are part of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(biology)">class</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lepidoptera">Lepidoptera</a>, along with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moth">moth</a>s. Adult butterflies have large, often brightly coloured wings, and conspicuous, fluttering flight. The group comprises the large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/superfamily_(zoology)">superfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Papilionoidea">Papilionoidea</a>, along with two smaller groups, the skippers (superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hesperioidea">Hesperioidea</a>) and the moth-butterflies (superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hedyloidea">Hedyloidea</a>). Butterfly fossils date to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Palaeocene">Palaeocene</a>, about 56 million years ago.
Butterflies have the typical four-stage insect life cycle. Winged adults lay eggs on the food plant on which their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e, known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caterpillar">caterpillar</a>s, will feed. The caterpillars grow, sometimes very rapidly, and when fully developed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pupa">pupa</a>te in a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chrysalis">chrysalis</a>. When <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> is complete, the pupal skin splits, the adult insect climbs out and, after its wings have expanded and dried, it flies off. Some butterflies, especially in the tropics, have several generations in a year, while others have a single generation, and a few in cold locations may take several years to pass through their whole life cycle.
Butterflies are often <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)">polymorphic</a>, and many species make use of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camouflage">camouflage</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mimicry">mimicry</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aposematism">aposematism</a> to evade their predators. Some, like the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monarch_(butterfly)">monarch</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vanessa_cardui">painted lady</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect_migration">migrate</a> over long distances. Some butterflies have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parasitoid">parasitoidal</a> relationships with organisms including protozoans, flies, ants, and other invertebrates, and are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predation">predated</a> by vertebrates. Some species are pests because in their larval stages they can damage domestic crops or trees; other species are agents of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollination">pollination</a> of some plants, and caterpillars of a few butterflies (e.g., <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miletinae">harvesters</a>) eat harmful insects. Culturally, butterflies are a popular motif in the visual and literary arts.
<strong>Centipedes</strong> (from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_language">Latin</a> prefix <em>[la<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wikt:centi-">centi-</a>]</em>, &quot;hundred&quot;, and <em>[la<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:pes">pes, pedis</a>]</em>, <em>&quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/foot">foot</a>&quot;</em>) are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arthropod">arthropod</a>s belonging to the class <strong>Chilopoda</strong> of the subphylum <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myriapoda">Myriapoda</a>. They are elongated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metamerism_(biology)">metameric</a> creatures with one pair of legs per body segment. Despite the name, centipedes can have a varying number of legs, ranging from 30 to 354. Centipedes always have an odd number of pairs of legs.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last="Lloyd " data-first="John " data-year="2006 " data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-location="London " data-publisher="Bloomsbury House " data-page="119 " data-isbn="0571273785 " data-accessdate="June 10, 2014">The Book of General Ignorance </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last="Arthur " data-first="W. " data-year="2002 " data-journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" heredity_(journal)"_data-heredity"="">Heredity (journal)&quot; data-Heredity =&quot;undefined&quot; data-volume=&quot;89 &quot; data-issue=&quot;4 &quot; data-pages=&quot;239&#x2013;246 &quot; data-pmid=&quot;12242638 &quot; data-doi=&quot;10.1038/sj.hdy.6800139&quot;&gt;The interaction between developmental bias and natural selection from centipede segmentation to a general hypothesis </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Arthur " data-first1="Wallace " data-last2="Chapman " data-first2="Ariel D. " data-year="2005 " data-journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" bioessays"="">Bioessays &quot; data-volume=&quot;27 &quot; data-issue=&quot;6 &quot; data-pages=&quot;653&#x2013;660 &quot; data-pmid=&quot;15892117 &quot; data-doi=&quot;10.1002/bies.20234&quot;&gt;The centipede <em>Strigamia maritima</em>: what it can tell us about development and evolution of segmentation </span></ref> Therefore, there is no centipede with exactly 100 legs. A key trait uniting this group is a pair of venom claws or <em>forcipules</em> formed from a modified first <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/appendage">appendage</a>. Centipedes are a predominantly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivore">carnivorous</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taxon">taxon</a>.(ref: Biology)<span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last="Lewis " data-first="J. G. E. " data-year="2007 " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" cambridge_university_press"="">Cambridge University Press &quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-521-03411-1 &quot; data-url=&quot;http://books.google.com/?id&quot;&gt;The Biology of Centipedes </span>[168]
Size can range from a few millimetres in the smaller lithobiomorphs and geophilomorphs to about [30cmabbr=on] in the largest scolopendromorphs. Centipedes can be found in a wide variety of environments. Centipedes normally have a drab coloration combining shades of brown and red. Cavernicolous (cave-dwelling) and subterranean species may lack pigmentation and many tropical scolopendromorphs have bright <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aposematic">aposematic</a> colours. 
Worldwide, there are estimated to be 8,000 species of centipede,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Adis " data-first1="Joachim " data-last2="Harvey " data-first2="Mark S. " data-year="2000 " data-journal="Studies on Neotropical Fauna and Environment " data-volume="35 " data-issue="2 " data-pages="139&#x2013;141 " data-doi="10.1076/0165-0521(200008)35:2;1-9;FT139">How many Arachnida and Myriapoda are there worldwide and in Amazonia? </span></ref> of which 3,000 have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/alpha_taxonomy">described</a>. Centipedes have a wide geographical range where they even reach beyond the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Circle">Arctic Circle</a>.(ref: Biology)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical_rainforest">tropical rainforest</a>s to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a>s. Within these habitats, centipedes require a moist micro-habitat because they lack the waxy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cuticle">cuticle</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arachnid">arachnid</a>s, and so lose water rapidly through the skin. Accordingly, they are found in soil and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leaf_litter">leaf litter</a>, under stones and dead wood, and inside logs. Centipedes are among the largest terrestrial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/invertebrate">invertebrate</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s and often contribute significantly to the invertebrate predatory biomass in terrestrial ecosystems.
<strong>Caribou</strong> (North America) refers to any of several North American subspecies, ecotypes, populations, and herds[CroninMacNeilPatton2005] of the species <em>Rangifer tarandus</em>.[Klein1980] In North America caribou range in size from the smallest, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peary_caribou">Peary caribou</a>, to the largest, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/boreal_woodland_caribou">boreal woodland caribou</a>. The North American range of caribou extends from Alaska, through the Yukon, the Northwest Territories, Nunavut, into the boreal forest and south through the Canadian Rockies and the Columbia and Selkirk Mountains.[Eder2011page=81] Barren-ground, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Porcupine_caribou">Porcupine caribou</a> and Peary caribou live in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tundra">tundra</a> while the shy woodland caribou, prefers the boreal forest. Two major subspecies in North America, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Porcupine_caribou"><em>R. t. granti</em></a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barren-ground_caribou"><em>R. t. groenlandicus</em></a> form large herds and undertake lengthy seasonal migrations from birthing grounds, to summer and winter feeding grounds in the tundra and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taiga">taiga</a>. The migrations of <em>R. t. granti</em> Porcupine herds are among the longest of any terrestrial mammal.[Eder2011page=81] Barren-land caribou are also found in western Greenland, but the larger herds are in Alaska, the Northwest Territories and Nunavut.[Grubb2005]
The circumpolar species itself, <em>Rangifer tarandus</em>, at a global level, is listed by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (ICUN) &quot;as Least Concern due to a wide circumpolar distribution and presumed large populations.&quot;[HenttonenTikhonov2008] The populations of subspecies, ecotypes, populations and herds of caribou in North America are in decline[Nunatsiaq News2013][Campbell2010][McCloskey2011page=76] and one subspecies, the iconic boreal woodland caribou, has been listed by COSEWIC as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Threatened_species">threatened</a> since 2002.[COSEWIC2011]
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_River_(Quebec)">George River caribou herd</a> (GRCH), in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ungava_Peninsula">Ungava</a> region of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quebec">Quebec</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labrador">Labrador</a> in eastern Canada was once the world&apos;s largest herd with 800&#x2009;000&#x2013;900&#x2009;000 animals.  By 2012 the herd numbered 27&#x2009;600 and declined to 14&#x2009;200 animals in 2014.(ref: DoENfld_2014)
The meta-population of the more sedentary subspecies <em>R. t. caribou</em> or woodland caribou spans the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boreal_forest_of_Canada">boreal forest</a> from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northwest_Territories">Northwest Territories</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Labrador">Labrador</a>. They are shy animals whose main food source is arboreal lichens[McCloskey2011page=76] of the mature forests[CPAWSNWT2010] and mainly live in marshes, bogs, lakes, and river regions.[Natural Resources Canada2000page=14][CullingCulling2006page=1] Since it takes hundreds of years for a biomass of tree lichen to be adequate to sustain boreal woodland caribou populations, deforestation is a major factor in the decline of their numbers.[McCloskey2011page=76] The historic range of the boreal woodland caribou covered over half of present-day Canada,[Canadian Parks and Wilderness Society and The David Suzuki Foundation2013] stretching from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska">Alaska</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a> and as far south as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_England">New England</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idaho">Idaho</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state)">Washington</a>. The smallest subspecies in North America, the Peary caribou is found in the High and Low Arctic, in the Northwest Territories&#x2014;particularly, Banks Island and in Nunavut&#x2014;particularly, Baffin Island.
The caribou is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species">specialist</a> that is well adapted to cooler climates with hollow-hair fur that covers almost all of its body including its nose, and provides insulation in winter and flotation for swimming.[Eder2011page=81] Caribou can reach a speed of [60-80km/habbr=on]. Young caribou can already outrun an Olympic sprinter when only a day old.[Hoare2009] The caribou&apos;s favourite winter food is fruticose <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cladonia_rangiferina">deer lichen</a>. Seventy percent of the diet of woodland caribou consists of arboreal lichen which take hundreds of years to grow and are therefore only found in mature forests.[CPAWSNWT2010]
Although there are many variations in colour and size, <em>Canadian Geographic</em> magazine states that in general, barren-ground caribou have larger antlers than the woodland caribou subspecies. Barren-ground caribou have large distinguishing white patches of fur that extend beyond the neck onto the back, a white muzzle and a face that is darker than the rest of the body. Their fur is sandy-beige in winter and light brown in summer. The woodland caribou have a wider more compact body and wider antlers. The coat is a rich dark brown in summer and dark grey in winter. Both the barren-ground and woodland caribou often have white &quot;socks&quot; above their hooves.[Canadian Geographic2007] On average the male weighs [90-110kglbabbr=on] and measures [0.9-1.7mftabbr=on] in shoulder height. The woodland caribou are the largest and the Peary caribou the smallest. The largest Alaskan male Porcupine caribou can weigh as much as [310kglb].
Female caribou can live up to 17 years and male caribou for four years less.[Bergerud2014]
Both sexes grow <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antler">antler</a>s, though in a some woodland caribou populations, females lack antlers completely. Antlers are larger in males.
Caribou are an integral part of First Nations and Inuit oral histories and legends including the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gwich&apos;in">Gwich&apos;in</a> creation story of how Gwich&#x2019;in people and the caribou separated from a single entity.[First Voices2014]
<strong>Crabs</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decapoda">decapod</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crustacean">crustacean</a>s of the infraorder <strong>Brachyura</strong>, which typically have a very short projecting &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a>&quot; (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/abdomen">abdomen</a>) ([<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:&#x3B2;&#x3C1;&#x3B1;&#x3C7;&#x3CD;&#x3C2;">&#x3B2;&#x3C1;&#x3B1;&#x3C7;&#x3CD;&#x3C2;</a> / <em>brachys</em>] = short,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc" data-author="Henry George Liddell &amp; Robert Scott " data-work="A Greek&#x2013;English Lexicon " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" perseus_digital_library"="">Perseus Digital Library &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;May 24, 2010&quot;&gt;&#x3B2;&#x3C1;&#x3B1;&#x3C7;&#x3CD;&#x3C2; </span></ref> [el<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:&#x3BF;&#x1F50;&#x3C1;&#x3AC;">&#x3BF;&#x1F50;&#x3C1;&#x3AC;</a> / <em>&#x3BF;ura</em>] = tail<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc" data-author="Henry George Liddell &amp; Robert Scott " data-work="A Greek&#x2013;English Lexicon " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" perseus_digital_library"="">Perseus Digital Library &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;May 24, 2010&quot;&gt;&#x3BF;&#x1F50;&#x3C1;&#x3AC; </span></ref>), usually entirely hidden under the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thorax">thorax</a>. They live in all the world&apos;s oceans, in fresh water, and on land, are generally covered with a thick exoskeleton and have a single pair of claws. Many other animals with similar names &#x2013; such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hermit_crab">hermit crab</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/king_crab">king crab</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/porcelain_crab">porcelain crab</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horseshoe_crab">horseshoe crab</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crab_louse">crab lice</a> &#x2013; are not true crabs.
The <strong>dingo</strong> (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canis_lupus_dingo">Canis lupus dingo</a></em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/free-ranging_dog">free-ranging dog</a> found mainly in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia_(continent)">Australia</a>. Its exact ancestry is debated, but dingoes are generally believed to be descended from semi-domesticated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog">dog</a>s from East or South Asia, which returned to a wild lifestyle when introduced to Australia. As such, it is currently classified as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subspecies_of_Canis_lupus">subspecies of the grey wolf</a>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canis_lupus">Canis lupus</a></em>. The Australian name has therefore sometimes been applied to similar dogs in Southeast Asia or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carolina_Dog">America</a> believed to be close relatives. As free-ranging animals, they are not considered <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taming">tame</a>, although tame dingoes and dingo-dog hybrids have been bred.
The dingo&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat">habitat</a> ranges from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a>s to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grassland">grassland</a>s and the edges of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forest">forest</a>s. Dingoes will normally make their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/burrow">dens</a> in deserted rabbit holes and hollow logs close to an essential supply of water.
The dingo is the largest terrestrial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predation">predator</a> in Australia, and plays an important role as an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/apex_predator">apex predator</a>. However, the dingo is seen as a pest by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/livestock">livestock</a> farmers due to attacks on animals. Conversely, their predation on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rabbit">rabbit</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo">kangaroo</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rat">rat</a>s may be of benefit to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pastoral_farming">graziers</a>.
For many Australians, the dingo is a cultural icon. The dingo is seen by many as being responsible for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thylacine">thylacine</a> extinction on the Australian mainland about two thousand years ago,(ref: extinction)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.dogslife.com.au/dogs_life_articles?cid" data-publisher="DOGSLife" data-accessdate="9 May 2009">Dingo Extinction</span> although a recent study challenges this view.(ref: ecological regime shift)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://dx.doi.org/10.1890/13-0746.1" data-publisher="ESA Ecology" data-accessdate="9 September 2013">An ecological regime shift resulting from disrupted predator-prey interactions in Holocene Australia</span> Dingoes have a prominent role in the culture of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aboriginal_Australians">Aboriginal Australians</a> as a feature of stories and ceremonies, and they are depicted on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petroglyph">rock carvings</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cave_paintings">cave paintings</a>.(ref: rose)
Despite being an efficient hunter, it is listed as vulnerable to extinction. It is proposed that this is due to susceptibility to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genetic_pollution">genetic pollution</a>: a controversial concept according to which interbreeding with domestic dogs may dilute the dingo&apos;s unique adaptations to the Australian environment.
<strong>Dinosaurs</strong> are a diverse group of animals of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> <strong>Dinosauria</strong>. They first appeared during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triassic">Triassic</a> period, 231.4&#xA0;million years ago, and were the dominant terrestrial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertebrate">vertebrate</a>s for 135&#xA0;million years, from the start of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jurassic">Jurassic</a> (about 200&#xA0;million years ago) until the end of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> (66&#xA0;million years ago), when the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous&#x2013;Paleogene_extinction_event">Cretaceous&#x2013;Paleogene extinction event</a> led to the extinction of most dinosaur groups (and three-quarters of plant and animal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth">Earth</a>) ending the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mesozoic_Era">Mesozoic Era</a>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fossil">fossil</a> record indicates that <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s evolved from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theropoda">theropod</a> dinosaurs during the Jurassic Period and, consequently, they are considered to be modern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/feathered_dinosaur">feathered dinosaur</a>s.(ref: SCI-20140731)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-authors="Lee, MichaelS.Y.; Cau, Andrea; Naish, Darren; Dyke, Gareth J. " data-url="http://www.sciencemag.org/content/345/6196/562 " data-date="1 August 2014 " data-journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" science_(journal)"_data-science"="">Science (journal)&quot; data-Science =&quot;undefined&quot; data-volume=&quot;345 &quot; data-number=&quot;6196 &quot; data-pages=&quot;562&#x2013;566 &quot; data-doi=&quot;10.1126/science.1252243 &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;August 2, 2014 &quot; data-bibcode =&quot; 2014Sci...345..562L &quot;&gt;Sustained miniaturization and anatomical innovation in the dinosaurian ancestors of birds </span> Some birds survived the extinction event that occurred 66 million years ago, and their descendants continue the dinosaur lineage to the present day.(ref: Holtz2008)
Dinosaurs are a varied group of animals from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomic</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/morphology_(biology)">morphological</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecology">ecological</a> standpoints. Birds, at over 10,000 living species,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.iucnredlist.org/documents/summarystatistics/2012_1_RL_Stats_Table_1.pdf " data-year="2012 " data-work="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" iucn_red_list"="">IUCN Red List &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;June 22, 2013&quot;&gt;Numbers of threatened species by major groups of organisms (1996&#x2013;2012) </span></ref> are the most diverse group of vertebrates besides <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/perciformes">perciform</a> fish. Using fossil evidence, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/paleontology">paleontologists</a> have identified over 500 distinct <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> and more than 1,000 different species of non-avian dinosaurs. Dinosaurs are represented on every continent by both <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> species and fossil remains. Some are herbivorous, others carnivorous. While dinosaurs were ancestrally <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bipedalism">bipedal</a>, many extinct groups included <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quadrupedalism">quadrupedal</a> species, and some were able to shift between these stances. Elaborate display structures such as horns or crests are common to all dinosaur groups, and some extinct groups developed skeletal modifications such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Armour_(anatomy)">bony armor</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spine_(zoology)">spines</a>. Evidence suggests that egg laying and nest building are additional traits shared by all dinosaurs. While modern dinosaurs (birds) are generally small due to the constraints of flight, many prehistoric dinosaurs were large-bodied&#x2014;the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sauropod">sauropod</a> dinosaurs are estimated to have reached lengths of over [40mftabbr=offsp=us]<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-title=" March of the Titans: The Locomotor Capabilities of Sauropod Dinosaurs" data-url=" http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3864407/" data-journal=" PLoS ONE" data-date=" 2013-10-30" data-issn=" 1932-6203" data-pmc=" 3864407" data-pmid=" 24348896" data-volume=" 8" data-issue=" 10" data-doi=" 10.1371/journal.pone.0078733" data-first=" William Irvin" data-last=" Sellers" data-first2=" Lee" data-last2=" Margetts" data-first3=" Rodolfo An&#xED;bal" data-last3=" Coria" data-first4=" Phillip Lars" data-last4=" Manning" data-bibcode=" 2013PLoSO...878733S "></span></ref> and heights of [18mftabbr=offsp=us]<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-title=" Sauroposeidon" data-url=" https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title"></span></ref> and were the largest land animals of all time. Still, the idea that non-avian dinosaurs were uniformly gigantic is a misconception based in part on preservation bias, as large, sturdy bones are more likely to last until they are fossilized. Many dinosaurs were quite small: <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xixianykus">Xixianykus</a></em>, for example, was only about [50cminabbr=on] long.
Although the word <em>dinosaur</em> means &quot;terrible lizard&quot;, the name is somewhat misleading, as dinosaurs are not <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lizards">lizards</a>. Instead, they represent a separate group of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptiles">reptiles</a> that, like many extinct forms, did not exhibit characteristics traditionally seen as reptilian, such as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_locomotion#Posture">sprawling limb posture</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ectothermy">ectothermy</a>. Additionally, many prehistoric animals, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mosasaur">mosasaur</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ichthyosaur">ichthyosaur</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pterosaur">pterosaur</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plesiosaur">plesiosaur</a>s, and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimetrodon">Dimetrodon</a></em>, are popularly conceived of as dinosaurs, but are not <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomically</a> classified as dinosaurs. Through the first half of the 20th century, before birds were recognized to be dinosaurs, most of the scientific community believed dinosaurs to have been sluggish and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poikilotherm">cold-blooded</a>. Most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dinosaur_renaissance">research conducted since the 1970s</a>, however, has indicated that all dinosaurs were active animals with elevated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/metabolism">metabolism</a>s and numerous adaptations for social interaction.
Since the first dinosaur <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fossil">fossil</a>s were recognized in the early 19th century, mounted fossil dinosaur skeletons have been major attractions at museums around the world, and dinosaurs have become an enduring part of world culture. The large sizes of some groups, as well as their seemingly monstrous and fantastic nature, have ensured dinosaurs<em>regular appearance in best-selling books and films, such as </em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jurassic_Park_(film)">Jurassic Park</a>. Persistent public enthusiasm for the animals has resulted in significant funding for dinosaur science, and new discoveries are regularly covered by the media.
<strong>Cod</strong> is the common name for the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gadus">Gadus</a></em> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/demersal_fish">demersal fish</a>es, belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gadidae">Gadidae</a>. Cod is also used as part of  the common name for a number of other fish species, and there are species suggested to belong to genus <em>Gadus</em> that are not called cod (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska_pollock">Alaska pollock</a>).
The two most important species of cod are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_cod">Atlantic cod</a> (<em>Gadus morhua</em>), which lives in the colder waters and deeper sea regions throughout the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Atlantic">North Atlantic</a>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_cod">Pacific cod</a> (<em>Gadus macrocephalus</em>), found in both eastern and western regions of the northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific</a>. <em>Gadus morhua</em> was named by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a> in 1758. (However, <em>G. morhua callarias</em>, a low-salinity, non-migratory <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subspecies">race</a> restricted to parts of the Baltic, was originally described as <em>Gadus callarias</em> by Linnaeus.)
Cod is popular as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/food">food</a> with a mild flavour and a dense, flaky <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whitefish_(fisheries_term)">white flesh</a>. Cod livers are processed to make <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cod_liver_oil">cod liver oil</a>, an important source of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vitamin_A">vitamin A</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vitamin_D">vitamin D</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vitamin_E">vitamin E</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/omega-3_fatty_acid">omega-3 fatty acid</a>s (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/eicosapentaenoic_acid">EPA</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/docosahexaenoic_acid">DHA</a>). Young Atlantic cod or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/haddock">haddock</a> prepared in strips for cooking is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scrod">scrod</a>. In the United Kingdom, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_cod">Atlantic cod</a> is one of the most common ingredients in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish_and_chips">fish and chips</a>, along with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/haddock">haddock</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plaice">plaice</a>.

A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
A <strong>buzzard</strong> is one of several large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s, but there are a number of meanings as detailed below.
<strong>Eagle</strong> is a common name for many large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/birds_of_prey">birds of prey</a> of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>; it belongs to several groups of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus">genera</a> that are not necessarily closely related to each other.
Most of the 60 species of eagles are from Eurasia and Africa.<ref>del Hoyo, J.; Elliot, A. &amp; Sargatal, J. (editors). (1994). <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a> Volume 2</em>: New World Vultures to Guineafowl. Lynx Edicions. ISBN 84-87334-15-6</ref> Outside this area, just 14 species can be found &#x2013; two in North America, nine in Central and South America, and three in Australia.
<strong>Condor</strong> is the common name for two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_vulture">New World vulture</a>s, each in a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monotypic">monotypic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a>. They are the largest flying land birds in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>.
They are:
<strong>Corals</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marine_invertebrates">marine invertebrates</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/class_(biology)">class</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthozoa">Anthozoa</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/phylum">phylum</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cnidaria">Cnidaria</a>. They typically live in compact <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colony_(biology)">colonies</a> of many identical individual <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polyp">polyp</a>s. The group includes the important <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coral_reef">reef</a> builders that inhabit tropical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ocean">ocean</a>s and secrete <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/calcium_carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> to form a hard skeleton.
A coral &quot;head&quot; is a colony of myriad <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cloning">genetically identical</a> polyps. Each polyp is a sac-like animal typically only a few millimeters in diameter and a few centimeters in length. A set of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tentacle">tentacle</a>s surround a central mouth opening. An <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> is excreted near the base. Over many generations, the colony thus creates a large skeleton that is characteristic of the species. Individual heads grow by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/asexual_reproduction">asexual reproduction</a> of polyps. Corals also breed sexually by spawning: polyps of the same species release <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gamete">gamete</a>s simultaneously over a period of one to several nights around a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/full_moon">full moon</a>.
Although some corals can catch small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plankton">plankton</a>, using <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cnidocyte">stinging cells</a> on their tentacles, most corals obtain the majority of their energy and nutrients from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/unicellular">unicellular</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dinoflagellate">dinoflagellate</a>s in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbiodinium">Symbiodinium</a></em> that live within their tissues. These are commonly known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zooxanthellae">zooxanthellae</a> and the corals that contain them are zooxanthellate corals. Such corals require sunlight and grow in clear, shallow water, typically at depths shallower than [60m]. Corals are major contributors to the physical structure of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coral_reef">coral reef</a>s that develop in tropical and subtropical waters, such as the enormous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Barrier_Reef">Great Barrier Reef</a> off the coast of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queensland">Queensland</a>, Australia.
Other corals do not rely on zooxanthellae and can live in much deeper water, with the cold-water <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lophelia">Lophelia</a></em> surviving as deep as [3000m].<ref name="Squires"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" author="Squires, D.F. " year="1959 " title="Deep sea corals collected by the Lamont Geological Observatory. 1. Atlantic corals " journal=" American Museum Novitates " volume="1965 " pages="1&#x2013;42"></span></ref> Some have been found on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darwin_Mounds">Darwin Mounds</a>, north-west of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cape_Wrath">Cape Wrath</a>, Scotland. Corals have also been found as far north as off the coast of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state)">Washington State</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleutian_Islands">Aleutian Islands</a>.
The <strong>chickadees</strong> are a group of North American birds in the tit family included in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poecile">Poecile</a></em>. Species found in North America are referred as chickadees, while other species in the genus are called tits. They also have quick movements and are notably shy.[date=January 2015]
They are small-sized birds overall, usually having the crown of the head and throat patch distinctly darker than the body. They are at least 6 to 14 centimeters in size.
Their name reputedly comes from the fact that their calls make a distinctive &quot;chick-a-dee-dee-dee&quot; sound.[date=July 2015]
<strong>Cockroaches</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s of the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blattodea">Blattodea</a>, sometimes called Blattaria, of which about 30 species out of 4,600(ref: Cockroach.SpeciesFile.org)<http: cockroach.speciesfile.org=""> total are associated with human habitats. About four species are well known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pest_(animal)">pest</a>s.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Valles " first1=" SM " last2=" Koehler " first2=" PG " last3=" Brenner " first3=" RJ. " year=" 1999 " title=" Comparative insecticide susceptibility and detoxification enzyme activities among pestiferous blattodea " journal=" Comp Infibous Biochem Physiol C Pharmacol Toxicol Endocrinol " volume=" 124 " issue=" 3" pages=" 227&#x2013;232 " pmid=" 10661713 " doi=" 10.1016/S0742-8413(99)00076-6 " url="http://www4.ncsu.edu/~coby/schal/1990SchalAREreview.pdf " format="PDF "></span></ref><ref name="are"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" doi=" 10.1146/annurev.en.35.010190.002513 " last1=" Schal " first1=" C " last2=" Hamilton " first2=" R. L. " year=" 1990 " title=" Integrated suppression of synanthropic cockroaches " url=" http://www4.ncsu.edu/~coby/schal/1990SchalAREreview.pdf " journal=" Annu. Rev. Entomol " volume=" 35" pages=" 521&#x2013;551 " pmid=" 2405773 "></span></ref></http:>
Among the best-known pest species are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_cockroach">American cockroach</a>, <em>Periplaneta americana</em>, which is about [30mminabbr=on] long; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_cockroach">German cockroach</a>, <em>Blattella germanica</em>, about [15mminabbr=on] long; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_cockroach">Asian cockroach</a>, <em>Blattella asahinai</em>, also about [15mminabbr=on] in length; and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oriental_cockroach">Oriental cockroach</a>, <em>Blatta orientalis</em>, about [25mminabbr=on]. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropics">Tropical</a> cockroaches are often much bigger, and, contrary to popular opinion, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blattoptera">extinct cockroach relatives</a> and roachoids such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimylacris">Archimylacris</a></em> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permian">Permian</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apthoroblattina">Apthoroblattina</a></em> were not as large as the biggest modern species.(ref: Archi)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-first="Russell J." data-last="Garwood" data-first2="Mark D." data-last2="Sutton" data-year="2010" data-journal="Biology Letters" data-volume="6" data-pages="699&#x2013;702" data-url="http://rsbl.royalsocietypublishing.org/content/6/5/699.abstract" data-accessdate="June 9, 2015" data-doi="10.1098/rsbl.2010.0199">X-ray micro-tomography of Carboniferous stem-Dictyoptera: New insights into early insects</span>
An <strong>eel</strong> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <strong>Anguilliformes</strong> ([&#xE6;&#x14B;&#x2CC;&#x261;w&#x26A;l&#x268;&#x2C8;f&#x254;rmi&#x2D0;z]), which consists of four suborders, 20 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">families</a>, 111 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> and about 800 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.  Most eels are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s. The term &quot;eel&quot; (originally referring to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_eel">European eel</a>) is also used for some other similarly shaped fish, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/electric_eel">electric eel</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spiny_eel">spiny eel</a>s, but these are not members of the Anguilliformes order.
<strong>Crane fly</strong> is a common name referring to any member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Tipulidae</strong> of insects in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fly">Diptera</a>, true <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fly">flies</a> in the superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tipuloidea">Tipuloidea</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cylindrotominae">Cylindrotominae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Limoniinae">Limoniinae</a>, and Pediciinae have been ranked as subfamilies of Tipulidae by most authors,<ref>Alexander C.P., Byers G.W. (1981) Tipulidae. in: McAlpine J.F. et al. (Ed.), <em>Manual of Nearctic Diptera</em>. Agriculture Canada, Ottawa, pp. 153&#x2013;1902 ISBN 0660107317 <a href="http://www.esc-sec.ca/aafcmono.php">pdf</a> <strong>download manual</strong></ref> though occasionally elevated to family rank. In the most recent classifications, only <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pediciidae">Pediciidae</a> is now ranked as a separate family, due to considerations of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/paraphyly">paraphyly</a>.(ref: Petersen, M.J. 2010) They are also known as <strong>daddy-long-legs</strong> around the world,(ref: delta)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arachnid">arachnid</a>s of the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opiliones">Opiliones</a> or the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pholcidae">Pholcidae</a>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e of crane flies are known commonly as leatherjackets. 
Crane flies <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmopolitan_distribution">are found worldwide</a>, though individual species usually have limited ranges. They are most diverse in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropics">tropics</a>, and are also common in northern latitudes and high elevations.<ref name="pritch">Pritchard, G. (1983). <a href="http://nlbif.eti.uva.nl/ccw/documents/Pritchard,_1983.pdf">Biology of Tipulidae.</a> <em>Anal beads Review of Entomology</em> 28(1), 1-22.</ref>
The Tipulidae <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sensu">sensu lato</a></em> is one of the largest groups of flies, including over 15,000 species and subspecies in 525 genera and subgenera.<ref name="de">de Jong, H., et al. (2008). <a href="http://www.carnegiemnh.org/uploadedFiles/CMNH_Site/Invertebrate_Zoology/Downloads/Young%202008%20Global%20diversity%20of%20craneflies%20in%20freshwater.pdf">Global diversity of craneflies (Insecta, Diptera: Tipulidea or Tipulidae <em>sensu lato</em>) in freshwater.</a> <em>Hydrobiologia</em> 595(1), 457-67.</ref> Most crane flies were described by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/entomology">entomologist</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Paul_Alexander">Charles Paul Alexander</a>, a fly specialist, in over 1000 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academic_publishing">research publications</a>.<ref name="oo">Oosterbroek, P. <a href="http://hbs.bishopmuseum.org/aocat/pdf/02tipulidae.pdf">Superfamily Tipuloidea, Family Tipulidae.</a> Chapter 2 In: Evenhuis, N. L. (Ed.) <em>Catalog of the Diptera of the Australasian and Oceanian Regions</em>, Issue 86 of Bernice P. Bishop Museum Special Publication. Apollo Press. 1989.</ref>

The <strong>stoat</strong> (<em>Mustela erminea</em>), also known as the <strong>short-tailed weasel</strong>, is a species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelidae">Mustelidae</a> native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>, distinguished from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/least_weasel">least weasel</a> by its larger size and longer tail with a prominent black tip. The name <strong>ermine</strong> is often, but not always, used for the animal in its pure white winter coat, or the fur thereof.<ref name="SOED"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-year="2007" data-publisher="Oxford University Press" data-location="United Kingdom" data-isbn="0199206872" data-pages="3804">Shorter Oxford English dictionary</span></ref> In the late 19th century, stoats were introduced into New Zealand to control rabbits. The stoats have had a devastating effect on native bird populations (see <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stoats_in_New_Zealand">stoats in New Zealand</a>).
It is classed by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">IUCN</a> as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/least_concern">least concern</a>, due to its wide circumpolar distribution, and because it does not face any significant threat to its survival.(ref: iucn)<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-="" url=" http://www.issg.org/database/species/ecology.asp?si" data-publisher="Invasive Species Specialist Group">100 of the Worlds Worst Invasive Species </span></ref>
Ermine luxury fur is often used by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catholic_monarch">Catholic monarch</a>s, pontiffs and cardinals, who sometimes use it as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mozetta">mozetta</a> cape. It is also used in capes on devotional images such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infant_Jesus_of_Prague">Infant Jesus of Prague</a>. The parliamentary and coronation robes of British peers of the realm are also made from ermine.
The <strong>cuckoos</strong> are a family of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">birds</a>, <strong>Cuculidae</strong>, the sole taxon in the order <strong>Cuculiformes</strong>.<ref name="Ericson">Ericson, P.G.P. <em>et al.</em> (2006) <a href="http://biology-web.nmsu.edu/houde/Ericson_et_al_2006.pdf">Diversification of Neoaves: integration of molecular sequence data and fossils</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology_Letters">Biology Letters</a>, 2(4):543&#x2013;547</ref><ref name="Hackett"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Hackett " first1=" S.J. " display-authors=" 1 " last2=" et al " year=" 2008 " title=" A Phylogenomic Study of Birds Reveals Their Evolutionary History " url=" " journal=" Science " volume=" 320 " issue=" 5884" pages=" 1763&#x2013;1768 " doi="10.1126/science.1157704"></span></ref><ref name="Jarvis2014"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Jarvis " first1=" E.D. " display-authors=" 1 " last2=" et al " year=" 2014 " title=" Whole-genome analyses resolve early branches in the tree of life of modern birds " url=" http://www.sciencemag.org/content/346/6215/1320.abstract " journal=" Science " volume=" 346 " issue=" 6215" pages=" 1320&#x2013;1331 "></span></ref> The cuckoo family includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_cuckoo">common or European cuckoo</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geococcyx">roadrunner</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/koel">koel</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/malkoha">malkoha</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coua">coua</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coucal">coucal</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ani_(bird)">anis</a>. The coucals and anis are sometimes separated as distinct families, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centropodidae">Centropodidae</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crotophagidae">Crotophagidae</a> respectively.
The cuckoos are generally medium-sized slender birds. The majority are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal">arboreal</a>, with a sizeable minority that are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_animal">terrestrial</a>. The family has a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cosmopolitan_distribution">cosmopolitan distribution</a>, with the majority of species being tropical. Some species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_migration">migratory</a>. The cuckoos feed on insects, insect larvae and a variety of other animals, as well as fruit. Many species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/brood_parasite">brood parasite</a>s, laying their eggs in the nests of other species, but the majority of species raise their own young.
In <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeus">Zeus</a> transformed himself into a cuckoo so that he could seduce <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hera">Hera</a>; the bird was sacred to her.
The <strong>aardvark</strong> ([&#x2C8;&#x251;rd.v&#x251;rk] [ARDvark]; <em>Orycteropus afer</em>) is a medium-sized, burrowing, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nocturnal">nocturnal</a> mammal native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>. It is the only living species of the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tubulidentata">Tubulidentata</a>,(ref: MSW3) although other prehistoric species and genera of Tubulidentata are known. Unlike other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insectivore">insectivore</a>s, it has a long pig-like snout, which is used to sniff out food. It roams over most of the southern two-thirds of the African continent, avoiding mainly rocky areas. A nocturnal feeder, it subsists on ants and termites, which it will dig out of their hills using its sharp claws and powerful legs. It also digs to create burrows in which to live and rear its young. It receives a &quot;least concern&quot; rating from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN">IUCN</a>; although its numbers seem to be decreasing.
<strong>Echidnas</strong> [&#x268;&#x2C8;k&#x26A;dn&#x259;], sometimes known as <strong>spiny anteaters</strong>, belong to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Tachyglossidae</strong> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monotreme">monotreme</a> order of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg_(biology)">egg</a>-laying <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s. The four <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">extant</a> species, together with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/platypus">platypus</a>, are the only surviving members of that <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> and are the only extant mammals that lay eggs. Their diet consists of ants and termites, but they are not closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anteater">true anteaters</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>. They live in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a>.
Echidnas evidently evolved between 20 and 50 million years ago, descending from a platypus-like monotreme. This ancestor was aquatic, but echidnas adapted to life on land.

<strong>Elephant seals</strong> are large, oceangoing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/earless_seal">earless seal</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Mirounga</em>. The two species, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_elephant_seal">northern elephant seal</a> (<em>M. angustirostris</em>) and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southern_elephant_seal">southern elephant seal</a> (<em>M. leonina</em>), both were hunted to the brink of extinction by the end of the 19th century, but numbers have since recovered.
The northern elephant seal, somewhat smaller than its southern relative, ranges over the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific">Pacific</a> coast of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States">U.S.</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a>. The most northerly breeding location on the Pacific Coast is at <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Race_Rocks_Marine_Protected_Area">Race Rocks</a>, at the southern tip of Vancouver Island in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Strait_of_Juan_de_Fuca">Strait of Juan de Fuca</a>. The southern elephant seal is found in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a> on islands such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Georgia">South Georgia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macquarie_Island">Macquarie Island</a>, and on the coasts of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa">South Africa</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina">Argentina</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peninsula_Vald&#xE9;s">Peninsula Vald&#xE9;s</a>, which is the fourth-largest elephant seal colony in the world. Fossils of an as yet unnamed species of <em>Mirounga</em> have been found in South Africa, and dated to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miocene">Miocene</a> epoch.<ref name="Berta2012"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" author=" Berta, A. &amp; Churchill, M. " year=" 2012 " title=" Pinniped Taxonomy: evidence for species and subspecies " journal=" Mammal Review " volume=" 42 " issue=" 3 " pages=" 207&#x2013;234 " doi=" 10.1111/j.1365-2907.2011.00193.x"></span></ref>
Elephant seals breed annually and are seemingly faithful to colonies that have been established breeding areas.
<strong>Cattle</strong>&#x2014;colloquially <strong>cows</strong>[group=noteThe noun <em>cattle</em> (which is treated as a plural and has no singular) encompasses both sexes. The singular, <em>cow</em>, unambiguously means the female, the male being <em>bull</em>. The plural feminine form <em>cows</em> is sometimes used colloquially to refer to both sexes collectively, as <em>e.g.</em> in a herd, but that usage can be misleading as the speakers intent may indeed be just the females. The bovine species per se<strong>is clearly dimorphic.]&#x2014;are the most common type of large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ungulates">ungulates</a>.  They are a prominent modern member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovinae">Bovinae</a>, are the most widespread species of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> </strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bos">Bos</a><em>, and are most commonly classified collectively as </em>Bos taurus.  Cattle are raised as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/livestock">livestock</a> for meat (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beef">beef</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/veal">veal</a>), as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy_cattle">dairy animals</a> for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/milk">milk</a> and other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy_product">dairy product</a>s, and as draft animals (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ox">ox</a>en or bullocks that pull <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cart">cart</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plow">plow</a>s and other implements). Other products include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leather">leather</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cow_dung">dung</a> for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/manure">manure</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fuel">fuel</a>. In some regions, such as parts of India, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cattle_in_religion">cattle have significant religious meaning</a>.  From as few as 80 progenitors domesticated in southeast Turkey about 10,500 years ago,(ref: HowNowCow)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Bollongino " data-first1="R. " data-last2="Burger " data-first2="J. " data-last3="Powell " data-first3="A. " data-last4="Mashkour " data-first4="M. " data-last5="Vigne " data-first5="J.-D. " data-last6="Thomas " data-first6="M. G. " data-year="2012 " data-journal="Molecular Biology and Evolution " data-volume="29 " data-issue="9 " data-pages="2101-2104 " data-url="http://mbe.oxfordjournals.org/content/early/2012/03/14/molbev.mss092">Modern taurine cattle descended from small number of Near-Eastern founders </span> Op. cit. in <span class="reference" data-type="news " data-last1="Wilkins " data-first1="Alasdair " data-work="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" io9"="">io9 &quot; data-url=&quot;http://io9.com/5897169/dna-reveals-that-cows-were-almost-impossible-to-domesticate?tag&quot; data-date=&quot;28 Mar 2012 &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;2 Apr 2012&quot;&gt;DNA reveals that cows were almost impossible to domesticate </span> according to an estimate from 2003, there are 1.3 billion cattle in the world.(ref: Breeds of Cattle at CATTLE TODAY)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-author="Cattle Today " data-url="http://cattle-today.com/ " data-publisher="Cattle-today.com " data-accessdate="15 October 2013">Breeds of Cattle at CATTLE TODAY </span> In 2009, cattle became one of the first livestock animals to have a fully mapped <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovine_genome">genome</a>.(ref: Cattle genome)<span class="reference" data-type="news " data-url="http://www.washingtonpost.com/wp-dyn/content/article/2009/04/23/AR2009042303453.html " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" the_washington_post"="">The Washington Post &quot; data-date=&quot;23 April 2009 &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;23 April 2009 &quot; data- first=&quot;David &quot; data- last=&quot;Brown&quot;&gt;Scientists Unravel Genome of the Cow </span>  Some consider cattle the oldest form of wealth, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle_raiding">cattle raiding</a> consequently one of the earliest forms of theft.
An <strong>earthworm</strong> is a tube-shaped, segmented <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/worm">worm</a> found in the phylum <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annelid">Annelid</a>a.  They are commonly found living in soil, feeding on live and dead organic matter. Its digestive system runs through the length of its body. It conducts respiration through its skin. An earthworm has a double transport system composed of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coelom#Coelomic_fluid">coelomic fluid</a> that moves within the fluid-filled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coelom">coelom</a> and a simple, closed blood circulatory system. It has a central and a peripheral nervous system. The central nervous system consists of two ganglia above the mouth, one on either side, connected to a nerve cord running back along its length to motor neurons and sensory cells in each segment. Large numbers of chemoreceptors are concentrated near its mouth. Circumferential and longitudinal muscles on the periphery of each segment enable the worm to move. Similar sets of muscles line the gut, and their actions move the digesting food toward the worm&apos;s anus.(ref: Integrated Principles of Zoology)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-authors="Cleveland P. Hickman Jr., Larry S. Roberts, Frances M Hickman" data-year="1984" data-edition="7th" data-publisher="Times Mirror/Mosby College Publishing" data-isbn="0-8016-2173-9" data-page="344">Integrated Principles of Zoology</span>
Earthworms are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a>s&#x2014;each individual carries both male and female sex organs.  They lack either an internal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skeleton">skeleton</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a>, but maintain their structure with fluid-filled coelom chambers that function as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hydrostatic_skeleton">hydrostatic skeleton</a>.
&quot;Earthworm&quot; is the common name for the largest members of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligochaeta">Oligochaeta</a> (which is either a class or a subclass depending on the author). In classical systems, they were placed in the order Opisthopora, on the basis of the male pores opening posterior to the female pores, though the internal male segments are anterior to the female. Theoretical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cladistics">cladistic</a> studies have placed them, instead, in the suborder Lumbricina of the order Haplotaxida, but this may again soon change. Folk names for the earthworm include &quot;dew-worm&quot;, &quot;rainworm&quot;, &quot;night crawler&quot;, and &quot;angleworm&quot; (due to its use as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fishing_bait">fishing bait</a>).
Larger terrestrial earthworms are also called megadriles (or big worms), as opposed to the microdriles (or small worms) in the semiaquatic families <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tubificidae">Tubificidae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lumbriculidae">Lumbriculidae</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enchytraeidae">Enchytraeidae</a>, among others. The megadriles are characterized by having a distinct <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clitellum">clitellum</a> (which is more extensive than that of microdriles) and a vascular system with true capillaries.
Earthworms are far less abundant in disturbed environments and are typically active only if water is present.(ref: brady)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Nyle C. Brady &amp; Ray R. Weil" data-title="Elements of the Nature and Properties of Soils (3rd Edition)" data-publisher="Prentice Hall" data-year="2009" data-page="" data-isbn="9780135014332"></span>
<strong><em>Stenorhynchus seticornis</em></strong>, the <strong>yellowline arrow crab</strong> or simply <strong>arrow crab</strong>, is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of marine <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crab">crab</a>.
