<strong>Armadillos</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World">New World</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/placental">placental</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s with a leathery <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/armour_(zoology)">armour</a> shell.  <strong>Dasypodidae</strong> is the only surviving <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cingulata">Cingulata</a>, part of the superorder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xenarthra">Xenarthra</a>, along with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anteater">anteater</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sloth">sloth</a>s. The word <em>armadillo</em> means &quot;little armoured one&quot; in Spanish. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztecs">Aztecs</a> called them <em>&#x101;y&#x14D;t&#x14D;chtli</em> [a&#x2D0;jo&#x2D0;&#x2C8;to&#x2D0;t&#x361;&#x283;t&#x361;&#x26C;i], <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nahuatl">Nahuatl</a> for &quot;turtle-rabbit&quot;: <em>&#x101;y&#x14D;tl</em> [&#x2C8;a&#x2D0;jo&#x2D0;t&#x361;&#x26C;] (turtle) and <em>t&#x14D;chtli</em> [&#x2C8;to&#x2D0;t&#x361;&#x283;t&#x361;&#x26C;i] (rabbit).
About 10 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> and 20 extant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of armadillo have been described, some of which are distinguished by the number of bands on their armour. Their average length is about [75cminabbr=on], including tail. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/giant_armadillo">giant armadillo</a> grows up to [150cminabbr=on] and weighs up to [54kglbabbr=on], while the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pink_fairy_armadillo">pink fairy armadillo</a> is a diminutive species, with an overall length of [13&#x2013;15cminsigfig=1abbr=on]. All species are native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>, where they inhabit a variety of environments.
Like all other xenarthran lineages, armadillos originated in South America. Due to the continent&apos;s former isolation, they were confined there for most of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cenozoic">Cenozoic</a>. The recent formation of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isthmus_of_Panama">Isthmus of Panama</a> allowed a few members of the family to migrate northward into southern North America by the early <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, as part of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_American_Interchange">Great American Interchange</a>.(ref: Woodburne2010)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cingulata">cingulate</a> relatives, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pampathere">pampathere</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/glyptodont">glyptodont</a>s, made the same journey.(ref: Woodburne2010)
Today, all extant armadillo species are still present in South America. They are particularly diverse in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraguay">Paraguay</a> (where eleven species exist) and surrounding areas. Many species are endangered. Some, including four species of <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dasypus">Dasypus</a></em>, are widely distributed over the Americas, whereas others, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yepes&apos;s_mulita">Yepes&apos;s mulita</a>, are restricted to small ranges. Two species, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_naked-tailed_armadillo">northern naked-tailed armadillo</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nine-banded_armadillo">nine-banded armadillo</a>, are found in Central America; the latter has also reached the United States, primarily in the south-central states (notably Texas), but with a range that extends as far east as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Carolina">South Carolina</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Florida">Florida</a>, and as far north as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nebraska">Nebraska</a> and central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indiana">Indiana</a>. Their range has consistently expanded in North America over the last century due to a lack of natural predators.
The <strong>ferret</strong> (<em>Mustela putorius furo</em>) is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication">domesticated</a> form of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_polecat">European polecat</a>, a mammal belonging to the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/weasel">weasel</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustela">Mustela</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelidae">Mustelidae</a>. They typically have brown, black, white, or mixed fur. They have an average length of [51cminabbr=on] including a [13cminabbr=on] tail, weigh about [{{convert1.5&#x2013;4lbsigfig=1]}}, and have a natural lifespan of 7 to 10&#xA0;years. Ferrets are sexually <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism">dimorphic</a> predators with males being substantially larger than females.
Several other Mustelids also have the word <em>ferret</em> in their common names, including an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">endangered species</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/black-footed_ferret">black-footed ferret</a>.
The history of the ferret&apos;s domestication is uncertain, like that of most other domestic animals, but it is likely that ferrets have been domesticated for at least 2,500&#xA0;years. They are still used for hunting rabbits in some parts of the world, but increasingly, they are kept only as pets.
Being so closely related to polecats, ferrets easily hybridize with them, and this has occasionally resulted in feral colonies of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polecat-ferret_hybrid">polecat-ferret hybrid</a>s that have caused damage to native fauna, especially in New Zealand.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url=" http://www.doc.govt.nz/conservation/threats-and-impacts/animal-pests/animal-pests-a-z/ferrets/" data-title=" Ferrets: New Zealand animal pests" data-date=" 11 August 2006" data-accessdate=" 25 July 2014" data-website=" Department of Conservation" data-publisher=" New Zealand Department of Conservation (DOC)" data-archiveurl=" https://web.archive.org/web/20140724222620/http://www.doc.govt.nz/conservation/threats-and-impacts/animal-pests/animal-pests-a-z/ferrets/" data-archivedate=" 24 July 2014" data-deadurl=" no"></span></ref> As a result, some parts of the world have imposed restrictions on the keeping of ferrets.
<strong>Amphibians</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ectotherm">ectotherm</a>ic, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tetrapod">tetrapod</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertebrate">vertebrate</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(biology)">class</a> <strong>Amphibia</strong>. Modern amphibians are all <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lissamphibia">Lissamphibia</a>. They inhabit a wide variety of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat">habitat</a>s with most species living within <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/terrestrial_animal">terrestrial</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fossorial">fossorial</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal">arboreal</a> or freshwater <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aquatic_animal">aquatic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecosystem">ecosystem</a>s. Amphibians typically start out as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e living in water, but some species have developed behavioural adaptations to bypass this. The young generally undergo <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a> from larva with gills to an adult air-breathing form with lungs. Amphibians use their skin as a secondary respiratory surface and some small terrestrial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/salamander">salamander</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/frog">frog</a>s lack lungs and rely entirely on their skin. They are superficially similar to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptile">reptile</a>s but, along with mammals and birds, reptiles are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/amniote">amniote</a>s and do not require water bodies in which to breed. With their complex reproductive needs and permeable skins, amphibians are often <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecological_indicator">ecological indicator</a>s and in recent decades there has been a dramatic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/decline_in_amphibian_populations">decline in amphibian populations</a> for many species around the globe.
The earliest amphibians <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/evolution_of_tetrapods">evolved</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Devonian">Devonian</a> period from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sarcopterygian">sarcopterygian</a> fish with lungs and bony-limbed fins, features that were helpful in adapting to dry land. They diversified and became dominant during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carboniferous">Carboniferous</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permian">Permian</a> periods, but were later displaced by reptiles and other vertebrates. Over time, amphibians shrank in size and decreased in diversity, leaving only the modern subclass <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lissamphibia">Lissamphibia</a>. The three modern orders of amphibians are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anura_(frog)">Anura</a> (the frogs and toads), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urodela">Urodela</a> (the salamanders), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caecilian">Apoda</a> (the caecilians). The number of known amphibian species is approximately 7,000, of which nearly 90% are frogs. The smallest amphibian (and vertebrate) in the world is a frog from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a> (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paedophryne_amauensis">Paedophryne amauensis</a></em>) with a length of just [7.7mminabbr=on]. The largest living amphibian is the [1.8m0abbr=on] <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_giant_salamander">Chinese giant salamander</a> (<em>Andrias davidianus</em>), but this is dwarfed by the extinct [9m0abbr=on] <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prionosuchus">Prionosuchus</a></em> from the middle Permian of Brazil. The study of amphibians is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/batrachology">batrachology</a>, while the study of both reptiles and amphibians is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herpetology">herpetology</a>.
<strong>Elephants</strong> are large mammals of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elephantidae">Elephantidae</a> and the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proboscidea">Proboscidea</a>. Two species are traditionally recognised, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_elephant">African elephant</a> (<em>Loxodonta africana</em>) and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asian_elephant">Asian elephant</a> (<em>Elephas maximus</em>), although some evidence suggests that <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_bush_elephant">African bush elephant</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_forest_elephant">African forest elephant</a>s are separate species (<em>L.&#xA0;africana</em> and <em>L.&#xA0;cyclotis</em> respectively). Elephants are scattered throughout <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sub-Saharan_Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia">South Asia</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. Elephantidae is the only surviving family of the order Proboscidea; other, now extinct, members of the order include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deinotheriidae">deinotheres</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gomphothere">gomphothere</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammoth">mammoth</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mastodon">mastodon</a>s. Male African elephants are the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/terrestrial_animal">terrestrial animal</a>s and can reach a height of [4mft0abbr=on] and weigh [7000kglb-3abbr=on]. All elephants have several distinctive features the most notable of which is a long trunk or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/proboscis">proboscis</a>, used for many purposes, particularly breathing, lifting water and grasping objects.  Their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/incisor">incisor</a>s grow into tusks, which can serve as weapons and as tools for moving objects and digging. Elephants&apos; large ear flaps help to control their body temperature. Their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/column">pillar</a>-like legs can carry their great weight. African elephants have larger ears and concave backs while Asian elephants have smaller ears and convex or level backs.
Elephants are herbivorous and can be found in different habitats including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savanna">savanna</a>hs, forests, deserts and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsh">marsh</a>es. They prefer to stay near water. They are considered to be <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/keystone_species">keystone species</a> due to their impact on their environments. Other animals tend to keep their distance; predators such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lion">lion</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tiger">tiger</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hyena">hyena</a>s and wild <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canidae">dogs</a> usually target only the young elephants (or &quot;calves&quot;). Females (&quot;cows&quot;) tend to live in family groups, which can consist of one female with her calves or several related females with offspring. The groups are led by an individual known as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matriarchy">matriarch</a>, often the oldest cow.  Elephants have a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fission-fusion_society">fission-fusion society</a> in which multiple family groups come together to socialise. Males (&quot;bulls&quot;) leave their family groups when they reach puberty, and may live alone or with other males. Adult bulls mostly interact with family groups when looking for a mate and enter a state of increased <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/testosterone">testosterone</a> and aggression known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/musth">musth</a>, which helps them gain <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)">dominance</a> and reproductive success. Calves are the centre of attention in their family groups and rely on their mothers for as long as three years. Elephants can live up to 70 years in the wild. They communicate by touch, sight, smell and sound; elephants use <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/infrasound">infrasound</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seismic_communication">seismic communication</a> over long distances. Elephant intelligence has been compared with that of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/primate">primate</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cetacea">cetacea</a>ns. They appear to have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/self-awareness">self-awareness</a> and show <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/empathy">empathy</a> for dying or dead individuals of their kind.
African elephants are listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species">vulnerable</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN), while the Asian elephant is classed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_species">endangered</a>. One of the biggest threats to elephant populations is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ivory_trade">ivory trade</a>, as the animals are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poaching">poached</a> for their ivory tusks. Other threats to wild elephants include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_destruction">habitat destruction</a> and conflicts with local people. Elephants are used as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/working_animal">working animal</a>s in Asia. In the past they were used in war; today, they are often put on display in zoos and circuses. Elephants are highly recognisable and have been featured in art, folklore, religion, literature and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/popular_culture">popular culture</a>.
The <strong>African leopard</strong> (<em>Panthera pardus pardus</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>. It is widely distributed in most of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sub-Saharan_Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, but the historical range has been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_fragmentation">fragmented</a> in the course of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_destruction">habitat conversion</a>.
<strong>Aphids</strong>, also known as <strong>plant lice</strong> and in Britain and the Commonwealth as <strong>greenflies</strong>, <strong>blackflies</strong>, or <strong>whiteflies</strong> (not to be confused with &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jumping_plant_louse">jumping plant lice</a>&quot; or true <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whitefly">whiteflies</a>), are small sap-sucking <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s, and members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">superfamily</a> <strong>Aphidoidea</strong>.(ref: McGavin)(ref: McGavin)<span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="George C. McGavin " data-year="1993 " data-isbn="0-8160-2737-4 " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" infobase_publishing"="">Infobase Publishing&quot;&gt;Bugs of the World </span> The damage they do to plants has made them enemies of farmers and gardeners the world over, though from a zoological standpoint they are a highly successful group of organisms.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" ross_piper"="">Ross Piper &quot; data-publisher=&quot;Greenwood Press &quot; data-year=&quot;2007 &quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-313-33922-6 &quot; data-pages=&quot;6&#x2013;9&quot;&gt;Extraordinary Animals: An Encyclopedia of Curious and Unusual Animals </span></ref> 
Their success is due in part to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/asexual_reproduction">asexual reproductive</a> capabilities of some species.
About 4,400 species are known, all included in the family Aphididae.(ref: ASF) Around 250 species are serious pests for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/agriculture">agriculture</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forestry">forestry</a> as well as an annoyance for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gardener">gardener</a>s. They vary in length from [1to10mm2].
Natural enemies include predatory <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coccinellidae">ladybirds</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hoverfly">hoverfly</a> larvae, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasitic_wasp">parasitic wasp</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphidoletes_aphidimyza">aphid midge larvae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crab_spider">crab spider</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lacewing">lacewing</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/entomopathogenic_fungus">entomopathogenic fungi</a> such as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lecanicillium_lecanii">Lecanicillium lecanii</a></em> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entomophthorales">Entomophthorales</a>.
The <strong>Arabian leopard</strong> (<em>Panthera pardus nimr</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and classified as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered">Critically Endangered</a></em> by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN">IUCN</a> since 1996. Fewer than 200 wild individuals were estimated to be alive in 2006. The population comprises fewer than 250 mature individuals and is severely fragmented. Subpopulations are isolated and not larger than 50 mature individuals. The population is thought to decline continuously.
The Arabian leopard is one of the smallest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a> subspecies.<ref>Biquand, S. (1990). Short review of the status of the Arabian leopard, <em>Panthera pardus nimr</em>, in the Arabian Peninsula. In: Shoemaker, A. (ed.). International Leopard Studbook, Riverbanks Zoological Park, Columbia.</ref>
It was tentatively affirmed as a distinct subspecies by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetics">genetic</a> analysis from a single captive leopard from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Israel">Israel</a> of south Arabian origin, which appeared most closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_leopard">African leopard</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="Uphyrkina, O., Johnson, W. E., Quigley, H., Miquelle, D., Marker, L., Bush, M., O&apos;Brien, S. " data-year="2001 " data-journal="Molecular Ecology " data-volume=" 10 " data-issue="11 " data-pages=" 2617&#x2013;2633 " data-url="http://www.biosoil.ru/files/00001386.pdf " data-pmid="11883877 " data-doi="10.1046/j.0962-1083.2001.01350.x">Phylogenetics, genome diversity and origin of modern leopard, Panthera pardus </span></ref>
An <strong>antelope</strong> is a member of a number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a> species indigenous to various regions in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. Antelopes comprise a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wastebasket_taxon">wastebasket taxon</a> (miscellaneous group) within the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovid">Bovidae</a>, encompassing those <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a> species that are neither <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle">cattle</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_buffalo">buffalo</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bison">bison</a>, nor <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goat">goat</a>s. A group of antelope is called a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herd">herd</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.hintsandthings.co.uk/kennel/collectives.htm " data-publisher="Hintsandthings.co.uk " data-date="1980-04-28 " data-accessdate="2013-07-29">Collective nouns, groups of animals, terms for animal and other groups including birds </span></ref>
The <strong>giraffe</strong> (<em>Giraffa camelopardalis</em>) is an African <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largest_mammals#Even-toed_Ungulates_.28Artiodactyla.29">tallest</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">living</a> terrestrial animal and the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ruminant">ruminant</a>. Its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/binomial_nomenclature">species name</a> refers to its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camel">camel</a>-like shape and its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a>-like colouring. Its chief distinguishing characteristics are its extremely long neck and legs, its horn-like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ossicone">ossicone</a>s, and its distinctive coat patterns. It is classified under the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giraffidae">Giraffidae</a>, along with its closest extant relative, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/okapi">okapi</a>. The nine subspecies are distinguished by their coat patterns.
The giraffe&apos;s scattered range extends from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chad">Chad</a> in the north to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa">South Africa</a> in the south, and from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niger">Niger</a> in the west to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Somalia">Somalia</a> in the east. Giraffes usually inhabit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savanna">savanna</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grassland">grassland</a>s, and open <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/woodland">woodland</a>s. Their primary food source is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimosoideae#Acacieae">acacia</a> leaves, which they browse at heights most other herbivores cannot reach. Giraffes are preyed on by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lion">lion</a>s; their calves are also targeted by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spotted_hyena">spotted hyena</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lycaon_pictus">wild dog</a>s. Adult giraffes do not have strong social bonds, though they do gather in loose aggregations if they happen to be moving in the same general direction. Males establish social hierarchies through &quot;necking&quot;, which are combat bouts where the neck is used as a weapon. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dominance_(ethology)">Dominant</a> males gain mating access to females, which bear the sole responsibility for raising the young.
The giraffe has intrigued various cultures, both ancient and modern, for its peculiar appearance, and has often been featured in paintings, books, and cartoons. It is classified by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_Concern">Least Concern</a>, but has been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extirpated">extirpated</a> from many parts of its former range, and some subspecies are classified as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered">Endangered</a>. Nevertheless, giraffes are still found in numerous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/national_park">national park</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/game_reserve">game reserve</a>s.

<strong>Pocket gophers</strong>, commonly referred to as <strong>gophers</strong>, are burrowing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s of the family <strong>Geomyidae</strong>.<ref><a href="http://www.dfw.state.or.us/species/mammals/gopher.asp">Oregon Dept. of Fish and Wildlife</a></ref> There are approximately 35 species of gopher living in Central and North America.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.outwitcritters.com/gopher/" data-accessdate="16 January 2014" data-quote="There are 35 species of gophers living in both North and Central America.">Outwit Critters:</span></ref>  They are commonly known for their extensive tunneling activities. Gophers are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemism">endemic</a> to North and Central America.
The name &quot;pocket gopher&quot; on its own may be used to refer to any of a number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a>. These are the &quot;true&quot; gophers; however there are several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ground_squirrel">ground squirrel</a>s in the distantly related family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciuridae">Sciuridae</a> that are often mistakenly called gophers as well.
<strong>Bandicoots</strong> are a group of about 20 species of small to medium-sized, terrestrial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsupial">marsupial</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/omnivore">omnivore</a>s in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peramelemorphia">Peramelemorphia</a>. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endemic">endemic</a> to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>&#x2013;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a> region.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
A <strong>black panther</strong> is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanism">melanistic</a> color variant of any <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panthera">Panthera</a></em> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>. Black panthers in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a>s (<em>Panthera pardus</em>) and black panthers in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a> are black <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/jaguar">jaguar</a>s (<em>Panthera onca</em>).(ref: Eizirik03)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-author="Eizirik, E., Yuhki, N., Johnson, W. E., Menotti-Raymond, M., Hannah, S. S., O&apos;Brien, S. J. " data-journal="Current Biology " data-year="2003 " data-volume="13 " data-issue="5 " data-pages="448&#x2013;453 " data-doi="10.1016/S0960-9822(03)00128-3 " data-url="http://www.pucrs.br/fabio/genomaenglish/index_arquivos/artigos_eduardo/Copy%20of%20Melanism_CB_reprint.pdf " data-pmid="12620197">Molecular Genetics and Evolution of Melanism in the Cat Family </span>
The <strong>goldfish</strong> (<em>Carassius auratus</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/freshwater_fish">freshwater fish</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyprinidae">Cyprinidae</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cypriniformes">Cypriniformes</a>. It was one of the earliest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> to be <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domesticated</a>, and is one of the most commonly kept <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_freshwater_aquarium_fish_species">aquarium fish</a>.
A relatively small member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carp">carp</a> family (which also includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/koi">koi carp</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crucian_carp">crucian carp</a>), the goldfish is a domesticated version of a less-colourful carp (<em>Carassius auratus</em>) native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/east_Asia">east Asia</a>. It was first domesticated in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China">China</a> more than a thousand years ago, and several distinct <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s have since been developed. Goldfish breeds vary greatly in size, body shape, fin configuration and colouration (various combinations of white, yellow, orange, red, brown, and black are known).
The mutation that gave rise to the domestic goldfish is also known from other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyprinidae">cyprinid</a> species, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_carp">common carp</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tench">tench</a>.
<strong>Fowl</strong> are birds belonging to one of two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">biological orders</a>, namely the gamefowl or landfowl (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galliformes">Galliformes</a>) and the waterfowl (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anseriformes">Anseriformes</a>). Studies of anatomical and molecular similarities suggest these two groups are close <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/evolution">evolution</a>ary relatives; together, they form the fowl <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> which is scientifically known as <strong>Galloanserae</strong> (initially termed Galloanseri). This clade is also supported by morphological and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_sequence">DNA sequence</a> data as well as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/retrotransposon">retrotransposon</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retrotransposon_Marker">presence/absence data</a>.<ref>Kriegs <em>et al.</em> (2007)</ref>
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
<strong>Megabats</strong> constitute the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/suborder">suborder</a> Megachiroptera, and its only family <strong>Pteropodidae</strong> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)">order</a> Chiroptera (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bat">bat</a>s). They are also called <strong>fruit bats</strong>, <strong>Old World fruit bats</strong>,(ref: MickleburghEtAl)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://data.iucn.org/dbtw-wpd/html/old%20world%20fruit%20bats/chapter%201.html" data-author="Mickleburgh, Hutson and Racey" data-publisher="International Union for Conservation of Nature" data-accessdate="July 19, 2013">Old World Fruit Bats:Introduction</span> or, especially the genera <em>Acerodon</em> and <em>Pteropus</em>, <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flying_fox">flying fox</a>es</strong>.  Fruit bats are restricted to the Old World in a tropical and subtropical distribution, ranging no further than the eastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia">South Asia</a>, and are absent from northwest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a> and southwest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>.(ref: LuzynskiEtAl)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://animaldiversity.ummz.umich.edu/accounts/Pteropodidae/" data-publisher="Animal Diversity Web/University of Michigan" data-author="Kenneth Cody Luzynski; Emily Margaret Sluzas; Megan Marie Wallen">PteropodidaeOld World fruit bats</span>(ref: Smith)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://people.wku.edu/charles.smith/faunmaps/Pteropodidae.htm" data-author="Charles H. Smith" data-publisher="MAMMFAUN/University of Kentucy">PTEROPODIDAE (Fruit Bats/Flying Foxes)</span> <em>(includes range map)</em> Compared to insectivorous bats, fruit bats are relatively large and, with some exceptions, do not navigate by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/animal_echolocation">echolocation</a>. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivore">herbivore</a>s and rely on their keen senses of sight and smell to locate food.(ref: Neuweiler2000)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last="Neuweiler" data-first="Gerhard" data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-accessdate="28 March 2015" data-year="2000" data-publisher="Oxford University Press" data-isbn="978-0-19-509950-8">The Biology of Bats</span>
There are hundreds of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s in existence.(ref: Ekarius 2007 23)  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domesticated">Domesticated</a> for thousands of years, distinguishable breeds of chicken have been present since the combined factors of geographical isolation and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selective_breeding">selection</a> for desired characteristics created regional types with distinct physical and behavioral traits passed on to their offspring.
The physical traits used to distinguish chicken breeds are size, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plumage">plumage</a> color, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comb_(anatomy)">comb</a> type, skin color, number of toes, amount of feathering, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg_(biology)">egg</a> color, and place of origin.(ref: Ekarius 2007 23)(ref: Ekarius 2007 23)
In the 21st century, chickens are frequently bred according to predetermined <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed_standard">breed standard</a>s set down by governing organizations. The first of such standards was the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Poultry_Standard">British Poultry Standard</a>, which is still in publication today.(ref: Ekarius 2007)(ref: Heinrichs 2007) Other standards include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_of_Perfection">Standard of Perfection</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australian_Poultry_Standard">Australian Poultry Standard</a>, and the standard of the American Bantam Association, which deals exclusively with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bantam_chicken">bantam</a> fowl.(ref: Heinrichs 2007)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Livestock_show">shown</a> competitively. There are additionally a few <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybrid</a> strains which are common in the poultry world, especially in large poultry farms. These types are first generation crosses of true breeds. Hybrids do not reliably pass on their features to their offspring, but are highly valued for their producing abilities.(ref: Ekarius 2007 23)

The <strong>elk</strong> or <strong>wapiti</strong> (<em>Cervus canadensis</em>) is one of the largest species within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cervidae">Cervidae</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/deer">deer</a> family in the world, and one of the largest land <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Asia">eastern Asia</a>. This animal should not be confused with the larger <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moose">moose</a> (<em>Alces alces</em>), to which the name &quot;elk&quot; applies in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles">British Isles</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a>. Apart from the moose, the only other member of the deer family to rival the elk in size is the south Asian <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sambar_(deer)">sambar</a> (<em>Rusa unicolor</em>).
Elk range in forest and forest-edge habitat, feeding on grasses, plants, leaves and bark. Male elk have large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antler">antler</a>s which are shed each year. Males also engage in ritualized mating behaviors during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rut_(mammalian_reproduction)">rut</a>, including posturing, antler wrestling (sparring), and <em>bugling</em>, a loud series of vocalizations which establishes dominance over other males and attracts females.
Although native to North America and eastern Asia, they have adapted well to countries where they have been introduced, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>. Their great adaptability may threaten <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endemism">endemic</a> species and ecosystems into which they have been introduced. Elk are susceptible to a number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/infectious_disease">infectious disease</a>s, some of which can be transmitted to livestock. Efforts to eliminate infectious diseases from elk populations, largely through vaccination, have had mixed success.
Some cultures revere the elk as a spiritual force. In parts of Asia, antlers and their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/velvet_(antler_covering)">velvet</a> are used in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/traditional_medicine">traditional medicine</a>s. Elk are hunted as a game species; the meat is leaner and higher in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/protein">protein</a> than <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beef">beef</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a>.<ref name="Robb"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" last="Robb " first="Bob " data-author2="Bethge, Gerald " title="The Ultimate Guide to Elk Hunting " publisher="The Lyons Press " year=" 2001" isbn="1-58574-180-9 " oclc="44818360 "></span></ref>
It was long believed to be a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> of the European <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red_deer">red deer</a> (<em>Cervus elaphus</em>), but evidence from a number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mitochondrial_DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> genetic studies beginning in 1998 indicate that the two are distinct <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.<ref> <span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last="Polziehn " data-first="R. O. " data-last2="Strobeck " data-first2="C. " data-journal="Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution " data-year="1998 " data-volume="10 " data-issue="2 " data-pages="249&#x2013;258 " data-url="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1055790398905279 " data-accessdate="2014-08-03 " data-doi="10.1006/mpev.1998.0527" data-pmid="9878235">Phylogeny of Wapiti, Red Deer, Sika Deer, and Other North American Cervids as Determined from Mitochondrial DNA </span></ref><ref> <span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-year="2001 " data-journal="Animal Conservation " data-volume="4 " data-issue="1 " data-pages="1&#x2013;11 " data-url="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1017/S1367943001001019/abstract " data-accessdate="2014-08-02 " data-doi="10.1017/s1367943001001019" data-last1="Randi " data-first1="Ettore " data-last2="Mucci " data-first2="Nadia " data-last3="Claro-Hergueta " data-first3="Fran&#xE7;oise " data-last4="Bonnet " data-first4="Am&#xE9;lie " data-last5="Douzery " data-first5="Emmanuel J. P. ">A mitochondrial DNA control region phylogeny of the Cervinae: speciation in Cervus and implications for conservation </span></ref><ref> <span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-authors="Pitra, Christian; Fickel, Joerns; Meijaard, Erik; Groves, P. Colin " data-journal="Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution 33 " data-year="2004 " data-pages="880&#x2013;895 " data-url="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S1055790304002374 " data-accessdate="2014-08-02 " data-doi="10.1016/j.ympev.2004.07.013 " data-volume="33">Evolution and phylogeny of old world deer </span></ref> Key morphological differences that distinguish <em>C. canadensis</em> from <em>C. elaphus</em> are the former&apos;s wider rump patch and paler-hued antlers.<ref> <span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-author="Groves, Colin " data-journal="European Journal of Wildlife Research " data-year="2006 " data-volume="52 " data-pages="14&#x2013;22 " data-url="http://archanth.anu.edu.au/sites/default/files/documents/groves_cervus2005.pdf " data-accessdate="2014-08-02 " data-doi="10.1007/s10344-005-0011-5">The genus Cervus in eastern Eurasia </span></ref>
