The <strong>guineafowl</strong> ([&#x2C8;&#x261;&#x26A;nifa&#x28A;l]; sometimes called &quot;Original Fowl&quot; or <em>guineahen</em>) are a family of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galliformes">Galliformes</a> order, although some authorities (for example the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Ornithologists&apos;_Union">American Ornithologists&apos; Union</a>) include the guineafowl as a subfamily, Numidinae, of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a>. The guineafowl are native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>, but the helmeted guineafowl as wild birds have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introduced_species">introduced</a> elsewhere.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
The <strong>Pointer</strong>, often called the <strong>English Pointer</strong>, is a medium to large-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog_breed">breed</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog">dog</a> developed as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gun_dog">gun dog</a>. It is one of several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pointing_breed">pointing breed</a>s.
<strong>Geese</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/waterfowl">waterfowl</a> belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tribe_(biology)">tribe</a> <strong>Anserini</strong> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatidae">Anatidae</a>. This tribe comprises the genera <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anser_(bird)">Anser</a></em> (the grey geese), <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Branta">Branta</a></em> (the black geese) and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chen_(genus)">Chen</a></em> (the white geese). A number of other birds, mostly related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shelduck">shelduck</a>s, have &quot;goose&quot; as part of their names. More distantly related members of the Anatidae family are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/swan">swan</a>s, most of which are larger than true geese, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/duck">duck</a>s, which are smaller.
<strong>Foxes</strong> are small-to-medium-sized, omnivorous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s belonging to several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canidae">Canidae</a> family. Foxes are slightly smaller than a medium-size domestic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog">dog</a>, with a flattened skull, upright triangular ears, a pointed, slightly upturned <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snout">snout</a>, and a long bushy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a> (or <em>brush</em>).
Twelve <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> belong to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monophyly">monophyletic</a> group of <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulpes">Vulpes</a></em> genus of &quot;true foxes&quot;. Approximately another 25 current or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extinction">extinct</a> species are always or sometimes called foxes; these foxes are either part of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/paraphyly">paraphyletic</a> group of the South American foxes, or of the outlying group, which consists of bat-eared fox, gray fox, and island fox.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last1="Macdonald" data-first1="edited by David W." data-last2="Sillero-Zubiri" data-first2="Claudio" data-date="2004" data-publisher="Oxford University Press" data-location="Oxford" data-isbn="0198515561" data-page="49" data-edition="Nachdr. d. Ausg. 2004." data-accessdate="1 October 2014">The biology and conservation of wild canids</span></ref> Foxes are found on every continent except Antarctica. By far the most common and widespread species of fox is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red_fox">red fox</a> (<em>Vulpes vulpes</em>) with about 47 recognized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a>.<ref name="Lloyd1980"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last1="Lloyd" data-first1="H.G." data-date="1981" data-publisher="Batsford" data-location="London" data-isbn="0-7134-11902" data-page="21" data-edition="2. impr." data-accessdate="1 October 2014">The red fox</span></ref> The global distribution of foxes, together with their widespread reputation for cunning, has contributed to their prominence in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/popular_culture">popular culture</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/folklore">folklore</a> in many societies around the world.  The hunting of foxes with packs of hounds, long an established pursuit in Europe, especially in the British Isles, was exported by European settlers to various parts of the New World.
The <strong>Lampyridae</strong> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> of insects in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beetle">beetle</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> Coleoptera. They are winged beetles, and commonly called <strong>fireflies</strong> or <strong>lightning bugs</strong> for their conspicuous use of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crepuscular">during twilight</a> to attract mates or prey. Fireflies produce a &quot;cold light&quot;, with no <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/infrared">infrared</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> frequencies. This chemically produced light from the lower abdomen may be yellow, green, or pale red, with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wavelength">wavelength</a>s from 510 to 670 nanometers.<ref><a href="http://science.howstuffworks.com/environmental/life/zoology/insects-arachnids/question554.htm">HowStuffWorks &quot;How do fireflies light up?&quot;</a>. Science.howstuffworks.com (19 January 2001). Retrieved on 22 June 2013.</ref>
About 2,000 species of fireflies are found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/temperate">temperate</a> and tropical environments. Many are in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsh">marsh</a>es or in wet, wooded areas where their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e have abundant sources of food. Their larvae emit light and often are called &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/glowworm">glowworm</a>s&quot;, in particular, in Eurasia. In <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/the_Americas">the Americas</a>, &quot;glow worm&quot; also refers to the related <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phengodidae">Phengodidae</a>. In many species, both male and female fireflies have the ability to fly, but in some species, the females are flightless.<ref><a href="http://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2011/04/110405093656.htm">In Fireflies, Flightless Females Lose out On Gifts from Males</a>. Science Daily (27 June 2011). Retrieved on 22 June 2013.</ref>
The <strong>Exocoetidae</strong> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marine_fish">marine fish</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beloniformes">Beloniformes</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/class_(biology)">class</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Actinopterygii">Actinopterygii</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish">Fish</a> of this family are known as <strong>flying fish</strong>. About 64 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> are grouped in seven to nine <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a>. Flying fish can make powerful, self-propelled leaps out of water into air, where their long, wing-like fins enable <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gliding_flight">gliding flight</a> for considerable distances above the water&apos;s surface. This uncommon ability is a natural defense mechanism to evade predators.
The oldest known fossil of a flying or gliding fish, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potanichthys_xingyiensis">Potanichthys xingyiensis</a></em>, dates back to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Triassic">Middle Triassic</a>, 235&#x2013;242&#xA0;million years ago. However, this fossil is not related to modern flying fish, which evolved independently about 66&#xA0;million years ago.<ref><a href="http://www.nature.com/news/oldest-flying-fish-fossil-found-in-china-1.11707">Oldest flying fish fossil found in China</a> <em>Nature</em>, News, 31 October 2012.</ref><ref>Xu, Guang-Hui; Li-Jun Zhao, Ke-Qin Gao and Fei-Xiang Wu (2012) <a href="http://rspb.royalsocietypublishing.org/content/early/2012/10/24/rspb.2012.2261">A new stem-neopterygian fish from the Middle Triassic of China shows the earliest over-water gliding strategy of the vertebrates&quot;</a> <em>Proceedings of the Royal Society B: Biological Sciences</em>, Published online before print October 31, 2012. [10.1098/rspb.2012.2261]</ref>
<strong>Kiwi</strong> (pronounced [ki&#x2D0;wi&#x2D0;]) or <strong>kiwis</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flightless_bird">flightless bird</a>s native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Apteryx</em></strong> and family <strong>Apterygidae</strong>. At around the size of a domestic chicken, kiwi are by far the smallest living <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ratite">ratite</a>s (which also consist of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ostrich">ostrich</a>es, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/emu">emu</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhea_(bird)">rhea</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassowary">cassowaries</a>), and lay the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_egg">egg</a> in relation to their body size of any species of bird in the world.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.sandiegozoo.org/animalbytes/t-kiwi.html" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" san_diego_zoo"="">San Diego Zoo&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;2008-09-19&quot;&gt;Birds: Kiwi</span></ref> DNA sequence comparisons have yielded the surprising conclusion that kiwi are much more closely related to the extinct Malagasy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elephant_bird">elephant bird</a>s than to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moa">moa</a> with which they shared New Zealand. There are five recognised species, two of which are currently <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species">vulnerable</a>, one of which is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_species">endangered</a>, and one of which is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/critically_endangered">critically endangered</a>. All species have been negatively affected by historic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deforestation_in_New_Zealand">deforestation</a> but currently the remaining large areas of their forest habitat are well protected in reserves and national parks. At present, the greatest threat to their survival is predation by invasive mammalian predators.
The kiwi is a national symbol of New Zealand, and the association is so strong that the term <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kiwi_(people)">Kiwi</a></em> is used internationally as the colloquial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/demonym">demonym</a> for New Zealanders.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://glossary.immigration.govt.nz/Kiwis.htm " data-publisher="Glossary.immigration.govt.nz " data-date=" " data-accessdate="2012-09-13">Kiwis/Kiwi - New Zealand Immigration Service (Summary of Terms) </span></ref>
The <strong>Galliformes</strong> are an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> of heavy-bodied ground-feeding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/turkey_(bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grouse">grouse</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_quail">New World quail</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_quail">Old World quail</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagopus">ptarmigan</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/partridge">partridge</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pheasant">pheasant</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/junglefowl">junglefowl</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a>.  The name derives from &quot;gallus&quot;, Latin for &quot;cock&quot; or &quot;rooster&quot;. Common names are <strong>gamefowl</strong> or <strong>gamebirds</strong>, <strong>landfowl</strong>, <strong>gallinaceous birds</strong>, or <strong>galliforms</strong>. &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfowl">Wildfowl</a>&quot; or just &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fowl">fowl</a>&quot; are also often used for the Galliformes, but usually these terms also refer to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/waterfowl">waterfowl</a> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anseriformes">Anseriformes</a>), and occasionally to other commonly hunted birds.  This group has about 290 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, one or more of which are found in essentially every part of the world&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continent">continent</a>s (except for the innermost <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a>s and perpetual <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ice">ice</a>).  They are rarer on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/island">island</a>s, and in contrast to the closely related waterfowl, are essentially absent from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ocean">ocean</a>ic islands&#x2014;unless introduced there by humans.  Several species have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> during their long and extensive relationships with humans.
This order contains five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">families</a>: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a> (including chicken, quail, partridges, pheasants, turkeys, peafowl and grouse), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odontophoridae">Odontophoridae</a> (New World quails), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numididae">Numididae</a> (guineafowl), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a> (including chachalacas and curassows), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megapodiidae">Megapodiidae</a> (incubator birds like mallee fowl and brush-turkeys). They are important as seed dispersers and predators in the ecosystems they inhabit, and are often reared as game birds by humans for their meat and eggs and for recreational hunting.  Many gallinaceous species are skilled runners and escape predators by running rather than flying.  Males of most species are more colorful than the females.  Males often have elaborate courtship behaviors that include strutting, fluffing of tail or head feathers, and vocal sounds. They are mainly nonmigratory.
<strong>Hornets</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s, and the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/eusociality">eusocial</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wasp">wasp</a>s. Some species can reach up to [5.5cmin1abbr=on] in length. The true hornets make up the genus <strong><em>Vespa</em></strong> and are distinguished from other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vespine">vespine</a>s by the width of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertex_(anatomy)">vertex</a> (part of the head behind the eyes), which is proportionally larger in <em>Vespa</em> and by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anteriorly">anteriorly</a> rounded <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gaster_(insect_anatomy)">gasters</a> (the section of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/abdomen">abdomen</a> behind the wasp waist). The best known species is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_hornet">European hornet</a> (<em>Vespa crabro</em>), about 2&#x2013;3.5&#xA0;cm in length, widely distributed throughout <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northeast_Asia">Northeast Asia</a>. Most species make exposed nests in trees and shrubs, but some (like <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vespa_orientalis">Vespa orientalis</a></em>) build their nests underground or in other cavities.
<strong>Hamsters</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <strong>Cricetinae</strong>. The subfamily contains about 25 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, classified in six or seven <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a>.(ref: Fox) They have become established as popular small house <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pet">pet</a>s and partly because they are easy to breed in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/captivity_(animal)">captivity</a>, hamsters are often used as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/animal_testing">laboratory animals</a>.
In the wild, hamsters are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crepuscular">crepuscular</a> and remain underground during the day to avoid being caught by predators. They feed primarily on seeds, fruits, and vegetation, and will occasionally eat burrowing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Patricia Pope Bartlett" data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-date="2003" data-publisher="Barron&apos;s Educational Series" data-isbn="978-0-7641-2294-1" data-page="[http://books.google.com.ph/books?id">The Hamster Handbook</span></ref> They have elongated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cheek_pouch">cheek pouch</a>es extending to their shoulders in which they carry food back to their burrows.
Hamster <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/behavior">behavior</a> varies depending on their environment, genetics, and interaction with people.
<strong>Hoverflies</strong>, sometimes called <strong>flower flies</strong>, <strong>sweat bees</strong> or <strong>syrphid flies</strong>, make up the insect <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Syrphidae</strong>. As their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> suggests, they are often seen hovering or nectaring at <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flower">flower</a>s; the adults of many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> feed mainly on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nectar">nectar</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollen">pollen</a>, while the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/maggot">maggot</a>s) eat a wide range of foods. In some species, the larvae are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/saprotroph">saprotroph</a>s, eating decaying plant and animal matter in the soil or in ponds and streams. In other species, the larvae are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insectivore">insectivore</a>s and prey on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aphid">aphid</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thrip">thrip</a>s, and other plant-sucking insects.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphid">Aphid</a>s alone cause tens of millions of dollars of damage to crops worldwide every year; because of this, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aphidophagous">aphid-eating</a> hoverflies are being recognized as important <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predation">natural enemies</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pest_(organism)">pests</a>, and potential agents for use in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biological_control">biological control</a>. Some adult syrphid flies are important <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollinator">pollinator</a>s.
About 6,000 species in 200 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> have been described. Hoverflies are common throughout the world and can be found on all continents except <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Hoverflies are harmless to most other animals, despite their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batesian_mimicry">mimicry</a> of more dangerous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wasp">wasp</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bee">bee</a>s, which wards off predators.
<strong>Grouse</strong> [&#x2C8;&#x261;ra&#x28A;s] are a group of birds from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galliformes">Galliformes</a>, in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a>. Grouse are frequently assigned to the subfamily <strong>Tetraoninae</strong> (sometimes Tetraonidae), a classification supported by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mitochondrial_DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> sequence studies, and applied by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Ornithologists&lt;em&gt;Union">American Ornithologists<em>Union</em></a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ITIS">ITIS</a>, and others. Grouse inhabit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/temperate">temperate</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subarctic">subarctic</a> regions of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_hemisphere">northern hemisphere</a>, from pine forests to moorland and mountainside, from 83&#xB0; North (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rock_ptarmigan">rock ptarmigan</a> in northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland">Greenland</a>) to 28&#xB0; North (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Attwater&lt;/em&gt;s_prairie_chicken">Attwaters prairie chicken</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Texas">Texas</a>).
  }}
<strong>Kingfishers</strong> are a group of small to medium-sized brightly colored <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coraciiformes">Coraciiformes</a>. They have a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cosmopolitan_distribution">cosmopolitan distribution</a>, with most species found outside of the Americas. The group is treated either as a single <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a>, Alcedinidae, or as a suborder <strong>Alcedines</strong> containing three families, Alcedinidae (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/river_kingfisher">river kingfisher</a>s), Halcyonidae (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tree_kingfisher">tree kingfisher</a>s), and Cerylidae (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/water_kingfisher">water kingfisher</a>s). There are roughly 90 species of kingfisher. All have large heads, long, sharp, pointed bills, short legs, and stubby tails. Most species have bright <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plumage">plumage</a> with little differences between the sexes. Most species are tropical in distribution, and a slight majority are found only in forests. They consume a wide range of prey as well as fish, usually caught by swooping down from a perch. While kingfishers are usually thought to live near rivers and eat fish, most of the kingfisher species in the world live away from water and eat small invertebrates. Like other members of their order they nest in cavities, usually tunnels dug into the natural or artificial banks in the ground. A quarter of all kingfishers nest in disused termite nests. A few species, principally insular forms, are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/threatened">threatened</a> with extinction. In Britain, the word kingfisher normally refers to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_kingfisher">common kingfisher</a>.
A hedgehog  is any of the spiny <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <strong>Erinaceinae</strong>, in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erinaceomorpha">Erinaceomorpha</a>. There are seventeen <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of hedgehog in five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a>, found through parts of Europe, Asia, Africa and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a> (by introduction). There are no hedgehogs native to Australia, and no living species native to the Americas. Hedgehogs share distant ancestry with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shrew">shrew</a>s (family Soricidae), with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gymnure">gymnure</a>s possibly being the intermediate link, and have changed little over the last 15 million years.<ref name="13" ways="">Reiter C, Gould GC, <a href="http://findarticles.com/p/articles/mi_m1134/is_n6_v107/ai_21031853/?tag=content;col1">&quot;Thirteen Ways of Looking at a Hedgehog&quot;</a> Natural History, Jul&#x2013;Aug 1998</ref> Like many of the first mammals, they have adapted to a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nocturnal">nocturnal</a> way of life.(ref: nocturnal)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.wildlifetrust.org.uk/facts/hedge.htm " data-publisher="WildlifeTrust.org.uk " data-accessdate="2013-02-28">WildlifeTrust.org.uk </span> Hedgehogs&apos; spiny protection resembles that of the unrelated rodent <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/porcupine">porcupine</a>s and monotreme <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/echidna">echidna</a>s.
The name <em>hedgehog</em> came into use around the mid 15th century (they were earlier known as <em>igls</em>). &quot;Hedge&quot; refers to its usual habitat, the hedgerow, while &quot;hog&quot; refers to its piglike snout, and the snuffling noise it makes whilst foraging. Other names include <em>urchin</em>, <em>hedgepig</em> and <em>furze-pig</em>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/collective_noun">collective noun</a> for a group of hedgehogs is <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_English_terms_of_venery,_by_animal#H">array</a></em> or <em>prickle</em>.
The common <strong>hippopotamus</strong> (<em>Hippopotamus amphibius</em>), or <strong>hippo</strong>, is a large, mostly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivore">herbivorous</a> mammal in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sub-Saharan_Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, and one of only two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scientific_classification">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippopotamidae">Hippopotamidae</a>, the other being the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pygmy_hippopotamus">pygmy hippopotamus</a> (<em>Choeropsis liberiensis</em> or <em>Hexaprotodon liberiensis</em>).  The name comes from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ancient_Greek">ancient Greek</a> for &quot;river horse&quot; ([grc&#x1F31;&#x3C0;&#x3C0;&#x3BF;&#x3C0;&#x3CC;&#x3C4;&#x3B1;&#x3BC;&#x3BF;&#x3C2;]). After the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elephant">elephant</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>, the common hippopotamus is the third-<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Largest_mammals#Even-toed_Ungulates_(Artiodactyla)">largest type of land mammal</a> and the heaviest extant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Even-toed_ungulate">artiodactyl</a>. Despite their physical resemblance to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pig">pig</a>s and other terrestrial even-toed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ungulate">ungulate</a>s, the closest living relatives of the Hippopotamidae are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cetacea">cetacea</a>ns (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/whale">whale</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/porpoise">porpoise</a>s, etc.) from which they diverged about [55million years ago]. The common ancestor of whales and hippos split from other even-toed ungulates around [60million years ago]. The earliest known hippopotamus fossils, belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenyapotamus">Kenyapotamus</a></em> in Africa, date to around [16million years ago].
Common hippos are recognisable by their barrel-shaped torsos, wide-opening mouths revealing large canine tusks, nearly hairless bodies, columnar-like legs and large size; adults average [1500kglbsabbr=on] and [1300kglbsabbr=on] for males and females respectively, making them the largest species of land mammal after the 3 species of elephants and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_rhinoceros">white</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_rhinoceros">Indian rhinoceros</a>. Despite its stocky shape and short legs, it is capable of running [30km/habbr=on] over short distances. The hippopotamus is a highly aggressive and unpredictable animal and is ranked among the most dangerous animals in Africa.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url=" http://animals.nationalgeographic.com/animals/wild/shows-deadly-60/fun-facts/" data-publisher=" &lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" national_geographic_(magazine)"_data-national_geographic"="">National Geographic (magazine)&quot; data-National Geographic=&quot;undefined&quot; data-archiveurl=&quot; https://web.archive.org/web/20141016145736/http://animals.nationalgeographic.com/animals/wild/shows-deadly-60/fun-facts/&quot; data-archivedate=&quot; 2014-10-16&quot; data-deadurl=&quot; no&quot;&gt; Deadly 60: 15 Deadly Animal Facts</span></ref> Nevertheless, they are still threatened by habitat loss and poaching for their meat and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ivory">ivory</a> canine teeth.
The common hippopotamus is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/semiaquatic">semiaquatic</a>, inhabiting rivers, lakes and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mangrove">mangrove</a> swamps, where territorial bulls preside over a stretch of river and groups of five to 30 females and young. During the day, they remain cool by staying in the water or mud; reproduction and childbirth both occur in water. They emerge at dusk to graze on grasses. While hippopotamuses rest near each other in the water, grazing is a solitary activity and hippos are not <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Territory_(animal)">territorial</a> on land.
A <strong>falcon</strong> ([&#x2C8;f&#x254;&#x2D0;lk&#x259;n,_&#x2C8;f&#xE6;l-]) is any one of 37 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_of_prey">raptors</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Falco</em>, widely distributed on all continents of the world except <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_birds_of_Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.
Adult falcons have thin, tapered wings, which enable them to fly at high speed and to change direction rapidly. Fledgling falcons, in their first year of flying, have longer <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flight_feather">flight feather</a>s, which make their configuration more like that of a general-purpose bird such as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wikt:broadwing">broadwing</a>. This makes it easier to fly while learning the exceptional skills required to be effective hunters as adults.
The falcons are the largest genus in the Falconinae subfamily of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Falconidae">Falconidae</a>, which itself also includes another subfamily comprising <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caracara">caracara</a>s and a few other species. All these birds kill with their beaks, using a &quot;tooth&quot; on the side of their beaks &#x2014; unlike the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hawk">hawk</a>s, eagles, and other birds of prey in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>, which use their feet.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peregrine_falcon">Peregrine falcon</a>s have been recorded diving at speeds of 200 miles per hour (320&#xA0;km/h), making them the fastest-moving creatures on Earth.<ref>&quot;The Speed of Animals&quot; in <em>The New Book of Knowledge</em>, Grolier Academic Reference, 2003, p. 278, ISBN 071720538X</ref> Other falcons include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gyrfalcon">gyrfalcon</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lanner_falcon">lanner falcon</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/merlin_(bird)">merlin</a>. Some small falcons with long, narrow wings are called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hobby_(bird)">hobbies</a>, and some which hover while hunting are called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kestrel_(bird)">kestrels</a>. 
As is the case with many birds of prey, falcons have exceptional powers of vision; the visual acuity of one species has been measured at 2.6 times that of a normal human.
The traditional term for a male falcon is tercel (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_English">British</a> spelling) or tiercel (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_English">American</a> spelling), from Latin <em>tertius</em> = <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3_(number)">third</a> because of the belief that only one in three eggs hatched a male bird. Some sources give the etymology as deriving from the fact that a male falcon is about one-third smaller than the female<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/tercel " data-publisher="Dictionary.reference.com " data-accessdate="2010-03-20">tercel </span></ref> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_French">Old French</a> <em>tiercelet</em>). A falcon chick, especially one reared for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/falconry">falconry</a>, still in its downy stage is known as an eyas <ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/eyas " data-publisher="Thefreedictionary.com " data-date=" " data-accessdate="2010-03-20">eyas </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.tiscali.co.uk/reference/dictionaries/difficultwords/data/d0005484.html " data-publisher="Tiscali.co.uk " data-date="1964-09-21 " data-accessdate="2010-03-20">Dictionary of Difficult Words &#x2013; eyas</span></ref> (sometimes spelt eyass). The word arose by mistaken division of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_French">Old French</a> <em>un niais</em>, from Latin presumed <em>nidiscus</em> (&quot;nestling&quot;, from <em>nidus</em> = <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nest">nest</a>). The technique of hunting with trained captive birds of prey is known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/falconry">falconry</a>.
<strong>Frogs</strong> are a diverse and largely <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnivore">carnivorous</a> group of short-bodied, tailless <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/amphibian">amphibian</a>s composing the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <strong>Anura</strong> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <em>an-,</em> without + <em>oura</em>, tail). The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triadobatrachus">oldest fossil &quot;proto-frog&quot;</a> appeared in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/early_Triassic">early Triassic</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, but <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/molecular_clock">molecular clock dating</a> suggests their origins may extend further back to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Permian">Permian</a>, 265 million years ago. Frogs are widely distributed, ranging from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropics">tropics</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subarctic">subarctic</a> regions, but the greatest concentration of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species_diversity">species diversity</a> is found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical_rainforest">tropical rainforest</a>s. There are approximately 4,800 recorded species, accounting for over 85% of extant amphibian species. They are also one of the five most diverse <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertebrate">vertebrate</a> orders.
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/body_plan">body plan</a> of an adult frog is generally characterized by a stout body, protruding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/eye">eye</a>s, cleft <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tongue">tongue</a>, limbs folded underneath, and the absence of a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a> in adults. Besides living in fresh water and on dry land, the adults of some species are adapted for living underground or in trees. The skin of the frog is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gland">gland</a>ular, with secretions ranging from distasteful to toxic. Warty species of frog tend to be called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/toad">toad</a>s but the distinction between frogs and toads is based on informal naming conventions concentrating on the warts rather than <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomy</a> or evolutionary history; some toads are more closely related to frogs than to other toads. Frogs&apos; skins vary in colour from well-<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camouflage">camouflage</a>d dappled brown, grey and green to vivid patterns of bright red or yellow and black to advertise toxicity and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aposematism">warn off predators</a>.
Frogs typically lay their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/egg_(biology)">eggs</a> in water. The eggs hatch into aquatic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tadpole">tadpole</a>s that have tails and internal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill">gill</a>s. They have highly specialized rasping mouth parts suitable for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivore">herbivorous</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/omnivore">omnivorous</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/planktivore">planktivorous</a> diets. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biological_life_cycle">life cycle</a> is completed when they <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metamorphosis">metamorphose</a> into adults. A few species deposit eggs on land or bypass the tadpole stage. Adult frogs generally have a carnivorous diet consisting of small invertebrates, but omnivorous species exist and a few feed on fruit. Frogs are extremely efficient at converting what they eat into body mass. They are an important food source for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predation">predators</a> and part of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/food_web">food web</a> dynamics of many of the world&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecosystem">ecosystem</a>s. The skin is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/semi-permeable">semi-permeable</a>, making them susceptible to dehydration, so they either live in moist places or have special adaptations to deal with dry habitats. Frogs produce a wide range of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_communication">vocalizations</a>, particularly in their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breeding_season">breeding season</a>, and exhibit many different kinds of complex <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethology">behaviours</a> to attract mates, to fend off predators and to generally survive.
Frog populations have declined significantly since the 1950s. More than one third of species are considered to be <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Threatened_species">threatened</a> with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extinct">extinct</a>ion and over one hundred and twenty are believed to have become extinct since the 1980s. The number of malformations among frogs is on the rise and an emerging <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fungus">fungal</a> disease, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chytridiomycosis">chytridiomycosis</a>, has spread around the world. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_biology">Conservation biologists</a> are working to understand the causes of these problems and to resolve them. Frogs are valued as food by humans and also have many cultural roles in literature, symbolism and religion.
<strong>Badgers</strong> are short-legged <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/omnivore">omnivore</a>s in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelidae">Mustelidae</a> which also includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/otter">otter</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polecat">polecat</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/weasel">weasel</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wolverine">wolverine</a>s. They belong to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caniform">caniform</a> sub-order of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnivoran">Carnivoran</a> mammals.
The 11 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of badger are grouped in three subfamilies: Melinae (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a>n badgers), Mellivorinae (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/honey_badger">honey badger</a> or ratel) and Taxideinae (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_badger">American badger</a>). The Asiatic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stink_badger">stink badger</a>s of the genus <em>Mydaus</em> were formerly included within Melinae (and thus Mustelidae), but recent genetic evidence<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-="" author="Goswami, Anjali and Friscia, Anthony " title="Carnivoran Evolution: New Views on Phylogeny, Form and Function" date="2010" publisher="Cambridge University Press" isbn="978-0-521-73586-5" data-page="30"></span></ref> indicates these are actually members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skunk">skunk</a> family, placing them in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taxonomic_family">taxonomic family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mephitidae">Mephitidae</a>.
Badgers include the species in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> <em>Meles</em>, <em>Arctonyx</em>, <em>Taxidea</em> and <em>Mellivora</em>. Their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mandible">lower jaws</a> are articulated to the upper by means of transverse <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandibular_condyle">condyles</a> firmly locked into long cavities of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skull">skull</a>, so dislocation of the jaw is all but impossible. This enables the badgers to maintain their hold with the utmost tenacity, but limits jaw movement to hinging open and shut, or sliding from side to side without the twisting movement possible for the jaws of most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s.
Badgers have rather short, fat bodies, with short legs for digging. They have elongated weasel-like heads with small ears. Their tails vary in length depending on species; the stink badger has a very short tail, while the ferret badger&apos;s tail can be [46-51cminabbr=on] long, depending on age. They have black faces with distinctive white markings, gray bodies with a light-coloured stripe from head to tail, and dark legs with light coloured underbellies. They grow to around [90cmin] in length including tail. The European badger is one of the largest; the American badger, the hog badger and the honey badger are generally a little smaller and lighter. The stink badgers are smaller still, and the ferret badgers are the smallest of all. They weigh around [9.1-11kglbabbr=on] on average, with some Eurasian badgers weighing in at around [18kglbsabbr=on].<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-="" url="http://www.badgers.org.uk/badgerpages/" archiveurl="https://web.archive.org/web/20120205092802/http://www.badgers.org.uk/badgerpages/" archivedate="5 February 2012" title="Badger Pages: Photos of and facts about the badgers of the world " publisher="Badgers.org.uk " accessdate="31 December 2011"></span></ref>
<strong>Leeches</strong> are segmented worms that belong to the phylum <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annelida">Annelida</a> and comprise the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(biology)">subclass</a> <strong>Hirudinea</strong>.<ref name="Buchsbaum"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="  Ralph Buchsbaum, Mildred Buchsbaum, John Pearse, &amp; Vicki Pearse " data-title=" Animals Without Backbone " data-edition="3rd " data-publisher=" The University of Chicago Press " data-location=" Chicago " data-year=" 1987 " data-isbn=" 0-226-07874-4 " data-pages="312&#x2013;317"></span></ref> Like the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligochaeta">oligochaetes</a>, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/earthworm">earthworm</a>s, leeches share a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clitellum">clitellum</a> and are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a>s. Nevertheless, they differ from the oligochaetes in significant ways. For example, leeches do not have bristles and the external segmentation of their bodies does not correspond with the internal segmentation of their organs. Their bodies are much more solid as the spaces in their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coelom">coelom</a> are dense with connective tissues. They also have two suckers, one at each end.
The majority of leeches live in freshwater environments, while some species can be found in terrestrial(ref: Notes on the Feeding of Land Leeches)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-author="S. Fogden &amp; J. Proctor" data-="" title="Notes on the Feeding of Land Leeches (Haemadipsa zeylanica Moore and H. picta Moore) in Gunung Mulu National Park, Sarawak " data-journal="Biotropica " data-volume="17 " data-issue="2 " data-pages="172&#x2013;174 " data-year="1985 " data-doi="10.2307/2388511" jstor="2388511 " last2="Proctor "></span> and marine environments, as well.  The best-known leeches, such as the medicinal leech, <em>Hirudo medicinalis</em>, are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hematophagous">hematophagous</a>, feeding on vertebrate blood and invertebrate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hemolymph">hemolymph</a>.<ref name="Sawyer"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author=" Roy Sawyer" data-="" title=" Neurobiology of the Leech " editor1-first=" Muller " data-editor1-last=" Kenneth " editor2-first=" John " data-editor2-last=" Nicholls " editor3-first=" Gunther " data-editor3-last="Stent " data-publisher=" Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory " location=" New York " year=" 1981 " isbn=" 0-87969-146-8 " data-pages="7&#x2013;26"></span></ref>  Most leech species, however, are predatory, feeding primarily by swallowing other invertebrates.
Almost 700 species of leeches are currently recognized, of which some 100 are marine, 90 terrestrial and the remainder freshwater <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taxa">taxa</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Boris Sket, Peter Trontelj " data-year="2008 " data-chapter="Global diversity of leeches (Hirudinea) in freshwater " data-journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" hydrobiologia"="">Hydrobiologia &quot; data-volume=&quot;595 &quot; data-issue=&quot;1 &quot; data-pages=&quot;129&#x2013;137 &quot; data-doi=&quot;10.1007/s10750-007-9010-8 &quot; data-editor=&quot;E. V. Balian, C. L&#xE9;v&#xEA;que, H. Segers &amp; K. Martens&quot;&gt;Freshwater Animal Diversity Assessment </span></ref>
Leeches, such as the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hirudo_medicinalis">Hirudo medicinalis</a></em>, have been historically used in medicine to remove blood from patients.<ref name="Payton1"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" author=" Brian Payton " title=" Neurobiology of the Leech " editor=" Kenneth Muller, John Nicholls, &amp; Gunther Stent " data-publisher=" Cold Spring Harbor Laboratory " location=" New York " year=" 1981 " isbn=" 0-87969-146-8 " data-pages="27&#x2013;34"></span></ref> The practice of leeching can be traced to ancient India and Greece, and continued well into the 18th and 19th centuries in both Europe and North America. In modern times, leeches are used medically in procedures such as the reattachment of body parts and reconstructive and plastic surgeries (ref: Adams)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Adam " data-first1="Robert " data-last2="Zakrzewski " data-first2="Peter " data-publisher="University of Toronto Medical Journal " data-volume="79 " data-issue="1 " data-pages="65&#x2013;7 " data-year="2001 " data-url="http://utmj.org/ojs/index.php/UTMJ/article/view/948">Therapeutic Use of Leeches: From the Annelids or Medicine </span> and, in Germany, treating osteoarthritis.(ref: teut)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="M. Teut &amp; A. Warning" data-year="2008" data-journal="Forsch Komplementmed" data-volume="15" data-issue="5" data-pages="269&#x2013;72" data-doi="10.1159/000158875" data-pmid="19001824" data-last2="Warning">Leeches, phytotherapy and physiotherapy in osteo-arthrosis of the knee&#x2014;a geriatric case study</span><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-pmid="12233807 " data-year="2002 " data-last1="Michalsen " data-first1="A " data-last2="Moebus " data-first2="S " data-last3="Spahn " data-first3="G " data-last4="Esch " data-first4="T " data-last5="Langhorst " data-first5="J " data-last6="Dobos " data-first6="GJ " data-volume="8 " data-issue="5 " data-pages="84&#x2013;8 " data-journal="Alternative therapies in health and medicine">Leech therapy for symptomatic treatment of knee osteoarthritis: Results and implications of a pilot study </span></ref>
<strong>Gulls</strong>, often referred to as <strong>seagulls</strong>,<ref><a href="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/seagull">&quot;Seagull&quot;</a>. Merriam&#x2013;Webster</ref> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seabird">seabird</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Laridae</strong> in the suborder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lari">Lari</a>. They are most closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tern">tern</a>s (family Sternidae) and only distantly related to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/auk">auk</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skimmer">skimmer</a>s, and more distantly to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wader">wader</a>s. Until the 21st century, most gulls were placed in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larus">Larus</a></em>, but this arrangement is now known to be <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polyphyly">polyphyletic</a>, leading to the resurrection of several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus">genera</a>. An older name for gulls is <strong>mew</strong>, cognate with German <em>M&#xF6;we</em>, Danish <em>m&#xE5;ge</em>, Dutch <em>meeuw</em>, and French <em>mouette</em>; this term can still be found in certain regional dialects.<ref><a href="http://machaut.uchicago.edu/?action=search&amp;resource=Webster%27s&amp;word=Mew&amp;quicksearch=on">Webster&apos;s Revised Unabridged Dictionary, 1913</a></ref><ref><a href="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/mew">The American Heritage Dictionary of the English Language, Fourth Edition</a></ref><ref><a href="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/mew">Merriam-Webster Online</a></ref>
Gulls are typically medium to large birds, usually grey or white, often with black markings on the head or wings. They typically have harsh wailing or squawking calls, stout, longish <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beak">bill</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/webbed_toes">webbed feet</a>. Most gulls, particularly <em>Larus</em> species, are ground-nesting <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivore">carnivore</a>s, which take live food or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scavenger">scavenge</a> opportunistically. Live food often includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crab">crab</a>s and small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a>. Gulls have unhinging jaws which allow them to consume large prey. Apart from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kittiwake">kittiwake</a>s, gulls are typically coastal or inland species, rarely venturing far out to sea.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="news " data-url=" http://www.allaboutbirds.org/guide/herring_gull/lifehistory#at_behavior" data-title=" Herring Gull" data-work=" The Cornell Lab of Ornithology" data-date=" 3 August 2011"></span></ref> The large species take up to four years to attain full adult <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feathers">plumage</a>, but two years is typical for small gulls. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gull#Taxonomy">Large white-headed gulls</a> are typically long-lived birds, with a maximum age of 49 years recorded for the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_herring_gull">herring gull</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://genomics.senescence.info/species/entry.php?species" data-work="The Animal Ageing and Longevity Database" data-accessdate="23 November 2008">AnAge entry for Larus argentatus</span></ref>
Gulls nest in large, densely packed noisy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_colony">colonies</a>. They lay two or three speckled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg_(biology)">eggs</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_nest">nests</a> composed of vegetation. The young are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/precocial">precocial</a>, being born with dark mottled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Down_feather">down</a>, and mobile upon hatching.<ref name="EoB"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-editor="Forshaw, Joseph" data-author=" Harrison, Colin J.O." data-year="1991" data-publisher=" Merehurst Press" data-location="London" data-pages=" 109&#x2013;111" data-isbn=" 1-85391-186-0">Encyclopaedia of Animals: Birds</span></ref>
Gulls&#x2014;the larger species in particular&#x2014;are resourceful, inquisitive and intelligent(ref: rspb)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.rspb.org.uk/wildlife/features/gulls.aspx" data-date="13 August 2007" data-publisher="The Royal Society for the Protection of Birds" data-accessdate="4 January 2010">Gulls and man</span> birds, demonstrating complex methods of communication and a highly developed social structure. For example, many gull colonies display <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mobbing_behaviour">mobbing behaviour</a>, attacking and harassing would-be predators and other intruders.<ref><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Alcock_(behavioral_ecologist)">Alcock, J.</a> (1998) Animal Behavior: An Evolutionary Approach (7th edition). Sinauer Associates, Inc. Sunderland, Massachusetts. ISBN 0-87893-009-4</ref> Certain species (e.g. the herring gull) have exhibited <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals">tool use behaviour</a>, using pieces of bread as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bait_(luring_substance)">bait</a> with which to catch <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goldfish">goldfish</a>, for example.(ref: henry)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Henry" data-first="Pierre-Yves" data-author2="Jean-Christophe Aznar" data-date="June 2006" data-journal="Waterbirds" data-publisher="The Waterbird Society" data-volume="29" data-issue="2" data-pages="233&#x2013;234" data-doi="10.1675/1524-4695(2006)29233:TICABB2.0.CO;2">Tool-use in Charadrii: Active Bait-Fishing by a Herring Gull</span> Many species of gulls have learned to coexist successfully with humans and have thrived in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urban_wildlife">human habitats</a>.(ref: seagull_crisps_aberdeen)<span class="reference" data-type="news " data-url="http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/scotland/north_east/6907994.stm " data-work="BBC News " data-date="20 July 2007">Seagull becomes crisp shoplifter </span> Others rely on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> to get their food. Gulls have been observed preying on live <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/whale">whale</a>s, landing on the whale as it surfaces to peck out pieces of flesh.<ref name="bbc_gull_whale"><span class="reference" data-type="news " data-url="http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/science/nature/8116551.stm " data-work="BBC News " data-date="24 June 2009">Gulls&apos; vicious attacks on whales </span></ref>
<strong><em>Iguana</em></strong> ([&#x26A;&#x2C8;&#x261;w&#x251;&#x2D0;n&#x259;],<ref><a href="http://dictionary.cambridge.org/us/dictionary/american-english/iguana?q=iguana">Cambridge Dictionary</a></ref><ref><a href="http://www.oxforddictionaries.com/definition/english/iguana?q=iguana">Oxford Dictionaries</a></ref> [i&#x2C8;&#x263;wanalang]) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivorous">herbivorous</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lizard">lizard</a>s native to tropical areas of Mexico, Central America, and the Caribbean. The genus was first described in 1768 by Austrian <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/naturalist">naturalist</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Josephus_Nicolaus_Laurenti">Josephus Nicolaus Laurenti</a> in his book <em>Specimen Medicum, Exhibens Synopsin Reptilium Emendatam cum Experimentis circa Venena</em>. Two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> are included in the genus <em>Iguana</em>: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/green_iguana">green iguana</a>, which is widespread throughout its range and a popular pet, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lesser_Antillean_iguana">Lesser Antillean iguana</a>, which is native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lesser_Antilles">Lesser Antilles</a> and endangered due to habitat destruction.
The word &quot;iguana&quot; is derived from the original <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ta&#xED;no_people">Taino</a> name for the species, <em>iwana</em>.(ref: Coles)
In addition to the two species in the genus <em>Iguana</em>, several other related genera in the same family have common names of the species including the word &quot;iguana&quot;.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
