The <strong>haddock</strong> (<em>Melanogrammus aeglefinus</em>) is a salt water fish, found in the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> and associated seas.
The haddock is easily recognized by a black <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lateral_line">lateral line</a> running along its white side (not to be confused with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollock">pollock</a> which has the reverse, i.e., white line on black side) and a distinctive dark blotch above the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish_anatomy#Types_of_fin">pectoral fin</a>, often described as a &quot;thumbprint&quot; or even the &quot;Devils thumbprint&quot; or &quot;St. Peters mark&quot;.<ref><a href="http://www.ocean.udel.edu/mas/seafood/haddock.html">Haddock</a></ref>
Haddock is most commonly found at depths of [40to133mftabbr=on], but has a range as deep as [300mftabbr=on]. It thrives in temperatures of 2 to 10&#xA0;&#xB0;C (36 to 50&#xA0;&#xB0;F). Juveniles prefer shallower waters and larger adults deeper water. Generally, adult haddock do not engage in long migratory behaviour as do the younger fish, but seasonal movements have been known to occur across all ages. Haddock feed primarily on small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/invertebrate">invertebrate</a>s, although larger members of the species may occasionally consume fish.
Haddock range in size between 38 to 69 centimeters in length and 0.9 to 1.8 kilograms in weight.(ref: Fisheries and Oceans Canada)<a href="http://www.dfo-mpo.gc.ca/fm-gp/sustainable-durable/fisheries-peches/haddock-aiglefin-eng.htm">Fisheries and Oceans Canada</a>  Growth rates of haddock have changed significantly over the past 30 to 40 years. Presently, growth is more rapid, with haddock reaching their adult size much earlier than previously noted.  However, the degree to which these younger fish contribute to reproductive success of the population is unknown. Growth rates of  haddock, however, had slowed in recent years. Some evidence indicates it may be the result of an exceptionally large year class in 2003.<ref><a href="http://www.nefsc.noaa.gov/nefsc/publications/crd/crd0216/">NEFSC Ref Doc 02-16</a></ref>[date=October 2015] <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spawn_(biology)">Spawning</a> occurs between January and June, peaking during late March and early April. The most important spawning grounds are in the waters off middle <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norway">Norway</a>, near southwest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iceland">Iceland</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georges_Bank">Georges Bank</a>. An average-sized female produces approximately 850,000 eggs, and larger females are capable of producing up to 3 million eggs each year.
<strong>Hummingbirds</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World#Usage">New World</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that constitute the family <strong>Trochilidae</strong>.  They are among the smallest of birds, most species measuring in the [7.5&#x2013;13cmin0abbr=on] range. Indeed, the smallest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> bird species is a hummingbird, the 5-cm <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bee_hummingbird">bee hummingbird</a>, weighing less than a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Penny_(United_States_coin)">U.S. penny</a> (2.5 g).
<strong>Manta rays</strong> are large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batoidea">ray</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Manta</em></strong>. The larger species, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Giant_oceanic_manta_ray">M. birostris</a></em>, reaches [7mftinabbr=on] in width while the smaller, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reef_manta_ray">M. alfredi</a></em>, reaches [5.5mftinabbr=on]. Both have triangular <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pectoral_fin#AnchPectoral">pectoral fins</a>, horn-shaped <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cephalic">cephalic</a> fins and large, forward-facing mouths. They are classified among the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elasmobranchii">Elasmobranchii</a> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shark">shark</a>s and rays) and are placed in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myliobatidae">Myliobatidae</a>.
Mantas are found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperate_climate">temperate</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subtropics">subtropical</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropics">tropical</a> waters. Both species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pelagic_fish">pelagic</a>; <em>M. birostris</em> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_migration">migrates</a> across open oceans, singly or in groups, while <em>M. alfredi</em> tends to be resident and coastal. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/filter_feeder">filter feeder</a>s and eat large quantities of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zooplankton">zooplankton</a>, which they swallow with their open mouths as they swim. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gestation">Gestation</a> lasts over a year, producing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovoviviparity">live</a> pups. Mantas may visit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cleaning_station">cleaning station</a>s for the removal of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasite">parasite</a>s. Like whales, they <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whale_surfacing_behaviour#Breaching.2C_lunging.2C_and_porpoising">breach</a>, for unknown reasons.
Both species are listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vulnerable_species">vulnerable</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropogenic">Anthropogenic</a> threats include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pollution">pollution</a>, entanglement in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fishing_net">fishing net</a>s, and direct harvesting for their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill_raker">gill raker</a>s for use in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_medicine">Chinese medicine</a>. Their slow reproductive rate exacerbates these threats. They are protected in international waters by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonn_Convention">Convention on Migratory Species of Wild Animals</a>, but are more vulnerable closer to shore. Areas where mantas congregate are popular with tourists. Only a few <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aquarium">aquarium</a>s are large enough to house them. In general, these large fish are seldom seen and difficult to study.
<strong>Lemmings</strong> are small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s, usually found in or near the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic">Arctic</a>, in tundra <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biome">biome</a>s. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subnivean">subniveal animals</a>, and together with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vole">vole</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muskrat">muskrat</a>s, they make up the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arvicolinae">Arvicolinae</a> (also known as Microtinae), which forms part of the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_radiation">radiation</a> by far, the superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muroidea">Muroidea</a>, which also includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rat">rat</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouse">mice</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hamster">hamster</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gerbil">gerbil</a>s.
<strong>Larks</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/passerine">passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s of the family <strong>Alaudidae</strong>. All species occur in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a>, and in northern and eastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>. Only one, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horned_lark">horned lark</a>, is also found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>.  Habitats vary widely, but many species live in dry regions.
http://www.constellationsofwords.com/Constellations/Lynx.htm |accessdate=October 5, 2010}} in reference to the luminescence of its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tapetum_lucidum">reflective eyes</a>.(ref: ConstelOfWords)
Neither the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caracal">caracal</a>, sometimes called the desert lynx, nor the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/jungle_cat">jungle cat</a>, called the jungle lynx, is a member of the <em>Lynx</em> genus.
Koi varieties are distinguished by coloration, patterning, and scalation. Some of the major colors are white, black, red, yellow, blue, and cream. The most popular category of koi is the <em>Gosanke</em>, which is made up of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kohaku_(Nishikigoi)"><em>Kohaku</em></a>, <em>Taisho Sanshoku</em>, and <em>Showa Sanshoku</em> varieties.
<strong>Marsupials</strong> are an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Class_(biology)">infraclass</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s living primarily in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australasia">Australasia</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>. A distinctive characteristic, common to most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, is that the young are carried in a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pouch_(marsupial)">pouch</a>. Well-known marsupials include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo">kangaroo</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallaby">wallabies</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/koala">koala</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/possum">possum</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/opossum">opossum</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wombat">wombat</a>s and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tasmanian_devil">Tasmanian devil</a>. Other marsupials include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/numbat">numbat</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bandicoot">bandicoot</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bettong">bettong</a>s, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macrotis">bilby</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quoll">quoll</a>s, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quokka">quokka</a>.
Marsupials represent the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> originating with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/last_common_ancestor">last common ancestor</a> of extant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/metatheria">metatheria</a>ns. Like other mammals in the Metatheria, they are characterized by giving birth to relatively undeveloped young, often residing in a pouch with the mother for a certain time after birth. Close to 70% of the 334 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> species occur in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia_(continent)">Australian continent</a> (the mainland, Tasmania, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a> and nearby islands) with the remaining 100 found in the Americas, primarily in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>, but with thirteen in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America">Central America</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virginia_opossum">one</a> in North America north of Mexico.
<strong>Magpies</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corvidae">Corvidae</a> (crow) family, including the black and white <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_magpie">Eurasian magpie</a>, which is considered one of the most intelligent animals in the world, and the only non-mammal species able to recognize itself in a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mirror_test">mirror test</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal  " data-vauthors="Prior H, etal " data-="" editor1-last="De Waal " editor1-first="Frans " title="Mirror-Induced Behavior in the Magpie (Pica pica): Evidence of Self-Recognition " journal="PLoS Biology " publisher="Public Library of Science " year="2008 " doi="10.1371/journal.pbio.0060202 " accessdate="2008-08-21 " url="http://biology.plosjournals.org/archive/1545-7885/6/8/pdf/10.1371_journal.pbio.0060202-L.pdf " volume="6 " pages="e202 " pmid="18715117 " issue="8 " pmc="2517622"></span></ref> In addition to other members of the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pica_(genus)">Pica</a></em>, corvids considered as magpies are in the genera <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cissa_(genus)">Cissa</a></em>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyanopica">Cyanopica</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Urocissa">Urocissa</a></em>.
<strong>Pheasants</strong> ([&#x2C8;f&#x25B;z&#x259;nt]) are birds of several genera within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily_(biology)">subfamily</a> <strong>Phasianinae</strong>, of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galliformes">Galliformes</a>.
Pheasants are characterised by strong <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexual_dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, males being highly ornate with bright <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/colour">colour</a>s and adornments such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wattle_(anatomy)">wattles</a> and long <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a>s. Males are usually larger than females and have longer tails. Males play a part in rearing the young. Pheasants typically eat seeds and some insects.
The best-known is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_pheasant">common pheasant</a>, which is widespread throughout the world in introduced feral populations and in farm operations. Various other pheasant species are popular in aviaries, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/golden_pheasant">golden pheasant</a> (<em>Chrysolophus pictus</em>).
A <strong>python</strong> is a constricting snake belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Python_(genus)"><em>Python</em> (genus)</a>, or, more generally, any snake in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pythonidae">Pythonidae</a> (containing the <em>Python</em> genus).
<strong>Pike</strong>, <strong>Pikes</strong> or <strong>The Pike</strong> may refer to:
The <strong>rainbow trout</strong> (<em>Oncorhynchus mykiss</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/trout">trout</a> and species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/salmonid">salmonid</a> native to cold-water <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tributary">tributaries</a> of the Pacific Ocean in Asia and North America.  The <strong>steelhead</strong> (sometimes called &quot;steelhead trout&quot;) is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish_migration#Classification">anadromous</a> (sea-run) form of the <strong>coastal rainbow trout</strong> [(<em>O. m. irideus</em>)] or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbia_River_redband_trout">Columbia River redband trout</a> [(<em>O. m. gairdneri</em>)] that usually returns to fresh water to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spawn_(biology)">spawn</a> after living two to three years in the ocean. Freshwater forms that have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introduced_species">introduced</a> into the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Lakes">Great Lakes</a> and migrate into tributaries to spawn are also called steelhead.
Adult freshwater stream rainbow trout average between [1and5lb1abbr=on], while lake-dwelling and anadromous forms may reach [20lbskg0abbr=on]. Coloration varies widely based on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a>, forms and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat">habitat</a>. Adult fish are distinguished by a broad reddish stripe along the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lateral_line">lateral line</a>, from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill">gill</a>s to the tail, which is most vivid in breeding males.
Wild-caught and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish_hatchery">hatchery</a>-reared forms of this species have been transplanted and introduced for food or sport in at least 45 countries and every continent except Antarctica. Introductions to locations outside their native range in the United States (U.S.), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southern_Europe">Southern Europe</a>, Australia, New Zealand and South America have damaged native fish species. Introduced populations may affect native species by preying on them, out-competing them, transmitting contagious diseases (such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/whirling_disease">whirling disease</a>), or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybridizing</a> with closely related species and subspecies, thus reducing genetic purity. Other introductions into waters previously devoid of any fish species or with severely depleted stocks of native fish have created world-class <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recreational_fishing">sport fisheries</a> such as the Great Lakes and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wyoming">Wyoming</a>&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Firehole_River">Firehole River</a>.
Some local populations of specific subspecies, or in the case of steelhead, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/distinct_population_segment">distinct population segment</a>s, are listed as either <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/threatened_species">threatened</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">endangered</a> under the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_Species_Act">Endangered Species Act</a>. The steelhead is the official <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/state_fish">state fish</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Washington_(state)">Washington</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.leg.wa.gov/symbols/pages/default.aspx " data-publisher="Washington State Legislature " data-accessdate="2013-11-29">Symbols of Washington State </span></ref>
<strong><em>Quelea</em></strong> [kwili&#x259;] is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/passerine">passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that belongs to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/weaver-bird">weaver</a> family Ploceidae, confined to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>. These are small-sized, sparrow- or finch-like gregarious birds, with bills adapted to eating seeds. Queleas may be nomadic over vast ranges; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red-billed_quelea">red-billed quelea</a> is said to be the most numerous bird species in the world.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last=" Sekercioglu " data-first="Cagan Hakki " data-year="2006 " data-chapter="Foreword " data-editor="Josep del Hoyo, Andrew Elliott &amp; David Christie (eds.) " data-location="Barcelona " data-publisher="Lynx Edicions " data-isbn="84-96553-06-X" data-="" page="48"><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Handbook_of_the_Birds_of_the_World">Handbook of the Birds of the World</a>, Volume 11: Old World Flycatchers to Old World Warblers </span></ref>
The <strong>quokka</strong> ([&#x2C8;kw&#x252;k&#x259;], <em>Setonix brachyurus</em>), the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monotypic">only member</a> of the genus <strong><em>Setonix</em></strong>, is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macropodidae">macropod</a> about the size of a domestic cat. Like other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsupial">marsupial</a>s in the macropod family (such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo">kangaroo</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallaby">wallabies</a>), the quokka is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivorous">herbivorous</a> and mainly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nocturnal">nocturnal</a>. Quokkas can be found on some smaller <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/island">island</a>s off the coast of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Australia">Western Australia</a>, in particular on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rottnest_Island">Rottnest Island</a> just off <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perth,_Western_Australia">Perth</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bald_Island">Bald Island</a> near <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albany,_Western_Australia">Albany</a>. A small mainland colony exists in the protected area of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Two_Peoples_Bay_Nature_Reserve">Two Peoples Bay Nature Reserve</a>, where they co-exist with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gilbert&apos;s_potoroo">Gilbert&apos;s potoroo</a>.
<strong>Sawfishes</strong>,  also known as <strong>carpenter sharks</strong>, are an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a>  (<strong>Pristiformes</strong>) of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/batoidea">rays</a> characterized by a long, narrow, flattened <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rostrum_(anatomy)">rostrum</a>, or nose extension, lined with sharp <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/transverse_plane">transverse</a> teeth, arranged so as to resemble a saw. Several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of sawfishes can grow to about [7mabbr=on]. The family as a whole is largely unknown and little studied. The Pristidae are the only living family within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <strong>Pristiformes</strong>, whose name comes from the [&#x3C0;&#x3C1;&#x3AF;&#x3C3;&#x3C4;&#x3B7;&#x3C2;pr&#xED;st&#x113;ssaw, sawyer].
Sawfishes should not be confused with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sawshark">sawshark</a>s (order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pristiophoriformes">Pristiophoriformes</a>), which have a similar appearance.
All species of sawfishes are listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered">Endangered</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered">Critically Endangered</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">IUCN</a>, and face the threat of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extinction">extinction</a> as a result of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_loss">habitat loss</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/overfishing">overfishing</a>.(ref: IUCN-Saved-Search)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.iucnredlist.org/search/link/5137f030-4d738b54 " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" international_union_for_conservation_of_nature"="">International Union for Conservation of Nature &quot; data-work=&quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN_Red_List">IUCN Red List</a> &quot; data-accessdate=&quot; 6 March 2013&quot;&gt;Pristidae </span>  Global <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/statistical_population">populations</a> of every species of sawfishes are estimated to have fallen to less than 10% of their historic levels, and they have been lost from at least 80% of their historic range.(ref: Dulvy2014AC)
 |last6=Carlson |first6=J.K. |last7=Fordham |first7=S.V. |year=2014 |title=Ghosts of the coast: global extinction risk and conservation of sawfishes |journal=Aquatic Conservation |url=http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/aqc.2525/full  |doi= 10.1002/aqc.2525 |accessdate=27 October 2014 }}  The smalltooth sawfish (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pristis_pectinata">Pristis pectinata</a></em>), for example, was once widely distributed, but available data indicate the range of this species has been reduced by about 90%, and population numbers have declined dramatically, perhaps by 95% or more.(ref: NOAA-pectinata)<em>Pristis microdon</em> to appropriate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/public_aquaria">public aquaria</a> for primarily conservation purposes.(ref: CITES-Appendix)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.cites.org/eng/app/index.php " data-publisher="Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" cites"="">CITES) &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;7 March 2013&quot;&gt;CITES Appendices I, II and III </span>
<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White-winged_Vampire_Bat">Diaemus youngi</a></em>}}
<strong>Vampire bats</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bat">bat</a>s whose food source is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/blood">blood</a>, a dietary trait called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hematophagy">hematophagy</a>. Three bat <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> feed solely on blood: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_vampire_bat">common vampire bat</a> (<em>Desmodus rotundus</em>), the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hairy-legged_vampire_bat">hairy-legged vampire bat</a> (<em>Diphylla ecaudata</em>), and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/white-winged_vampire_bat">white-winged vampire bat</a> (<em>Diaemus youngi</em>). All three species are native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>, ranging from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina">Argentina</a>.
A <strong>tree frog</strong> is any species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/frog">frog</a> that spends a major portion of its lifespan in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tree">tree</a>s, known as an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal">arboreal</a> state.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-author="Amphibians " data-url="http://animals.howstuffworks.com/amphibians/tree-frog-info.htm " data-publisher="Animals.howstuffworks.com " data-date="2008-04-22 " data-accessdate="2013-06-03">Tree Frog Info </span></ref> Several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)">lineages</a> of frogs among the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neobatrachia">Neobatrachia</a> have given rise to tree frogs, although they are not closely related to each other.
Many millions of years of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/convergent_evolution">convergent evolution</a> have resulted in almost identical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology)">morphology</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecology">ecologies</a>. They are so similar as regards their ecological niche that in one biome where one group of tree frogs occurs, the other is almost always absent. The last common ancestor of some such tree frog groups lived long before the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous&#x2013;Paleogene_extinction_event">extinction of the dinosaurs</a>.[date=March 2012]
<strong>Starfish</strong> or <strong>sea stars</strong> are star-shaped <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/echinoderm">echinoderm</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/class_(biology)">class</a> <strong>Asteroidea</strong>. Common usage frequently finds these names being also applied to ophiuroids, which are correctly referred to as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/brittle_star">brittle star</a>s or &quot;basket stars&quot;. About 1,500 species of starfish occur on the seabed in all the world&apos;s oceans, from the tropics to frigid polar waters. They are found from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/intertidal_zone">intertidal zone</a> down to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/abyssal_zone">abyssal</a> depths, [6000mabbr=on] below the surface.
Starfish are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marine_invertebrates">marine invertebrates</a>. They typically have a central disc and five arms, though some species have a larger number of arms. The aboral or upper surface may be smooth, granular or spiny, and is covered with overlapping plates. Many species are brightly coloured in various shades of red or orange, while others are blue, grey or brown. Starfish have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tube_feet">tube feet</a> operated by a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vascular_system">hydraulic system</a> and a mouth at the centre of the oral or lower surface. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/opportunistic">opportunistic</a> feeders and are mostly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predation">predators</a> on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/benthic">benthic</a> invertebrates. Several species have specialized feeding behaviours including eversion of their stomachs and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/suspension_feeding">suspension feeding</a>. They have complex <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_life_cycle">life cycles</a> and can reproduce both sexually and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asexual_reproduction_in_starfish">asexually</a>. Most can <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)">regenerate</a> damaged parts or lost arms and they can shed arms as a means of defence. The Asteroidea occupy several significant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecology">ecological</a> roles. Starfish, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ochre_sea_star">ochre sea star</a> (<em>Pisaster ochraceus</em>) and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reef_starfish">reef sea star</a> (<em>Stichaster australis</em>), have become widely known as examples of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/keystone_species">keystone species</a> concept in ecology. The tropical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crown-of-thorns_starfish">crown-of-thorns starfish</a> (<em>Acanthaster planci</em>) is a voracious predator of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coral">coral</a> throughout the Indo-Pacific region, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asterias_amurensis">northern Pacific sea star</a> is considered to be one of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/list_of_the_worlds_100_worst_invasive_species">worlds 100 worst invasive species</a>.
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fossil">fossil</a> record for starfish is ancient, dating back to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordovician">Ordovician</a> around 450 million years ago, but it is rather poor, as starfish tend to disintegrate after death. Only the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ossicle_(echinoderm)">ossicles</a> and spines of the animal are likely to be preserved, making remains hard to locate. With their appealing symmetrical shape, starfish have played a part in literature, legend, design and popular culture. They are sometimes collected as curios, used in design or as logos, and in some cultures, despite possible toxicity, they are eaten.
<strong>Puma</strong> may refer to:
<strong>Trapdoor spiders</strong>, members of the family <strong>Ctenizidae</strong>, are medium-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mygalomorph">mygalomorph</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spiders">spiders</a> that construct burrows with a cork-like trapdoor made of soil, vegetation and silk. Some similar species are also called trapdoor spiders, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liphistiidae">Liphistiidae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barychelidae">Barychelidae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrtaucheniidae">Cyrtaucheniidae</a> and some <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Idiopidae">Idiopidae</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nemesiidae">Nemesiidae</a>. Some <em>Conothele</em> species do not build a burrow, but construct a silken tube with trapdoor in bark crevices.
A <strong>shrew</strong> or <strong>shrew mouse</strong> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Soricidae</strong>) is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mole_(animal)">mole</a>-like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a> classified in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soricomorpha">Soricomorpha</a>. True shrews are also not to be confused with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/West_Indies_shrews">West Indies shrews</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/treeshrew">treeshrew</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/otter_shrew">otter shrew</a>s, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elephant_shrew">elephant shrew</a>s, which belong to different families or orders.
Although its external appearance is generally that of a long-nosed mouse, a shrew is not a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>, as mice are. It is in fact a much closer relative of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mole_(animal)">mole</a>s, and related to rodents only in that both belong to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boreoeutheria">Boreoeutheria</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnorder">Magnorder</a>. Shrews have sharp, spike-like teeth, not the familiar gnawing front incisor teeth of rodents.
Shrews are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cosmopolitan_distribution">distributed almost worldwide</a>: of the major tropical and temperate land masses, only <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a> do not have any native shrews; in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>, shrews are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_American_Interchange">relatively recent immigrants</a> and are present only in the northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andes">Andes</a>. In terms of species diversity, the shrew family is the fourth most successful mammal family, being rivalled only by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muroidea">muroid rodent</a> families <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muridae">Muridae</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cricetidae">Cricetidae</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bat">bat</a> family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vespertilionidae">Vespertilionidae</a>.
<strong>Seahorse</strong> is the name given to 54 species of small marine <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fishes</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Hippocampus</em></strong>. &quot;Hippocampus&quot; comes from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <em>hippos</em> meaning &quot;horse&quot; and <em>kampos</em> meaning &quot;sea monster&quot;.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last1=" Duvernoy " data-first1=" HM " data-edition=" 3rd " data-year=" 2005 " data-publisher=" Springer-Verlag " data-location=" Berlin " data-isbn=" 3-540-23191-9 " data-page=" 1 " data-chapter=" Introduction " data-chapterurl=" http://books.google.com.au/books?id"> The Human Hippocampus </span></ref> The word &quot;seahorse&quot; is often written as two separate words (sea horse), or hyphenated (sea-horse).(ref: SOED)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-title=" Shorter Oxford English Dictionary" data-date="2007" data-location=" Oxford, UK" data-isbn=" 0199206872" data-publisher=" Oxford University Press"></span> Having a head and neck suggestive of a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horse">horse</a>, seahorses also feature segmented bony armour, an upright posture and a curled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prehensile_tail">prehensile tail</a>.
The <strong>roadrunner</strong> (genus <strong><em>Geococcyx</em></strong>), also known as a <strong>chaparral bird</strong> or <strong>chaparral cock</strong>, is a fast-running ground <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cuckoo">cuckoo</a> that has a long tail and a crest. It is found in the southwestern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States">United States</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a>,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="encyclopedia" data-publisher="Farlex" data-encyclopedia="The Free Dictionary" data-url="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/roadrunner" data-accessdate="3 May 2012">roadrunner</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="encyclopedia" data-url="http://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/roadrunner" data-encyclopedia="Merriam Webster" data-accessdate="3 May 2012">roadrunner</span></ref> usually in the desert. Some have been clocked at [20mph].
<strong>Scorpions</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predation">predatory</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arthropod">arthropod</a> animals of the order <strong>Scorpiones</strong> within the class <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arachnid">Arachnid</a>a. They have eight legs<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url=" http://www.scorpionworlds.com/" data-publisher="ScorpionWorlds" data-accessdate="19 February 2015"> SCORPION FACTS AND INFORMATION</span></ref> and are easily recognised by the pair of grasping <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pedipalp">pedipalp</a>s and the narrow, segmented <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a>, often carried in a characteristic forward curve over the back, ending with a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venom">venom</a>ous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stinger">stinger</a>. Scorpions range in size from 9&#xA0;mm (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Typhlochactas_mitchelli">Typhlochactas mitchelli</a></em>) to 20&#xA0;cm (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadogenes_troglodytes">Hadogenes troglodytes</a></em>).<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Edward E. Ruppert, Richard S. Fox, Robert D. Barnes " data-year="2004 " data-publisher="Brooks/Cole " data-isbn="978-81-315-0104-7">Invertebrate Zoology </span></ref>
The evolutionary history of scorpions goes back to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Silurian">Silurian</a> era 430 million years ago. They have adapted to a wide range of environmental conditions and can now be found on all continents except <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>. Scorpions number about 1750 described <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://kovarex.com/scorpio/pdf/scorpions-Kovarik-2009.pdf " data-format="PDF " data-author="Franti&#x161;ek Kova&#x159;&#xED;k " data-year="2009 " data-accessdate="January 22, 2011">Illustrated catalog of scorpions, Part I </span></ref> with 13 extant families recognised to date. Only about 25 of these species are known to have venom capable of killing a human being.(ref: Biology)<span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Gary A. Polis " data-year="1990 " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" stanford_university_press"="">Stanford University Press &quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-8047-1249-1 &quot; data-url=&quot;http://books.google.com/?id&quot;&gt;The Biology of Scorpions </span>[1]The taxonomy has undergone changes and is likely to change further, as genetic studies are bringing forth new information.
Scorpion stings are painful but are usually harmless. For stings from species found in the United States, no treatment is normally needed for healthy adults although medical care should be sought for children and for the elderly. Stings from species found elsewhere may require medical attention.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.mayoclinic.org/diseases-conditions/scorpion-stings/basics/definition/con-20033894" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" mayo_clinic"="">Mayo Clinic&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;3 July 2015&quot;&gt;Diseases and Conditions - Scorpion stings</span></ref>
The <strong>bonobo</strong> ([b&#x259;&#x2C8;no&#x28A;bo&#x28A;] or [&#x2C8;b&#x252;n&#x259;bo&#x28A;]) (<em>Pan paniscus</em>), formerly called the <strong>pygmy chimpanzee</strong> and less often, the <strong>dwarf</strong> or <strong>gracile chimpanzee</strong>,(ref: deWaal1997)<span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" first1=" Frans " last1=" de Waal " first2=" Frans " last2=" Lanting " year=" 1997 " title=" Bonobo: The Forgotten Ape " publisher=" University of California Press" data-isbn="0-520-20535-9"></span> is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_Species">endangered</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/great_ape">great ape</a> and one of the two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> making up the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee">Pan</a></em>; the other is <em>Pan troglodytes</em>, or the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_chimpanzee">common chimpanzee</a>. Although the name &quot;chimpanzee&quot; is sometimes used to refer to both species together, it is usually understood as referring to the common chimpanzee, whereas <em>Pan paniscus</em> is usually referred to as the bonobo.
The bonobo is distinguished by relatively long legs, pink lips, dark face and tail-tuft through adulthood, and parted long hair on its head. The bonobo is found in a [500000km2sqmiabbr=on] area of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_Basin">Congo Basin</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, Central Africa. The species is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omnivore">omnivorous</a> and inhabits <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old-growth_forest">primary</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/secondary_forest">secondary forest</a>s, including seasonally inundated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/swamp_forest">swamp forest</a>s. Political instability in the region and the timidity of bonobos has meant there has been relatively little field work done observing the species in its natural habitat.
Along with the common chimpanzee, the bonobo is the closest extant relative to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>s. Because the two species are not proficient swimmers, the formation of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_River">Congo River</a> 1.5&#x2013;2 million years ago possibly led to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/speciation">speciation</a> of the bonobo. Bonobos live south of the river, and thereby were separated from the ancestors of the common chimpanzee, which live north of the river. There is no concrete data on population numbers, but the estimate is between 29,500 and 50,000 individuals. The species is listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_species">Endangered</a> on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN_Red_List">IUCN Red List</a> and is threatened by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_destruction">habitat destruction</a> and human population growth and movement, though commercial poaching is the most prominent threat. They typically live 40 years in captivity,<ref>Rowe, N. (1996) <em>Pictural Guide to the Living Primates</em>, Pogonias Press, East Hampton, ISBN 0-9648825-1-5.</ref> though their lifespan in the wild is unknown.
<strong>Squirrels</strong> are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Sciuridae</strong>, consisting of small or medium-size <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s. The family includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tree_squirrel">tree squirrel</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ground_squirrel">ground squirrel</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chipmunk">chipmunk</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marmot">marmot</a>s (including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/groundhog">woodchuck</a>s), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flying_squirrel">flying squirrel</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prairie_dog">prairie dog</a>s. Squirrels are indigenous to the Americas, Eurasia, and Africa, and have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/introduced_species">introduced</a> to Australia.<ref name="Seebeck"><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-last="Seebeck" data-first="J. H." data-url="http://www.scarysquirrel.org/vacation/australia/fauna.pdf" data-work="Fauna of Australia" data-accessdate="2013-11-24">Sciuridae</span></ref>  The earliest known squirrels date from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eocene">Eocene</a> and are most closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mountain_beaver">mountain beaver</a> and to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dormouse">dormouse</a> among living rodent families.
<strong>Toucans</strong> are members of the family <strong>Ramphastidae</strong> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/near_passerine">near passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neotropic_ecozone">Neotropics</a>.  The Ramphastidae family is most closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitonidae">American barbets</a>. They are brightly marked and have large, often colorful <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beak">bills</a>. The family includes five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> and about forty different <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>. The name of this bird group is derived from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tupi_language">Tupi</a> word  <em>tukana</em>, via <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portuguese_language">Portuguese</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-="" url="http://tupi.fflch.usp.br/node/5" title="Vocabul&#xE1;rio Tupi-Portugu&#xEA;s do Curso Elementar de Tupi Antigo" publisher="Universidade de S&#xE3;o Paulo" language="Portuguese" accessdate="1 June 2015"></span></ref> The family includes toucans, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aracari">aracari</a>s and toucanets; more distant relatives include various families of barbets and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/woodpecker">woodpecker</a>s in the suborder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piciformes">Pici</a>.
The <strong>snow leopard</strong> (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panthera">Panthera</a> uncia</em> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/syn.">syn.</a> <em>Uncia uncia</em>) is a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felidae">cat</a> native to the mountain ranges of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia">Central</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Asia">South Asia</a>. It is listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endangered_species">endangered</a> on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IUCN_Red_List_of_Threatened_Species">IUCN Red List of Threatened Species</a> because as of 2003, the size of the global population was estimated at 4,080&#x2013;6,590 adults, of which fewer than 2,500 individuals may reproduce in the wild.(ref: iucn)
Snow leopards inhabit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpine_climate">alpine</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subalpine_zone">subalpine</a> zones at elevations from [3000to4500mftabbr=on]. In the northern range countries, they also occur at lower elevations.<ref name="McCarthy2003"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-year="2003 " data-last1="McCarthy " data-first1=" T. M. " data-last2="Chapron " data-first2="G. (eds.) " data-publisher=" International Snow Leopard Trust and Snow Leopard Network " data-location="Seattle, USA">Snow Leopard Survival Strategy </span></ref>
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">Taxonomically</a>, the snow leopard was classified as <em>Uncia uncia</em> since the early 1930s. Based on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genotyping">genotyping</a> studies, the cat has been considered a member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus_(biology)">genus</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panthera">Panthera</a></em> since 2008.<ref name="Janecka2008"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Janecka " first1=" J. E. " last2=" Jackson " first2=" R. " last3=" Zhang " first3=" Y. " last4=" Diqiang Li " first4=" Munkhtsog " last5=" Buckley-Beason " first5=" V. " last6=" Murphy " first6=" W. J. " year=" 2008 " title=" Population Monitoring of Snow Leopards Using Noninvasive Genetics " journal=" Cat News " volume=" 48 " pages=" 7&#x2013;10 "></span></ref> Two subspecies have been attributed, but genetic differences between the two have not been settled.
The snow leopard is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_national_animals">National Heritage Animal</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-publisher="Government of Pakistan " data-url="http://www.pakistan.gov.pk/gop/index.php?q" data-accessdate="2013-11-27">National Symbols and Things of Pakistan </span></ref>
<strong>Snakes</strong> are elongated, legless, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivore">carnivorous</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptile">reptile</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/suborder">suborder</a> <strong>Serpentes</strong><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" doi=" 10.1371/journal.pone.0118199" title=" Integrated Analyses Resolve Conflicts over Squamate Reptile Phylogeny and Reveal Unexpected Placements for Fossil Taxa" journal=" PLoS ONE" volume=" 10" issue=" 3" pages=" e0118199" year=" 2015" last1=" Reeder " first1=" T. W. " last2=" Townsend " first2=" T. M. " last3=" Mulcahy " first3=" D. G. " last4=" Noonan " first4=" B. P. " last5=" Wood " first5=" P. L. " last6=" Sites " first6=" J. W. " last7=" Wiens " first7=" J. J. "></span></ref> that can be distinguished from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/legless_lizard">legless lizard</a>s by their lack of eyelids and external ears. Like all <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squamata">squamates</a>, snakes are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ectothermic">ectothermic</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/amniote">amniote</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertebrate">vertebrate</a>s covered in overlapping <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scale_(zoology)">scales</a>. Many species of snakes have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skulls">skulls</a> with several more joints than their lizard ancestors, enabling them to swallow prey much larger than their heads with their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cranial_kinesis">highly mobile jaws</a>. To accommodate their narrow bodies, snakes&apos; paired organs (such as kidneys) appear one in front of the other instead of side by side, and most have only one functional <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lung">lung</a>. Some species retain a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pelvic_girdle">pelvic girdle</a> with a pair of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vestigial">vestigial</a> claws on either side of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cloaca">cloaca</a>.
Living snakes are found on every continent except Antarctica, and on most smaller land masses &#x2014; exceptions include some large islands, such as Ireland and New Zealand, and many small islands of the Atlantic and central Pacific.<ref name="Bauchot"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-editor="Roland Bauchot" data-year="1994" data-publisher="Sterling Publishing Co., Inc." data-location="New York" data-isbn="1-4027-3181-7" data-page="220">Snakes: A Natural History</span></ref> Additionally, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrophiinae">sea snakes</a> are widespread throughout the Indian and Pacific Oceans. More than 20 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">families</a> are currently recognized, comprising about 500 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus">genera</a> and about 3,400 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.(ref: ITIS)(ref: NRDB-Cs)<a href="http://reptile-database.reptarium.cz/search.php?taxon=snake&amp;submit=Search">snake species list</a> at the <a href="http://reptile-database.org/">Reptile Database</a>. Accessed 22 May 2012. They range in size from the tiny, 10&#xA0;cm-long <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leptotyphlops_carlae">thread snake</a> to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Python_reticulatus">reticulated python</a> of up to [6.95mftsp=us] in length.(ref: SunBear)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="Fredriksson, G. M. " data-journal=" Raffles Bulletin of Zoology " data-volume="53" data-issue="1" data-year=" 2005" data-pages="165&#x2013;168" data-url="http://dare.uva.nl/document/161117">Predation on Sun Bears by Reticulated Python in East Kalimantan, Indonesian Borneo</span> The fossil species <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titanoboa_cerrejonensis">Titanoboa cerrejonensis</a></em> was [13mftsp=us] long.(ref: head)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jurassic">Jurassic</a> period, with the earliest known fossils dating to between 143 and 167 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mega-annum">Ma</a> ago.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" last=" Perkins " first=" Sid " date=" 27 January 2015 " title=" Fossils of oldest known snakes unearthed " url=" http://news.sciencemag.org/paleontology/2015/01/fossils-oldest-known-snakes-unearthed " website=" news.sciencemag.org " accessdate=" 29 January 2015 "></span><p><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Caldwell " first1=" M. W. " last2=" Nydam " first2=" R. L. " data-last3=" Palci " first3=" A. " data-last4=" Apestegu&#xED;a " first4=" S. " year=" 2015 " title=" The oldest known snakes from the Middle Jurassic-Lower Cretaceous provide insights on snake evolution " journal=" Nature Communications " volume=" 6 " number=" 5996 " doi=" 10.1038/ncomms6996 " pages="5996"></span></p></ref> The diversity of modern snakes appeared during the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleocene">Paleocene</a> period (<em>c</em> 66 to 56 Ma ago). The oldest preserved descriptions of snakes can be found in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brooklyn_Papyrus">Brooklyn Papyrus</a>.
<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Caldwell " first1=" M. W. " last2=" Nydam " first2=" R. L. " data-last3=" Palci " first3=" A. " data-last4=" Apestegu&#xED;a " first4=" S. " year=" 2015 " title=" The oldest known snakes from the Middle Jurassic-Lower Cretaceous provide insights on snake evolution " journal=" Nature Communications " volume=" 6 " number=" 5996 " doi=" 10.1038/ncomms6996 " pages="5996"></span>
Most species are nonvenomous and those that have venom use it primarily to kill and subdue prey rather than for self-defense. Some possess venom potent enough to cause painful injury or death to humans. Nonvenomous snakes either swallow prey alive or kill by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/constriction">constriction</a>.
<strong>Squid</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cephalopod">cephalopod</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <strong>Teuthida</strong>, which comprises around 304 species. Like all other cephalopods, squid have a distinct head, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_(biology)#Bilateral_symmetry">bilateral symmetry</a>, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mantle_(mollusc)">mantle</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cephalopod_arm">arms</a>. Squid, like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, have eight <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cephalopod_arm">arms</a> arranged in pairs and two, usually longer, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tentacle">tentacle</a>s. Squid are strong swimmers and certain species can &quot;fly&quot; for short distances out of the water.<ref>Jabr, F. 2010. <a href="http://www.scientificamerican.com/article.cfm?id=can-squid-fly&amp;sc=WR_20100804">Fact or Fiction: Can a Squid Fly Out of the Water?</a> <em>Scientific American</em>, August 2, 2010.</ref>
<strong>Sheep</strong> (<em>Ovis aries</em>) are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quadruped">quadruped</a>al, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ruminant">ruminant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s typically kept as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/livestock">livestock</a>. Like all ruminants, sheep are members of the order Artiodactyla, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a>s. Although the name &quot;sheep&quot; applies to many species in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovis">Ovis</a></em>, in everyday usage it almost always refers to <em>Ovis aries</em>. Numbering a little over one billion, domestic sheep are also the most numerous species of sheep. An adult female sheep is referred to as a <em>ewe</em> ([ju&#x2D0;]), an intact male as a <em>ram</em> or occasionally a <em>tup</em>, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castration">castrated</a> male as a <em>wether</em>, and a younger sheep as a  <em>lamb</em>.
Sheep are most likely descended from the wild <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouflon">mouflon</a> of Europe and Asia. One of the earliest animals to be domesticated for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/agriculture">agricultural</a> purposes, sheep are raised for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wool">fleece</a>, meat (lamb, hogget or mutton) and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep_milk">milk</a>. A sheep&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wool">wool</a> is the most widely used animal fiber, and is usually harvested by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheep_shearing">shearing</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikt:ovine">Ovine</a> meat is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton">lamb</a> when from younger animals and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton">mutton</a> when from older ones.  Sheep continue to be important for wool and meat today, and are also occasionally raised for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheepskin_(material)">pelts</a>, as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy">dairy</a> animals, or as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/model_organism">model organism</a>s for science.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheep_husbandry">Sheep husbandry</a> is practised throughout the majority of the inhabited world, and has been fundamental to many civilizations. In the modern era, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, the southern and central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>n nations, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles">British Isles</a> are most closely associated with sheep production.
Sheepraising has a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry">lexicon of unique terms</a> which vary considerably by region and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dialect">dialect</a>. Use of the word <em>sheep</em> began in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_English">Middle English</a> as a derivation of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English">Old English</a> word <em>sc&#x113;ap</em>; it is both the singular and plural name for the animal. A group of sheep is called a flock, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herd">herd</a> or mob. Many other specific terms for the various life stages of sheep exist, generally related to lambing, shearing, and age.
Being a key animal in the history of farming, sheep have a deeply entrenched place in human culture, and find representation in much modern language and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/symbology">symbology</a>. As livestock, sheep are most often associated with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pastoral">pastoral</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arcadia_(utopia)">Arcadian</a> imagery. Sheep figure in many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mythology">mythologies</a>&#x2014;such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Fleece">Golden Fleece</a>&#x2014;and major religions, especially the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrahamic">Abrahamic</a> traditions. In both ancient and modern religious ritual, sheep are used as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_sacrifice">sacrificial animals</a>.
<strong>Swordfish</strong> (<em>Xiphias gladius</em>; from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_Language">Greek</a> &#x3BE;&#x3AF;&#x3C6;&#x3BF;&#x3C2;: sword, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin">Latin</a> <em>gladius</em>: sword), also known as <strong>broadbills</strong> in some countries, are large, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Highly_migratory_species">highly migratory</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predatory_fish">predatory fish</a> characterized by a long, flat bill. They are a popular sport fish of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/billfish">billfish</a> category, though elusive. Swordfish are elongated, round-bodied, and lose all teeth and scales by adulthood. These fish are found widely in tropical and temperate parts of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>s, and can typically be found from near the surface to a depth of [550mabbr=on]. They commonly reach [3mabbr=on] in length, and the maximum reported is [4.55mabbr=on] in length and [650kgabbr=on] in weight.<ref name="Florida">Gardieff, S. <em><a href="http://www.flmnh.ufl.edu/fish/Gallery/Descript/Swordfish/Swordfish.html">Swordfish.</a></em> Florida Museum of Natural History. Accessed 26 December 2011</ref>
They are the sole member of their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a>, <strong>Xiphiidae</strong>.
<strong>Sailfish</strong> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Istiophorus</em> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/billfish">billfish</a> living in warmer sections of all the oceans of the world. They are predominantly blue to gray in colour and have a characteristic erectile <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dorsal_fin">dorsal fin</a> known as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sail_(anatomy)">sail</a>, which often stretches the entire length of the back. Another notable characteristic is the elongated bill, resembling that of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/swordfish">swordfish</a> and other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marlin">marlin</a>s. They are therefore described as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/billfish">billfish</a> in sport-fishing circles.
<strong>Swordtail</strong> may refer to:
