In <strong>other uses</strong>:
A <strong>camel</strong> is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a> within the genus <strong><em>Camelus</em></strong>, bearing distinctive fatty deposits known as &quot;humps&quot; on its back. The two surviving species of camel are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dromedary">dromedary</a>, or one-humped camel (C. dromedarius), which inhabits the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East">Middle East</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horn_of_Africa">Horn of Africa</a>; and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bactrian_camel">bactrian</a>, or two-humped camel (C. bactrianus), which inhabits <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_Asia">Central Asia</a>. Both species have been domesticated; they provide <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camel_milk">milk</a>, meat, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camel_hair">hair</a> for textiles or goods such as felted pouches, and are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/working_animal">working animal</a>s with tasks ranging from human transport to bearing loads.
The term &quot;camel&quot; is derived via <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin">Latin</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek">Greek</a> (<em><span lang="la" dir="ltr">camelus</span></em> and <span lang="el" dir="ltr">&#x3BA;&#x3AC;&#x3BC;&#x3B7;&#x3BB;&#x3BF;&#x3C2; <em>kam&#x113;los</em></span> respectively) from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language">Hebrew</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phoenician_language">Phoenician</a> <em><span lang="he">g&#x101;m&#x101;l</span></em>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="encyclopedia" data-encyclopedia="The New Oxford American Dictionary" data-edition="2nd" data-publisher="Oxford University Press, Inc" data-year="2005">camel</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" last=" Herper " first=" Douglas " title=" camel " work=" Online Etymology Dictionary " accessdate=" 28 November 2012" url=" http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?term"></span></ref> The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hebrew_language">Hebrew</a> meaning of the word <em>g&#x101;m&#x101;l</em> is derived from the verb root g.m.l, meaning (1) stopping, weaning, going without;[date=June 2014] or (2) repaying in kind. This refers to its ability to go without food or water, as well as the increased ability of service the animal provides when being properly cared for.[date=June 2014]
&quot;Camel&quot; is also used more broadly to describe any of the six camel-like mammals in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Camelid">Camelid</a>ae: the two true camels and the four New World camelids: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/llama">llama</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/alpaca">alpaca</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/guanaco">guanaco</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vicu&#xF1;a">vicu&#xF1;a</a> of South America.<ref name="wordnik"><span class="reference" data-type="encyclopedia " data-="" publisher=" wordnik " title=" llama " encyclopedia=" Century Dictionary and Cyclopedia " accessdate=" 28 November 2012 " url=" http://www.wordnik.com/words/llama "></span></ref><ref name="bornstein10"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" doi=" 10.1186/1751-0147-52-S1-S17 " issn=" 1751-0147 " volume=" 52 " issue=" Suppl 1 " page=" S17 " last=" Bornstein " first=" Set " title=" Important ectoparasites of Alpaca (Vicugna pacos) " journal=" Acta Veterinaria Scandinavica " accessdate=" 28 November 2012 " year=" 2010 " url=" http://www.actavetscand.com/content/52/S1/S17 "></span></ref>
The <strong>Xerinae</strong> comprise a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/squirrel">squirrel</a>s, many of which are highly terrestrial. It includes the tribes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marmotini">Marmotini</a> (marmots, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chipmunk">chipmunk</a>s, prairie dogs, and other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Holarctic">Holarctic</a> ground squirrels), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Xerini">Xerini</a> (African and some Eurasian ground squirrels), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protoxerini">Protoxerini</a> (African tree squirrels).<ref>Thorington, R. W. and R. S. Hoffmann. 2005. Family Sciuridae. pp 754&#x2013;818 in Wilson, E. D. and Reeder, D. M. (eds.), <em>Mammal Species of the World A Taxonomic and Geographic Reference</em>, Vol. 2.  Johns Hopkins University Press, Baltimore.</ref>
<strong>Rattlesnakes</strong> are a group of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venomous_snake">venomous snake</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crotalus">Crotalus</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sistrurus">Sistrurus</a></em>(ref: price-38-39)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Price, Andrew H." data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" university_of_texas_press"="">University of Texas Press&quot; data-year=&quot;2009&quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-292-71967-5&quot; data-pages=&quot;38&#x2013;39&quot; data-url=&quot;http://books.google.com/books?id&quot;&gt;Venomous Snakes of Texas: A Field Guide</span> of the subfamily <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crotalinae">Crotalinae</a></em> (&quot;pit vipers&quot;). The 36 known <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_rattlesnake_species_and_subspecies">species of rattlesnakes</a> have between 65 and 70 subspecies,(ref: Barceloux-1026)<a href="http://books.google.com/books?id=CpqzhHc072AC&amp;pg=PA1026">p. 1026</a> all native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>, ranging from southern Alberta and southern British Columbia in Canada to Central Argentina.
Rattlesnakes are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s that live in a wide array of habitats, hunting small animals such as birds and rodents. 
The threat of envenomation, advertised by the loud shaking of the titular noisemaker (&quot;rattle&quot;) at the end of their tails, deters many predators. However, rattlesnakes fall prey to hawks, weasels, king snakes, and a variety of other species. Rattlesnakes are heavily preyed upon as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/neonate">neonate</a>s, while they are still weak and mentally immature. Large numbers of rattlesnakes are killed by humans. Rattlesnake populations in many areas are severely <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/threatened_species">threatened</a> by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_destruction">habitat destruction</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/poaching">poaching</a>, and extermination campaigns.
Rattlesnake bites are the leading cause of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snakebite">snakebite</a> injuries in North America. However, rattlesnakes rarely bite unless provoked or threatened; if treated promptly, the bites are rarely fatal.
The <strong>Viperidae</strong> (vipers) are a family of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venomous_snake">venomous snake</a>s found all over the world, except in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, various other isolated islands, and north of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Circle">Arctic Circle</a>. All have relatively long, hinged fangs that permit deep penetration and injection of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venom">venom</a>. Four subfamilies are currently recognised.(ref: ITIS) They are also known as viperids.
A <strong>tapir</strong> ([&#x2C8;te&#x26A;p&#x259;r] [TAYp&#x259;r] or [t&#x259;&#x2C8;p&#x26A;&#x259;r] [t&#x259;PEER]) is a large, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivorous">herbivorous</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>, similar in shape to a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Suidae">pig</a>, with a short, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prehensile">prehensile</a> snout. Tapirs inhabit jungle and forest regions of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America">Central America</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeastern_Asia">Southeastern Asia</a>. The five extant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of tapirs are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_American_tapir">Brazilian tapir</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malayan_tapir">Malayan tapir</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bairds_tapir">Bairds tapir</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kabomani_tapir">kabomani tapir</a>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mountain_tapir">mountain tapir</a>. The four species that have been evaluated (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_American_tapir">Brazilian</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malayan_tapir">Malayan</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bairds_tapir">Bairds</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mountain_tapir">mountain tapir</a>) are all classified as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">endangered</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species">vulnerable</a>. Their closest relatives are the other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/odd-toed_ungulate">odd-toed ungulate</a>s, which include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horse">horse</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/donkey">donkey</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zebra">zebra</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rhinoceros">rhinoceros</a>es.
A <strong>vole</strong> is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a> resembling a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouse">mouse</a> but with a stouter body, a shorter, hairy tail, a slightly more round head, smaller ears and eyes, and differently formed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/molar_(tooth)">molars</a> (high-crowned and with angular cusps instead of low-crowned and with rounded cusps). There are approximately 155 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of voles. They are sometimes known as meadow mice or field mice in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>. Vole species form the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arvicolinae">Arvicolinae</a> with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lemming">lemming</a>s and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muskrat">muskrat</a>s.
A <strong>toad</strong> is any of a number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/amphibian">amphibian</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frog">Anura</a> (frogs) that are characterized by dry, leathery skin, short legs, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parotoid_gland">parotoid gland</a>s.[date=March 2012]
<strong>Cobra</strong> (/&#x2C8;k&#x14D;br&#x259;/ [Br-Cobra.oggpronunciation]) is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Portuguese_language">Portuguese</a> word for &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snake">snake</a>&quot;. In English and in some other languages, it has been adopted as the name for any of various species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venomous_snake">venomous snake</a>s. Most of those species are in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elapidae">Elapidae</a>, all of which are venomous. Most of them can spread their neck ribs to form a flattened, widened hood.
Not all snakes commonly referred to as cobras are of the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a>, or even in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> Elapidae. The name &quot;cobra&quot; is short for <em>cobra de capelo</em> or <em>cobra-de-capelo</em>, which is Portuguese for &quot;snake with hood&quot;, or &quot;hood-snake&quot;.(ref: COED)<em>The Compact Oxford English Dictionary</em>. Second Edition. Clarendon Press, Oxford. ISBN 0-19-861258-3. In some modern languages, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afrikaans">Afrikaans</a>, the other part of the Portuguese name was adopted, and the predominant name for a cobra in Afrikaans is &quot;kapel&quot;.<ref name="Afrikaanse Etimologiee"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Boshoff, S. P. E.; Nienaber, G. S. " data-publisher="Die Suid-Afrikaanse Akademie vir Wetenskap en Kuns " data-location="Pretoria " data-year="1967 " data-pages=" " data-isbn=" " data-oclc=" " data-doi=" " data-accessdate=""> AfrikaanseEtimologie&#xEB; </span></ref><ref name="isbn0-624-00533-X"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Bosman, D. B.; Van der Merwe, I. W.; Hiemstra, L. W. " data-publisher="Tafelberg-uitgewers " data-location=" " data-year="1984 " data-pages=" " data-isbn="0-624-00533-X " data-oclc=" " data-doi=" " data-accessdate="">Tweetalige Woordeboek Afrikaans-Engels </span></ref> When disturbed, most of these snakes rear up and spread their necks (or hoods) in a characteristic threat display, making them a favorite of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snake_charmers">snake charmers</a> because of the dramatic effect. Long ago, snake charming used to be a religious ritual, though nowadays it has become an entertainment. Cobras, which may live up to 20&#xA0;years, are found from southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>, through southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia">Asia</a>, to some of the islands of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.
<strong>Cobra</strong> may refer to:
Most so-called, and all &quot;true&quot;, species of cobras belong to the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elapidae">Elapidae</a>. Many other notoriously venomous snake species, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mamba">mamba</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrophiinae">sea snake</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coral_snake">coral snake</a>s, also belong to Elapidae. The genus <em>Naja</em> contains over twenty species of cobras and is the most widespread and widely recognized genus of cobras, sometimes called the &quot;true&quot; cobras. Members of the genus inhabit a range from Africa through the Middle East, India, and Southeast Asia to Indonesia.
Although the king cobra, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ophiophagus_hannah">Ophiophagus hannah</a></em>, the world&#x2019;s longest venomous snake, is a member of the Elapidae and can raise a rather narrow hood if disturbed, it is not in the genus <em>Naja</em>, and, accordingly, is not a true cobra.
The other cobra of Asia is known as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_cobra">Asian, Indian or Spectacled cobra</a> (<em>Naja naja</em>) due to the eyeglass-shaped pattern on its skin. The hood of the Asian cobra is larger than that of the king cobra and is usually yellow or brown with a black and white spectacle pattern on top and two black and white spots on the lower surface although albino specimens are also found.<ref>Rylah, Juliet Bennett (4 September 2014) <a href="http://losangeles.cityzine.net/2014/09/04/albino-cobra-continues-its-suburban-reign-of-terror/">&quot;Albino Cobra Continues Its Suburban Reign Of Terror&quot;</a>. <em>Los Angeles Cityzine &#x2013; Your City &#x2013; Your Cityzine</em>.</ref>
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rinkhals">Rinkhals</a>, <em>Hemachatus haemachatus</em>, also called a spitting cobra, is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemism">endemic</a> to southern Africa. It also is not in the genus <em>Naja</em>.
Although the bites of some species are extremely dangerous because of their potent neurotoxins, cobras have not been shown to attack people unprovoked. Cobras almost never attack without a threat display, which typically involves raising the hood and hissing.[date=May 2014]
Various species of cobras prey mainly on other snakes, birds and small mammals, while its main natural predators in turn are other snakes, birds of prey, and small predatory mammals such as mongooses. [date=May 2014]
Although most cobras do not make nests, some species protect their eggs until they hatch (incubation typically taking around 60&#xA0;days).[date=May 2014]
A <strong>planarian</strong> is one of many  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flatworm">flatworm</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turbellaria">Turbellaria</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/class_(biology)">class</a>.(ref: Encyclopedia)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/462868/planarian " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" encyclopædia_britannica,_inc."="">Encyclop&#xE6;dia Britannica, Inc.&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;2010-05-01 &quot;&gt;Planarian (flatworm) &#x2013; Britannica Online Encyclopedia </span> It is also the common name for a member of the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planaria">Planaria</a></em> within the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planariidae">Planariidae</a>. Sometimes it also refers to the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dugesia">Dugesia</a></em>.<ref name="Campbell"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last="Campbell" data-first="Neil A." data-authorlink="Neil Campbell (scientist)" data-author2="Reece, Jane B. " data-authorlink2="Jane B. Reece " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" benjamin_cummings"="">Benjamin Cummings&quot; data-year=&quot;2005&quot; data-pages=&quot;1230 pp&quot; data-isbn=&quot;0-8053-7146-X&quot;&gt;Biology</span></ref>
Planaria are common to many parts of the world, living in both saltwater and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fresh_water">freshwater</a> ponds and rivers. Some species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terrestrial_animal">terrestrial</a> and are found under logs, in or on the soil, and on plants in humid areas.
Some planarians exhibit an extraordinary ability to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regeneration_(biology)">regenerate</a> lost body parts. For example, a planarian split lengthwise or crosswise will regenerate into two separate individuals. Some planarian species have two eye-spots (also known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ocellus">ocelli</a>) that can detect the intensity of light, while others have several eye-spots. The eye-spots act as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_eyes_in_invertebrates">photoreceptors</a> and are used to move away from light sources. Planaria have three <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/germ_layer">germ layer</a>s (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ectoderm">ectoderm</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mesoderm">mesoderm</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endoderm">endoderm</a>), and are acoelomate (they have a very solid body with no <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/body_cavity">body cavity</a>). They have a single-opening digestive tract; in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tricladida">Tricladida</a> planarians this consists of one anterior branch and two posterior branches.
Planarians move by beating <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cilia">cilia</a> on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ventral">ventral</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dermis">dermis</a>, allowing them to glide along on a film of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mucus">mucus</a>. Some also may move by undulations of the whole body by the contractions of muscles built into the body membrane.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Rompolas " first1=" Panteleimon " last2=" Patel-King " first2=" Ramila S. " last3=" King " first3=" Stephen M. " year=" 2009 " title=" Chapter 4 &#x2013; Schmidtea mediterranea: A Model System for Analysis of Motile Cilia " url=" " journal=" Methods in Cell Biology " volume=" 93 " issue=" " pages=" 81&#x2013;98 " doi=" 10.1016/S0091-679X(08)93004- "></span></ref>
Triclads play an important role in watercourse ecosystems and are often very important as bio-indicators.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Manenti " first1=" R " year=" 2010 " title=" &#x2013; Effect of landscape features and water quality on Triclads inhabiting head waters: the example of &lt;em&gt;Polycelis felina&lt;/em&gt; " url=" " journal=" Revue Ecologie Terre et Vie " volume=" 65 " issue=" " pages=" 279&#x2013;285 "></span></ref>
The most frequently used planarian in high school and first-year college laboratories is the brownish <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Girardia_tigrina">Girardia tigrina</a></em>. Other common species used are the blackish <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planaria_maculata">Planaria maculata</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Girardia_dorotocephala">Girardia dorotocephala</a></em>. Recently, however, the species <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schmidtea_mediterranea">Schmidtea mediterranea</a></em> has emerged as the species of choice for modern molecular biological and genomic research due to its diploid chromosomes and the existence of both asexual and sexual strains.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Newmark" data-first="Phillip A." data-author2="Alvarado, Alejandro S&#xE1;nchez " data-journal="Nature Reviews Genetics" data-date="1 March 2002" data-volume="3" data-issue="3" data-pages="210&#x2013;219" data-doi="10.1038/nrg759" data-pmid="11972158">NOT YOUR FATHERS PLANARIAN: A CLASSIC MODEL ENTERS THE ERA OF FUNCTIONAL GENOMICS</span></ref> Recent genetic screens utilizing double-stranded RNA technology have uncovered 240 genes that affect regeneration in S. mediterranea. Many of these genes have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ortholog">ortholog</a>s in the human genome.(ref: Web)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.devbio.biology.gatech.edu/?page_id" data-author="Developmental Biology, Georgia Tech" data-accessdate="2014-03-31 ">Regeneration in S. mediterranea </span>
<strong>Baboons</strong> are African and Arabian <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_monkey">Old World monkey</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Papio</em></strong>, part of the subfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cercopithecinae">Cercopithecinae</a>. The five species are some of the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ape">non-hominoid</a> members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/primate">primate</a> order; only the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mandrill">mandrill</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drill_(animal)">drill</a> are larger. Previously, the closely related <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gelada">gelada</a> (genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theropithecus">Theropithecus</a></em>) and the two species (mandrill and drill) of genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mandrillus">Mandrillus</a></em> were grouped in the same genus, and these <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_monkey">Old World monkey</a>s are still often referred to as baboons in everyday speech. They range in size and weight depending on species. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea_baboon">Guinea baboon</a> is [50cminabbr=on] and weighs only [14kglbabbr=on], while the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chacma_baboon">chacma baboon</a> can be [120cminabbr=on] and weigh [40kglbabbr=on].
<strong>Rats</strong> are various medium-sized, long-tailed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">superfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muroidea">Muroidea</a>. &quot;True rats&quot; are members of the genus <em>Rattus</em>, the most important of which to humans are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/black_rat">black rat</a>, <em>Rattus rattus</em>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/brown_rat">brown rat</a>, <em>Rattus norvegicus</em>. Many members of other rodent genera and families are also referred to as rats, and share many characteristics with true rats.
Rats are typically distinguished from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouse">mice</a> by their size. Generally, when someone discovers a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muroid">muroid</a> rodent, its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> includes the term <em>rat</em>, while if it is smaller, the name includes the term <em>mouse</em>. The muroid family is broad and complex, and the common terms <em>rat</em> and <em>mouse</em> are not <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomically</a> specific.  Scientifically, the terms are not confined to members of the <em>Rattus</em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mus_(genus)">Mus</a></em> genera, for example, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pack_rat">pack rat</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cotton_mouse">cotton mouse</a>.
The <strong>yak</strong> (<em>Bos grunniens</em> and <em>Bos mutus</em>) is a long-haired <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bovid">bovid</a> found throughout the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Himalayas">Himalaya</a> region of southern Central Asia, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> and as far north as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Russia">Russia</a>. Most yaks are domesticated <em>Bos grunniens</em>. There is also a small, vulnerable population of wild yaks, <em>Bos mutus</em>.
<strong>Tarantulas</strong> comprise a group of very large and often hairy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arachnid">arachnid</a>s  belonging to the  <strong>Theraphosidae</strong> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(taxonomy)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spider_taxonomy#Table_of_Families">of spiders</a>, of which approximately 900 species have been identified. This article only describes members of Theraphosidae, although some other members of the same suborder are commonly referred to as &quot;tarantulas&quot;. Most species of tarantulas are not dangerous to humans, and some species have become popular in the exotic pet trade.
<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utah_prairie_dog">Cynomys parvidens</a></em>}}
<strong>Prairie dogs</strong> (genus <strong><em>Cynomys</em></strong>) are mostly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herbivorous">herbivorous</a> burrowing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a>s native to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grassland">grassland</a>s of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>. The five different species of prairie dogs are: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black-tailed_prairie_dog">black-tailed</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White-tailed_prairie_dog">white-tailed</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gunnisons_prairie_dog">Gunnisons</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utah_prairie_dog">Utah</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexican_prairie_dog">Mexican prairie dog</a>s. They are a type of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ground_squirrel">ground squirrel</a>, found in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States">United States</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a>. In Mexico, prairie dogs are found primarily in the northern states, which lie at the southern end of the Great Plains: northeastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sonora">Sonora</a>, north and northeastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chihuahua_(state)">Chihuahua</a>, northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coahuila">Coahuila</a>, northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuevo_Le&#xF3;n">Nuevo Le&#xF3;n</a>, and northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tamaulipas">Tamaulipas</a>. In the United States, they range primarily to the west of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mississippi_River">Mississippi River</a>, though they have also been introduced in a few eastern locales.
The <strong>llama</strong> ([&#x2C8;l&#x251;&#x2D0;m&#x259;]; [&#x2C8;&#x29D;amalang] [&#x2C8;&#x28E;amalocal] <small>or</small> [&#x2C8;&#x292;ama]) (<em>Lama glama</em>) is a domesticated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>n <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camelid">camelid</a>, widely used as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_meat_animals">meat</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pack_animal">pack animal</a> by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inca_empire">Andean cultures</a> since <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pre-Hispanic">pre-Hispanic</a> times.
The height of a full-grown, full-size llama is [1.7to1.8mftabbr=on] tall at the top of the head, and can weigh between [130and200kglbabbr=on]. At birth, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cria">baby llama</a> (called a <em>cria</em>) can weigh between [9and14kglbabbr=on]. Llamas typically live for 15&#x2013;25 years, with some individuals surviving 30 years or more.(ref: Characteristics)
 |date=25 June 2007}}
 |date=25 June 2007}}
 |date=25 June 2007}}
They are very social animals and live with other llamas as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herd">herd</a>. The wool produced by a llama is very soft and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lanolin">lanolin</a>-free. Llamas are intelligent and can learn simple tasks after a few repetitions. When using a pack, they can carry about 25% to 30% of their body weight for 8&#x2013;13 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kilometre">km</a> (5&#x2013;8 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mile">mile</a>s).(ref: OK State)
 |date=25 June 2007}}
The name <em>llama</em> (in the past also spelled lama or glama) was adopted by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas">European settlers</a> from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_people_in_Peru">native Peruvians</a>.<ref>Oxford English Dictionary, 2nd edition, &quot;<em>llama</em>&quot;</ref>
Llamas appear to have originated from the central plains of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> about 40 million <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/year">year</a>s ago. They migrated to South America about three million years ago. By the end of the last <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation">ice age</a> (10,000&#x2013;12,000 years ago), camelids were extinct in North America.(ref: OK State)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/alpaca">alpaca</a>s in South America, and due to importation from South America in the late 20th century, there are now over 158,000 llamas and 100,000 alpacas in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States">United States</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canada">Canada</a>.(ref: Numbers)
 |date=22 January 2009}}
A <strong>wallaby</strong> is a small- or mid-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macropodidae">macropod</a> found in Australia and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a>.  They belong to the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomic</a> family as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo">kangaroo</a>s and sometimes the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a>, but kangaroos are specifically categorised into the six largest species of the family. The term wallaby is an informal designation generally used for any macropod that is smaller than a kangaroo or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wallaroo">wallaroo</a> that has not been designated otherwise.(ref: Australian Wildlife)(ref: Australian Wildlife)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.australianwildlife.com.au/kangaroo.htm" data-accessdate="6 November 2013">The Kangaroo</span>
There are 11 species of brush wallabies (g. <em>Macropus</em>, s.g. <em>Protemnodon</em>). Their head and body length is 45 to 105&#xA0;cm and the tail is 33 to 75&#xA0;cm long. The six named species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock-wallaby">rock-wallabies</a> (g. <em>Petrogale</em>) live among rocks, usually near water; two species are endangered.  The two species of hare-wallabies (g. <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagorchestes">Lagorchestes</a></em>) are small animals that have the movements and some of the habits of hares. Often called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pademelon">&quot;pademelons&quot;</a>, the three species of scrub wallabies (g. <em>Thylogale</em>) of New Guinea, the Bismarck Archipelago, and Tasmania are small and stocky, with short hind limbs and pointed noses. They are hunted for meat and fur. A similar species is the short-tailed scrub wallaby, or quokka (<em>Setonix brachyurus</em>); this species is now restricted to two offshore islands of Western Australia.  The three named species of forest wallabies (g. <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dorcopsulus">Dorcopsulus</a></em>) are native to the island of New Guinea. The dwarf wallaby is the smallest member of the genus and the smallest known member of the kangaroo family. Its length is about 46&#xA0;cm from nose to tail, and it weighs about 1.6&#xA0;kg.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/634732/wallaby" data-work="Encyclop&#xE6;dia Britannica" data-accessdate="17 January 2015">Wallaby</span></ref>
<strong>Zebras</strong> ([&#x2C8;z&#x25B;br&#x259;] [ZEBr&#x259;] or [&#x2C8;zi&#x2D0;br&#x259;] [ZEEbr&#x259;])<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?term" data-publisher=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" online_etymology_dictionary"="">Online Etymology Dictionary&quot; data-archiveurl=&quot; http://www.webcitation.org/6Wlak2VFn&quot; data-archivedate=&quot; 2015-03-04&quot; data-deadurl=&quot; no&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;2011-12-10&quot;&gt; Zebra</span></ref> are several species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>n <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equidae">equids</a> (horse family) united by their distinctive black and white striped coats. Their stripes come in different patterns, unique to each individual. They are generally social animals that live in small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harem_(zoology)">harems</a> to large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herds">herds</a>. Unlike their closest relatives, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horse">horse</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/donkey">donkey</a>s, zebras have never been truly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a>.
There are three species of zebras: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plains_zebra">plains zebra</a>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gr&#xE9;vys_zebra">Gr&#xE9;vys zebra</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mountain_zebra">mountain zebra</a>. The plains zebra and the mountain zebra belong to the subgenus Hippotigris<em>, but Gr&#xE9;vy</em>s zebra is the sole species of subgenus <em>Dolichohippus</em>. The latter resembles an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asinus">ass</a>, to which it is closely related, while the former two are more horse-like. All three belong to the genus <em>Equus</em>, along with other living equids.
The unique stripes of zebras make them one of the animals most familiar to people. They occur in a variety of habitats, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grasslands">grasslands</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savanna">savanna</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/woodland">woodland</a>s, thorny <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scrubland">scrubland</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mountain">mountain</a>s, and coastal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hill">hill</a>s. However, various anthropogenic factors have had a severe impact on zebra populations, in particular hunting for skins and habitat destruction. Gr&#xE9;vy&apos;s zebra and the mountain zebra are endangered. While plains zebras are much more plentiful, one subspecies, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quagga">quagga</a>, became extinct in the late 19th century &#x2013; though there is currently a plan, called the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quagga_Project">Quagga Project</a>, that aims to breed zebras that are phenotypically similar to the quagga in a process called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breeding_back">breeding back</a>.
A <strong>tigon</strong> [&#x2C8;ta&#x26A;&#x261;&#x259;n]  or <strong>tiglon</strong> [&#x2C8;ta&#x26A;&#x261;l&#x259;n]  is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybrid</a> cross between a male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tiger">tiger</a> (<em>Panthera tigris</em>) and a female <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lion">lion</a> (<em>Panthera leo</em>).<ref><a href="http://www.snopes.com/photos/animals/liger.asp">Liger</a>. snopes.com. Retrieved on 2013-09-17.</ref><ref><a href="http://www.gk12.iastate.edu/ask_a_scientist/questions_animals.asp">Questions about Animals</a>. Gk12.iastate.edu. Retrieved on 2013-09-17.</ref> Thus, it has parents with the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> but of different <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.  The tigon is not currently as common as the converse hybrid, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/liger">liger</a>; however, in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, Gerald Iles wrote that he had been able to obtain three tigons, but he had never seen a liger.<ref><a href="http://iam.colum.edu/students/ronald.bass/aim2/p2.pdf">Liger. Basically the coolest animal ever. Bred for their skills in magic</a>. iam.colum.edu</ref>
The tigons genome includes genetic components of both parents.(ref: autogenerated1)<a href="http://scholar.lib.vt.edu/ejournals/SPT/v6n3/lee.html">Techn&#xE9; v6n3 &#x2013; Patenting and Transgenic Organisms: A Philosophical Exploration</a>. Scholar.lib.vt.edu. Retrieved on 2013-09-17. Tigons can exhibit visible characteristics from both parents:(ref: autogenerated1)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mane_(lion)">mane</a> that a male tigon may have will appear shorter and less noticeable than a lions mane and is closer in type to the ruff of a male tiger. It is a common misconception that tigons are smaller than lions or tigers. They do not exceed the size of their parent species because they inherit growth-inhibitory <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gene">gene</a>s from the lioness mother, but they do not exhibit any kind of dwarfism or miniaturization; they often weigh around [180kglb].[date=December 2013]
<strong>Tarsiers</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/haplorrhine">haplorrhine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/primate">primate</a>s of the family <strong>Tarsiidae</strong>, which is itself the lone extant family within the infraorder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tarsiiformes">Tarsiiformes</a>. Although the group was once more widespread, all the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> living today are found in the islands of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.
<strong>Prawn</strong> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a>, used particularly in the United Kingdom, Ireland, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations">Commonwealth</a> nations, for large swimming <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crustacean">crustacean</a>s or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shrimp">shrimp</a>, especially those with commercial significance in the seafood industry. Shrimp that fall in this category often belong to the suborder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dendrobranchiata">Dendrobranchiata</a>. In North America, the term is used less frequently, typically for freshwater shrimp.
In the United Kingdom, prawn is more common on menus than shrimp, while the opposite is the case in the United States. The term prawn also loosely describes any large shrimp, especially those at 15 (or fewer) to the pound (such as king prawns or jumbo shrimp).
<strong>Swans</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatidae">Anatidae</a> within the genus <strong><em>Cygnus</em></strong>. The swans<em>close relatives include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goose">geese</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/duck">duck</a>s. Swans are grouped with the closely related geese in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anserinae">Anserinae</a> where they form the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tribe_(biology)">tribe</a> </em>Cygnini<strong>. Sometimes, they are considered a distinct subfamily, </strong>Cygninae<strong>. There are six or seven species of swan in the genus </strong>Cygnus; in addition there is another species known as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coscoroba_swan">coscoroba swan</a>, although this species is no longer considered one of the true swans. Swans usually <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mate_for_life">mate for life</a>, though &quot;divorce&quot; does sometimes occur, particularly following nesting failure, and if a mate dies, the remaining swan will take up with another. The number of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_egg">eggs</a> in each <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:clutch">clutch</a> ranges from three to eight.
<strong>Snail</strong> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> that is applied most often to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/land_snails">land snails</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/terrestrial_animal">terrestrial</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pulmonate">pulmonate</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gastropod">gastropod</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mollusc">mollusc</a>s.
However, the common name &quot;snail&quot; is also applied to most of the members of the molluscan class <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gastropoda">Gastropoda</a> that have a coiled <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gastropod_shell">shell</a> that is large enough for the animal to retract completely into. When the word &quot;snail&quot; is used in this most general sense, it includes not just land snails but also thousands of species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sea_snail">sea snail</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/freshwater_snail">freshwater snail</a>s. Occasionally a few other molluscs that are not actually gastropods, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monoplacophora">Monoplacophora</a>, which superficially resemble small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/limpet">limpet</a>s, may also informally be referred to as &quot;snails&quot;.
Snail-like animals that naturally lack a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gastropod_shell">shell</a>, or have only an internal shell, are mostly called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/slug">slug</a>s, and land snails that have only a very small shell (that they cannot retract into) are often called semi-slug.
<strong>Skinks</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lizard">lizard</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Scincidae</strong> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">infraorder</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scincomorpha">Scincomorpha</a>. With more than 1,500 described <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, the Scincidae are one of the most diverse families of lizards.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" journal=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" zootaxa"="">Zootaxa &quot; data- volume =&quot; 3701 &quot; data- issue =&quot; 5 &quot; data- year =&quot; 2013 &quot; data- pages =&quot; 473&#x2013;517 &quot; data- title =&quot; Redescription of <em>Eremiascincus fasciolatus</em> (G&#xFC;nther, 1867) (Reptilia: Squamata: Scincidae) with clarification of its synonyms and the description of a new species &quot; data- url=&quot; http://mapress.com/zootaxa/2013/f/zt03701p517.pdf &quot; data- author =&quot; Mecke, Doughty &amp; Donnellan &quot; data- doi=&quot;10.11646/zootaxa.3701.5.1&quot;&gt;</span></ref>
