The <strong>whooping crane</strong> (<em>Grus americana</em>), the tallest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>n bird, is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered">endangered</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crane_(bird)">crane</a> species named for its whooping sound. In 2003, there were about 153 pairs of whooping cranes. Along with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sandhill_crane">sandhill crane</a>, it is one of only two crane species found in North America. The whooping crane&apos;s lifespan is estimated to be 22 to 24 years in the wild. After being pushed to the brink of extinction by unregulated hunting and loss of habitat to just 21 wild and two captive whooping cranes by 1941, conservation efforts have led to a limited recovery. As of 2011, there are an estimated 437 birds in the wild and more than 165 in captivity.<ref><a href="http://www.fws.gov/northflorida/WhoopingCrane/whoopingcrane-fact-2001.htm">Whooping Crane Status and Fact Sheet</a>, U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service. Retrieved on: February 03, 2008</ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="news " data-author="Dennis Sherer " data-newspaper="TimesDaily.com " data-date="2012-02-03 " data-location="Florence, Alabama " data-url="http://www.timesdaily.com/stories/Whooping-cranes-looking-for-home-after-assisted-migration-stops,186977 " data-accessdate="2012-02-02 ">Whooping cranes looking for home after assisted migration stops </span></ref>
The <strong>domestic goat</strong> (<em>Capra aegagrus hircus</em>) is a subspecies of goat <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wild_goat">wild goat</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southwest_Asia">southwest Asia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eastern_Europe">Eastern Europe</a>.
The goat is a member of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovidae">Bovidae</a> and is closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep">sheep</a> as both are in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goat-antelope">goat-antelope</a> subfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caprinae">Caprinae</a>. There are over 300 distinct breeds of goat.(ref: hadog)<a href="http://archaeology.about.com/od/domestications/qt/goats.htm">&quot;The History of the Domestication of Goats&quot;.</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/About.com">About.com</a></em>. Accessed August 18, 2008. Goats are one of the oldest domesticated species, and have been used for their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/milk">milk</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goat_meat">meat</a>, hair, and skins over much of the world.<ref>Coffey, Linda, Margo Hale, and Ann Wells; <a href="http://attra.ncat.org/attra-pub/goatoverview.html">&quot;Goats: Sustainable Production Overview.</a></ref> In 2011, there were more than 924 million live goats around the globe, according to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations">UN</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>.<ref>[url = http://faostat3.fao.org/faostat-gateway/go/to/home/Epublisher = United Nations <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Food_and_Agricultural_Organization">Food and Agricultural Organization</a>title = FAOSTAT]</ref>
Female goats are referred to as &quot;does&quot; or &quot;nannies&quot;, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entire_(animal)">intact</a> males as &quot;bucks&quot;, &quot;billies&quot;, or &quot;rams&quot; and their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/offspring">offspring</a> are &quot;kids&quot;. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castration">Castrated</a> males are &quot;wethers&quot;. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goat_meat">Goat meat</a> from younger animals is called &quot;kid&quot; or <em>cabrito</em> (Spanish), and from older animals is simply known as &quot;goat&quot; or sometimes called <em>chevon</em> (French), or in some areas &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mutton">mutton</a>&quot; (which more often refers to adult <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep">sheep</a> meat).
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image:Sint_Michael_Zwolle_Stadszegel_1295.jpg">thumb</a> from 1295 with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_(archangel)">Saint Michael</a> killing a basilisk]]
In <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe">Europe</a>an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bestiary">bestiaries</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/legend">legend</a>s, a <strong>basilisk</strong> ([&#x2C8;b&#xE6;z&#x26A;l&#x26A;sk],<ref><a href="http://www.askoxford.com/concise_oed/basilisk?view=uk">AskOxford: &quot;basilisk&quot;</a></ref> from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_language">Greek</a> &#x3B2;&#x3B1;&#x3C3;&#x3B9;&#x3BB;&#x3AF;&#x3C3;&#x3BA;&#x3BF;&#x3C2; <em>basil&#xED;skos</em>, &quot;little king;&quot; Latin <em>regulus</em>) is a legendary <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptile">reptile</a> reputed to be king of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serpent_(symbolism)">serpents</a> and said to have the power to cause <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/death">death</a> with a single glance. According to the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)">Naturalis Historia</a></em> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>, the basilisk of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya">Cyrene</a> is a small snake, &quot;being not more than twelve fingers in length,&quot; that is so <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venom">venom</a>ous, it leaves a wide trail of deadly venom in its wake, and its gaze is likewise lethal; its weakness is in the odor of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/weasel">weasel</a>, which, according to Pliny, was thrown into the basilisk&apos;s hole, recognizable because all the surrounding shrubs and grass had been scorched by its presence.  It is possible that the legend of the basilisk and its association with the weasel in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe">Europe</a> was inspired by accounts of certain species of Asiatic snakes (such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/king_cobra">king cobra</a>) and their natural predator, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mongoose">mongoose</a>.
A <strong>snipe</strong> is any of about 25 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wader">wading</a> bird species in three <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scolopacidae">Scolopacidae</a>. They are characterized by a very long, slender bill and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crypsis">crypsis</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plumage">plumage</a>. The <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallinago">Gallinago</a></em> snipes have a nearly worldwide distribution, the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/jack_snipe">Lymnocryptes</a></em> jack snipe is restricted to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Europe">Europe</a> and the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coenocorypha">Coenocorypha</a></em> snipes are found only in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand_Outlying_Islands">Outlying Islands of New Zealand</a>.  The three species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/painted_snipe">painted snipe</a> are not closely related to the typical snipes, and are placed in their own family, the Rostratulidae.
The <strong>takin</strong> ([&#x2C8;t&#x251;&#x2D0;k&#x26A;n]; <em>Budorcas taxicolor</em>; [t=&#xF62;&#xF0B;&#xF62;&#xF92;&#xFB1;&#xF0B;w=ra rgya]), also called <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle">cattle</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chamois">chamois</a></strong> or <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildebeest">gnu</a> goat</strong>,(ref: diversity)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/goat-antelope">goat-antelope</a> found in the eastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Himalayas">Himalayas</a>. The four <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> are: <em>B. t. taxicolor</em>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mishmi_takin">Mishmi takin</a>; <em>B. t. bedfordi</em>, the Shaanxi takin or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/golden_takin">golden takin</a>; <em>B. t. tibetana</em>, the Tibetan or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sichuan_takin">Sichuan takin</a>; and <em>B. t. whitei</em>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bhutan_takin">Bhutan takin</a>. Whilst the takin has in the past been placed together with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muskox">muskox</a> in the tribe Ovibovini, more recent <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mitochondrial_DNA">mitochondrial</a> research shows a closer relationship to <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovis">Ovis</a></em> (sheep). Its physical similarity to the muskox is therefore an example of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/convergent_evolution">convergent evolution</a>.<ref> <a href="http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/B6WNH-45M2XR5-6/2/4aaf029ac7f051f4f28b7668809ca6a7">Pamela Groves, Gerald F. Shields, CytochromeBSequences Suggest Convergent Evolution of the Asian Takin and Arctic Muskox, <em>Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution</em>, Volume 8, Issue 3, December 1997, Pages 363-374, ISSN 1055-7903, </a> [10.1006/mpev.1997.0423].</ref> The takin is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/national_animal">national animal</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bhutan">Bhutan</a>.(ref: BrownArmington2007)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-editor="Lindsay Brown, Stan Armington" data-url="http://books.google.com/books?id" data-accessdate="15 September 2011" data-year="2007" data-publisher="Lonely Planet" data-isbn="978-1-74059-529-2" data-page="87" data-chapter=" The Takin - Bhutan&apos;s National Animal" data-author="Tashi Wangchuk">Bhutan</span>
A <strong>weasel</strong> [&#x2C8;wi&#x2D0;z&#x259;l] is a mammal belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Mustela</em> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelidae">Mustelidae</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a>. The genus includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/least_weasel">least weasel</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polecat">polecat</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stoat">stoat</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ferret">ferret</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mink">mink</a>s.[reason=the preceding two sentences are difficult to reconciledate=February 2015] Members of this genus are small, active predators, with long and slender bodies and short legs. The Mustelidae family (which also includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/badger">badger</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/otter">otter</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wolverine">wolverine</a>s) is often referred to as the &quot;weasel family&quot;. In the UK, the term &quot;weasel&quot; usually refers to the smallest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustela_nivalis">Mustela nivalis</a></em> (the least weasel).<ref name="SOED"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-year="2007" data-publisher="Oxford University Press" data-location="United Kingdom" data-isbn="0199206872" data-pages="3804">Shorter Oxford English dictionary</span></ref>
Weasels vary in length from [173to217mmin1abbr=on],(ref: WeaselSize)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.mammal.org.uk/species-factsheets/Weasel" data-publisher="The Mammal Society" data-="" accessdate="18 January 2013">The Weasel</span> females being smaller than the males, and usually have red or brown upper coats and white bellies; some populations of some species <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moult">moult</a> to a wholly white coat in winter. They have long, slender bodies, which enable them to follow their prey into burrows. Their tails may be from [34to52mmin1abbr=on] long.(ref: WeaselSize)
Weasels feed on small mammals, and have from time to time been considered <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vermin">vermin</a>, since some species took <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/poultry">poultry</a> from farms, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rabbit">rabbit</a>s from commercial warrens. They can be found all across the world except for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallace_Line">neighbouring islands</a>.
The <strong>woodpeckers</strong> are part of the <strong>Picidae</strong> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a>, a group of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/near-passerine">near-passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that also consist of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/piculet">piculet</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wryneck">wryneck</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sapsucker">sapsucker</a>s. Members of this family are found worldwide, except for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, and the extreme polar regions. Most species live in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forests">forests</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/woodland">woodland</a> habitats, although a few species are known to live in treeless areas, such as rocky hillsides and deserts.
The Picidae are just one of eight living families in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piciformes">Piciformes</a>. Other members of Piciformes, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/jacamar">jacamar</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/puffbird">puffbird</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capitonidae">barbets</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/toucan">toucan</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/honeyguide">honeyguide</a>s, have traditionally been thought to be closely related to the woodpeckers, piculets, wrynecks, and sapsuckers. More recently, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_sequence">DNA sequence</a> analyses have confirmed this view.
There are about 200 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> and about 30 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a> in this family. Many species are threatened or endangered due to loss of habitat or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_fragmentation">habitat fragmentation</a>. Two species of woodpeckers, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ivory-billed_woodpecker">ivory-billed woodpecker</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/imperial_woodpecker">imperial woodpecker</a>, have been considered extinct for about 30 years (although there has been speculation recently[date = January 2015] that these species may still exist).
<strong>Domesticated ducks</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ducks">ducks</a> that are raised for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/meat">meat</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/egg_(food)">egg</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Down_feathers">down</a>. Many ducks are also kept for show, as pets, or for their ornamental value. Almost all varieties of domesticated duck are descended from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mallard">mallard</a> (<em>Anas platyrhynchos</em>), apart from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muscovy_duck">Muscovy duck</a> (<em>Cairina moschata</em>).<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://digimorph.org/specimens/anas_platyrhynchos/skull/ " data-publisher="Digimorph.org " data-date=" " data-accessdate="23 December 2012">Anas platyrhynchos, Domestic Duck; DigiMorph Staff - The University of Texas at Austin </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-author="Sy Montgomery " data-url="http://www.britannica.com/eb/topic-360302/mallard " data-publisher="Britannica.com " data-date=" " data-accessdate="23 December 2012">Mallard; Encyclop&#xE6;dia Britannica </span></ref>
The <strong>sugar glider</strong> (<em>Petaurus breviceps</em>) is a small, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/omnivorous">omnivorous</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal">arboreal</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nocturnality">nocturnal</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gliding_possum">gliding possum</a> belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsupial">marsupial</a> infraclass. The common name refers to its preference for sugary nectarous foods and ability to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gliding_flight">glide</a> through the air, much like a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flying_squirrel">flying squirrel</a>. Due to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/convergent_evolution">convergent evolution</a>, they have very similar appearance and habits to the flying squirrel, but are not closely related.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://evolution.berkeley.edu/evolibrary/article/analogy_02" data-work="Understanding Evolution" data-publisher="The University of California Museum of Paleontology" data-accessdate="1 October 2012">Analogy: Squirrels and Sugar Gliders</span></ref> The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature">scientific name</a>, <em>Petaurus breviceps</em>, translates from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin_(language)">Latin</a> as &quot;short-headed rope-dancer&quot;, a reference to their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canopy_(biology)">canopy</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/acrobatics">acrobatics</a>.(ref: Tasmania Online)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.parks.tas.gov.au/index.aspx?base" data-publisher="Parks &amp; Wildlife Service, Tasmania Online" data-accessdate="7 October 2012">Sugar Glider, Petaurus breviceps</span>
Sugar gliders are characterised by their gliding membrane, known as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/patagium">patagium</a>, which extends from their forelegs to hindlegs.<ref name="Endo(1998)"><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last1="Endo" data-first1="H" data-last2="Yokokawa" data-first2="K" data-last3="Kurohmaru" data-first3="M" data-last4="Hiyashi" data-first4="Y" data-journal="Annals of Anatomy" data-date="1998" data-volume="180" data-pages="93&#x2013;96" data-doi="10.1016/S0940-9602(98)80149-0" data-accessdate="1 May 2015">Functional anatomy of gliding membrane muscles in the sugar glider (&quot;Petaurus breviceps&quot;)</span></ref> Gliding serves as an efficient means of both locating food and evading predators.(ref: dpiwtas)
The sugar glider is endemic to mainland <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Guinea">New Guinea</a> and its surrounding islands; and was introduced to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tasmania">Tasmania</a> in 1835.<ref name="smith(73)"><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last1="Smith" data-first1="Meredith J." data-journal="Mammalian Species" data-date="13 June 1973" data-issue="30" data-pages="1&#x2013;5" data-doi="10.2307/3503785" data-accessdate="1 May 2015">Petaurus breviceps</span></ref>
The <strong>walrus</strong> (<em>Odobenus rosmarus</em>) is a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pinniped">flippered</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marine_mammal">marine mammal</a> with a discontinuous distribution about the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Pole">North Pole</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arctic_Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subarctic">subarctic</a> seas of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>. The walrus is the only living species in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> Odobenidae and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <em>Odobenus</em>. This species is subdivided into three <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a>: the Atlantic walrus (<em>O. r. rosmarus</em>) which lives in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, the Pacific walrus (<em>O. r. divergens</em>) which lives in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, and <em>O. r. laptevi</em>, which lives in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laptev_Sea">Laptev Sea</a> of the Arctic Ocean.
Adult walruses are easily recognized by their prominent <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tusk">tusk</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vibrissa">whiskers</a>,  and bulkiness. Adult males in the Pacific  can weigh more than [2000kglbabbr=on](ref: Walrus: Physical Characteristics)<a href="http://www.seaworld.org/infobooks/walrus/phycharwal.html">Walrus: Physical Characteristics</a>. seaworld.org and, among <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pinniped">pinniped</a>s, are exceeded in size only by the two species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elephant_seal">elephant seal</a>s.(ref: Fay85)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author=" Fay, F.H." data-year=" 1985" data-url=" http://www.science.smith.edu/departments/Biology/VHAYSSEN/msi/default.html" data-title=" Odobenus rosmarus" data-journal=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" mammalian_species"="">Mammalian Species&quot; data-volume =&quot; 238&quot; data-pages =&quot; 1&#x2013;7&quot; data-doi =&quot; 10.2307/3503810&quot; data-issue =&quot; 238&quot; data-jstor =&quot; 3503810&quot;&gt;</span> Walruses live mostly in shallow waters above the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continental_shelves">continental shelves</a>, spending significant amounts of their lives on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sea_ice">sea ice</a> looking for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/benthic_zone">benthic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bivalvia">bivalve mollusk</a>s to eat. Walruses are relatively long-lived, social animals, and they are considered to be a &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/keystone_species">keystone species</a>&quot; in the Arctic marine regions.
The walrus has played a prominent role in the cultures of many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/circumpolar_peoples">indigenous Arctic peoples</a>, who have hunted the walrus for its meat, fat, skin, tusks, and bone. During the 19th century and the early 20th century, walruses were widely hunted and killed for their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/blubber">blubber</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/walrus_ivory">walrus ivory</a>, and meat. The population of walruses dropped rapidly all around the Arctic region. Their population has rebounded somewhat since then, though the populations of Atlantic and Laptev walruses remain fragmented and at low levels compared with the time before human interference.
<strong>Whale</strong> is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> for a widely distributed and diverse group of fully aquatic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marine_mammal">marine mammal</a>s. They are an informal grouping within the infraorder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cetacea">Cetacea</a>, excluding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dolphin">dolphin</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/porpoise">porpoise</a>s, so to zoologists the grouping is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraphyly">paraphyletic</a>. The whales comprise the extant families <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cetotheriidae">Cetotheriidae</a> (whose only living member is the pygmy right whale), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balaenopteridae">Balaenopteridae</a> (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rorquals">rorquals</a>), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balaenidae">Balaenidae</a> (right whales),  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eschrichtiidae">Eschrichtiidae</a> (the gray whale), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monodontidae">Monodontidae</a> (belugas and narwhals), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physeteridae">Physeteridae</a> (the sperm whale), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kogiidae">Kogiidae</a> (the dwarf and pygmy sperm whale), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ziphiidae">Ziphiidae</a> (the beaked whales). There are 40 extant species of whales. The two parvorders of whales, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mysticeti">Mysticeti</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odontoceti">Odontoceti</a>, are thought to have split apart around 34 million years ago. Whales, dolphins and porpoises belong to the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cetartiodactyla">Cetartiodactyla</a> with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a>s and their closest living relatives are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hippopotamidae">hippopotamuses</a>, having diverged about 40 million years ago.
Whales range in size from the [2.6mft] and [135kglbs] <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dwarf_sperm_whale">dwarf sperm whale</a> to the [34mft] and [190MTST] <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/blue_whale">blue whale</a>, which is the largest creature on earth. Several species exhibit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexual_dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, in that the females are larger than males. They have streamlined bodies and two limbs that are modified into flippers. Though not as flexible or agile as seals, whales can travel at up to 20 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Knot_(unit)">knots</a>. Balaenopterids use their throat pleats to expand the mouth to take in gulps of water. Balaenids have heads that can make up 40% of their body mass to take in water. Odontocetes have conical teeth designed for catching fish or squid. Mysticetes have a well developed sense of &quot;smell&quot;, whereas odontocetes have well-developed hearing &#x2212; their hearing, that is adapted for both air and water, is so well developed that some can survive even if they are blind. Some species are well adapted for diving to great depths. They have a layer of fat, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/blubber">blubber</a>, under the skin to keep warm in the cold water.
Although whales are widespread, most species prefer the colder waters of the Northern and Southern Hemispheres, and migrate to the equator to give birth. Odontocetes feed largely on fish and squid. A few, like the sperm whale, feed on large invertebrates, such as giant squid. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grey_whale">Grey whale</a>s are specialized for feeding on bottom-dwelling molluscs. Male whales typically mate with multiple females every year, but females only mate every two to three years. Calves are typically born in the spring and summer months and females bear all the responsibility for raising them. Mothers of some species fast and nurse their young for a relatively long period of time. Whales produce a variety of vocalizations, notably the songs of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/humpback_whale">humpback whale</a>.
Once relentlessly hunted for their products, whales are now protected by international law. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale">North Atlantic right whale</a>s nearly became extinct in the twentieth century, with a population low of 450, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gray_whale#North_Pacific">North Pacific gray whale population</a> is ranked <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Critically_Endangered">Critically Endangered</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">IUCN</a>. Besides whaling, they also face threats from bycatch and marine pollution. The meat, blubber and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/baleen">baleen</a> of whales have traditionally been used by indigenous peoples of the Arctic. Whales have been depicted in various cultures worldwide, notably by the Inuit and the coastal peoples of Vietnam and Ghana, who sometimes hold whale funerals. Whales occasionally feature in literature and film, as in the great white whale of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herman_Melville">Herman Melville</a>s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moby_Dick">Moby Dick</a>. Small whales, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beluga_whale">belugas</a>, are sometimes kept in captivity and trained to perform tricks, but breeding success has been poor and the animals often die within a few months of capture. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whale_watching">Whale watching</a> has become a form of tourism around the world.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rabbit">Rabbit</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s are different varieties of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestic_rabbit">domestic rabbit</a> created through <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/selective_breeding">selective breeding</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/natural_selection">natural selection</a>. Breeds recognized by organizations such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Rabbit_Breeders_Association">American Rabbit Breeders Association</a> (ARBA) or the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Rabbit_Council">British Rabbit Council</a> (BRC) may be exhibited and judged in rabbit shows. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Breeder">Breeder</a>s attempt to emulate the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed_standard">breed standard</a> by which each breed is judged.
A <em>list</em> is any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/enumeration">enumeration</a> of a set of items. <strong>List</strong> or <strong>lists</strong> may also refer to:
<strong>Smelt</strong> may refer to:
A <strong>tuna</strong> is a saltwater <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/finfish">finfish</a> that belongs to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tribe_(biology)">tribe</a> <strong>Thunnini</strong>, a sub-grouping of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scombridae">mackerel family</a> (Scombridae)&#xA0;&#x2013; which together with the tunas, also includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bonito">bonito</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mackerel">mackerel</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_mackerel">Spanish mackerel</a>s.  Thunnini comprises fifteen <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> across five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a>, the sizes of which vary greatly, ranging from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bullet_tuna">bullet tuna</a> (max. length: [50cmftabbr=on], weight: [1.8kglb0abbr=on]) up to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna">Atlantic bluefin tuna</a> (max. length: [4.6mft0abbr=on], weight: [684kglb0abbr=on]). The bluefin averages [2mftabbr=on], and is believed to live for up to 50 years.
Tuna and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamniformes">mackerel sharks</a> are the only species of fish that can maintain a body temperature higher than that of the surrounding water. An active and agile predator, the tuna has a sleek, streamlined body, and is among the fastest-swimming <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pelagic">pelagic</a> fish&#xA0;&#x2013; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/yellowfin_tuna">yellowfin tuna</a>, for example, is capable of speeds of up to [75km/hmphabbr=on].<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Block" data-first="Barbara A." data-author2="Booth, David " data-author3="Carey, Francis G. " data-journal="Journal of Experimental Biology" data-year="1992" data-volume="166" data-pages="267&#x2013;284" data-url="http://jeb.biologists.org/content/166/1/267.full.pdf" data-accessdate="19 September 2012" data-publisher="Company of Biologists Ltd." data-format="PDF" data-issn="0022-0949">Direct measurement of swimming speeds and depth of blue marlin</span></ref>  Found in warm seas, it is extensively fished commercially, and is popular as a game fish. As a result of over-fishing, stocks of some tuna species such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southern_bluefin_tuna">southern bluefin tuna</a> have been reduced dangerously close to the point of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extinction">extinction</a>.
The <strong>tiger shark</strong> (<em>Galeocerdo cuvier</em>) is a species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/requiem_shark">requiem shark</a> and the only extant member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Galeocerdo</em></strong>. Commonly known as the &quot;Sea Tiger&quot;, the tiger shark is a relatively large macropredator, capable of attaining a length over [5mftabbr=on]. It is found in many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical">tropical</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/temperate">temperate</a> waters, and it is especially common around central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific">Pacific</a> islands. Its name derives from the dark stripes down its body which resemble a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tiger">tiger</a>&apos;s pattern, which fade as the shark matures.
The tiger shark is a solitary, mostly nocturnal hunter, and is notable for having the widest food spectrum of all sharks, consuming a variety of prey ranging from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crustacean">crustacean</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pinniped">seals</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/squid">squid</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/turtle">turtle</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrophiinae">sea snake</a>s to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dolphin">dolphin</a>s and even other smaller sharks. The tiger shark has been known to eat inedible manmade objects that linger in its stomach, and it has a reputation as a &quot;garbage eater&quot;. While the tiger shark sits atop the food chain as an apex predator, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/killer_whales">killer whales</a> have been known on occasion to prey on them.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.dailymail.co.uk/video/sciencetech/video-1122910/Incredible-moment-killer-whales-hunt-kill-tiger-shark.html" data-website="DailyMail.com">Incredible moment killer whales hunt and kill a tiger shark</span></ref>   The tiger shark is considered a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">near threatened species</a> due to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shark_finning">finning</a> and fishing by humans.
The <strong>guppy</strong> ([la<em>Poecilia reticulata</em>]), also known as <strong>millionfish</strong> and <strong>rainbow fish</strong>,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.fishbase.org/comnames/CommonNamesList.php?ID" data-work="FishBase.org " data-accessdate="16 November 2013">Common Names of <em>Poecilia reticulata</em> </span></ref> is one of the world&apos;s most widely distributed tropical fish, and one of the most popular <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_freshwater_aquarium_fish_species">freshwater aquarium fish species</a>. It is a member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Poeciliidae">Poeciliidae</a> family and, like almost all American members of the family, is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/live-bearing_aquarium_fish">live-bearing</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.aquaticcommunity.com/livebearer/guppy.php " data-work="AquaticCommunity.com " data-accessdate="24 February 2013 " data-archiveurl="http://web.archive.org/web/20120609133734/http://www.aquaticcommunity.com/livebearer/guppy.php " data-archivedate="9 June 2012 " data-deadurl="no">Guppy Fish </span></ref> Guppies, whose natural range is in northeast South America, were introduced to many habitats and are now found all over the world. They are highly adaptable and thrive in many different environmental and ecological conditions.(ref: Magurran2005)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish_fin">fins</a>, while females are duller in colour. Wild guppies generally feed on a variety of food sources, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/benthic_zone">benthic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/algae">algae</a> and aquatic insect <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>e.(ref: Dussault1981)(ref: Magurran2005)
<strong>Sheep</strong> (<em>Ovis aries</em>) are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quadruped">quadruped</a>al, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ruminant">ruminant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s typically kept as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/livestock">livestock</a>. Like all ruminants, sheep are members of the order Artiodactyla, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/even-toed_ungulate">even-toed ungulate</a>s. Although the name &quot;sheep&quot; applies to many species in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ovis">Ovis</a></em>, in everyday usage it almost always refers to <em>Ovis aries</em>. Numbering a little over one billion, domestic sheep are also the most numerous species of sheep. An adult female sheep is referred to as a <em>ewe</em> ([ju&#x2D0;]), an intact male as a <em>ram</em> or occasionally a <em>tup</em>, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Castration">castrated</a> male as a <em>wether</em>, and a younger sheep as a  <em>lamb</em>.
Sheep are most likely descended from the wild <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mouflon">mouflon</a> of Europe and Asia. One of the earliest animals to be domesticated for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/agriculture">agricultural</a> purposes, sheep are raised for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wool">fleece</a>, meat (lamb, hogget or mutton) and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sheep_milk">milk</a>. A sheep&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wool">wool</a> is the most widely used animal fiber, and is usually harvested by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheep_shearing">shearing</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikt:ovine">Ovine</a> meat is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton">lamb</a> when from younger animals and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton">mutton</a> when from older ones.  Sheep continue to be important for wool and meat today, and are also occasionally raised for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheepskin_(material)">pelts</a>, as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dairy">dairy</a> animals, or as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/model_organism">model organism</a>s for science.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sheep_husbandry">Sheep husbandry</a> is practised throughout the majority of the inhabited world, and has been fundamental to many civilizations. In the modern era, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>, the southern and central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>n nations, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Isles">British Isles</a> are most closely associated with sheep production.
Sheepraising has a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_sheep_husbandry">lexicon of unique terms</a> which vary considerably by region and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dialect">dialect</a>. Use of the word <em>sheep</em> began in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_English">Middle English</a> as a derivation of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_English">Old English</a> word <em>sc&#x113;ap</em>; it is both the singular and plural name for the animal. A group of sheep is called a flock, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herd">herd</a> or mob. Many other specific terms for the various life stages of sheep exist, generally related to lambing, shearing, and age.
Being a key animal in the history of farming, sheep have a deeply entrenched place in human culture, and find representation in much modern language and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/symbology">symbology</a>. As livestock, sheep are most often associated with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pastoral">pastoral</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arcadia_(utopia)">Arcadian</a> imagery. Sheep figure in many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mythology">mythologies</a>&#x2014;such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Fleece">Golden Fleece</a>&#x2014;and major religions, especially the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abrahamic">Abrahamic</a> traditions. In both ancient and modern religious ritual, sheep are used as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_sacrifice">sacrificial animals</a>.
The <strong>bald eagle</strong> (<em>Haliaeetus leucocephalus</em>, from Greek <em>hali</em> &quot;sea&quot;, <em>ai&#x113;tos</em> &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/eagle">eagle</a>&quot;, <em>leuco</em> &quot;white&quot;, <em>cephalos</em> &quot;head&quot;) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_of_prey">bird of prey</a> found in North America. A <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sea_eagle">sea eagle</a>, it has two known subspecies and forms a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species_complex">species pair</a> with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/white-tailed_eagle">white-tailed eagle</a> (<em>Haliaeetus albicilla</em>). Its range includes most of Canada and Alaska, all of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/contiguous_United_States">contiguous United States</a>, and northern Mexico. It is found near large bodies of open water with an abundant food supply and old-growth trees for nesting.
The bald eagle is an opportunistic feeder which subsists mainly on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a>, which it swoops down and snatches from the water with its talons. It builds the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_nest">nest</a> of any North American bird and the largest tree nests ever recorded for any animal species, up to [4mftsp=usabbr = on] deep, [2.5mftsp=usabbr = on] wide, and [1tSTlk = onsp = us] in weight. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_maturity">Sexual maturity</a> is attained at the age of four to five years.
Bald eagles are not actually <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bald">bald</a>; the name derives from an older meaning of &quot;white headed&quot;. The adult is mainly brown with a white head and tail. The sexes are identical in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/plumage">plumage</a>, but females are about 25 percent larger than males. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beak">beak</a> is large and hooked. The plumage of the immature is brown.
The bald eagle is both the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_national_birds">national bird</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_national_animals">national animal</a> of the United States of America. The bald eagle appears on its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Seal_of_the_United_States">seal</a>. In the late 20th century it was on the brink of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/local_extinction">extirpation</a> in the contiguous United States. Populations have since recovered and the species was removed from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States">U.S. government</a>&apos;s list of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">endangered species</a> on July 12, 1995 and transferred to the list of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/threatened_species">threatened species</a>. It was removed from the List of Endangered and Threatened Wildlife in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Continental_United_States">Lower 48</a> States on June 28, 2007.
The <strong>capybara</strong> (<em>Hydrochoerus hydrochaeris</em>) is a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a> of the genus <em>Hydrochoerus</em> of which the only other extant member is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lesser_capybara">lesser capybara</a> (<em>Hydrochoerus isthmius</em>).  The capybara is the largest rodent in the world. Close relatives are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/guinea_pig">guinea pig</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rock_cavy">rock cavies</a>, and it is more distantly related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_agouti">agouti</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chinchilla">chinchilla</a>s, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coypu">coypu</a>. Native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>, the capybara inhabits <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/savanna">savanna</a>s and dense forests and lives near bodies of water. It is a highly social species and can be found in groups as large as 100 individuals, but usually lives in groups of 10&#x2013;20 individuals. The capybara is not a threatened species and is hunted for its meat, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hide_(skin)">hide</a> and also for a grease from its thick fatty <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skin">skin</a> which is used in the pharmaceutical trade.<ref><a href="http://www.arkive.org/capybara/hydrochoerus-hydrochaeris/">Capybara (<em>Hydrochoerus hydrochaeris</em>)</a>. ARKive.org</ref>
<strong>Chipmunks</strong> are small, striped <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodents">rodents</a> of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sciuridae">Sciuridae</a>.  All species of chipmunks are found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>, with the exception of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siberian_chipmunk">Siberian chipmunk</a>, which is found primarily in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia">Asia</a>.
An <strong>alpaca</strong> (<em>Vicugna pacos</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication">domesticated</a> species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>n <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camelid">camelid</a>. It resembles a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/llama">llama</a> in appearance.
There are two breeds of alpaca; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:es:Suri_(alpaca)">Suri</a> alpaca and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:es:Huacaya_(alpaca)">Huacaya</a> alpaca.
Alpacas are kept in herds that graze on the level heights of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andes">Andes</a> of southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peru">Peru</a>, northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecuador">Ecuador</a>, and northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chile">Chile</a> at an altitude of [3500mabbr=on] to [5000mabbr=on] above sea level, throughout the year.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url=" http://www.fao.org/docrep/v9384e/v9384e05.htm#1.4.3 " data-publisher=" UN Food and Agriculture Organization">Harvesting of textile animal fibres</span></ref> Alpacas are considerably smaller than llamas, and unlike llamas, they were not bred to be <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Working_animal">beasts of burden</a>, but were bred specifically for their fiber. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpaca_fiber">Alpaca fiber</a> is used for making knitted and woven items, similar to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wool">wool</a>. These items include blankets, sweaters, hats, gloves, scarves, a wide variety of textiles and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/poncho">poncho</a>s in South America, and sweaters, socks, coats and bedding in other parts of the world. The fiber comes in more than 52 natural colors as classified in Peru, 12 as classified in Australia and 16 as classified in the United States.
In the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/textile">textile</a> industry, &quot;alpaca&quot; primarily refers to the hair of Peruvian alpacas, but more broadly it refers to a style of fabric originally made from alpaca hair, but now often made from similar fibers, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mohair">mohair</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Icelandic_sheep">Icelandic sheep</a> wool, or even high-quality English wool. [date=June 2007] In trade, distinctions are made between alpacas and the several styles of mohair and luster.
An adult alpaca generally is between 81 and 99&#xA0;cm in height at the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/withers">withers</a>. They usually weigh between 48 and 84&#xA0;kg (106 and 185&#xA0;lbs).
<strong>African elephants</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elephant">elephant</a>s of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Loxodonta</em></strong> (from the Greek words <em>loxo</em> (oblique sided) and <em>donta</em> (tooth)).<ref name="Fossils"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last1="Kalb " data-first1="Jon E. " data-authorlink1="Jon Kalb " data-last2="Mebrate " data-first2="Assefa " data-publisher="The American Philosophical Society " data-year="1993 " data-location="Independence Square, Philadelphia " data-pages="52&#x2013;59 " data-url="https://books.google.com/books?id" data-isbn="0-87169-831-5">Fossil Elephantoids from the Hominid-Bearing Awash Group, Middle Awash Valley, Afar Depression, Ethiopia </span></ref> The genus consists of two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">extant</a> species: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_bush_elephant">African bush elephant</a> and the smaller <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_forest_elephant">African forest elephant</a>. <em>Loxodonta</em> is one of two existing genera of the family, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elephantidae">Elephantidae</a>.<ref name="MSW3">[id=11500008 page=91 heading=Genus <em>Loxodonta</em>]</ref> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil">Fossil</a> remains of <em>Loxodonta</em> have been found only in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>, in strata as old as the middle <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliocene">Pliocene</a>.
<strong>Crayfish</strong>, also known as <strong>crawfish</strong>, <strong>crawdads</strong>, <strong>freshwater lobsters</strong>, or <strong>mudbugs</strong>, are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/freshwater">freshwater</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crustacean">crustacean</a>s resembling small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lobster">lobster</a>s, to which they are related; taxonomically, they are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/superfamilies">superfamilies</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astacoidea">Astacoidea</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parastacoidea">Parastacoidea</a>. They breathe through feather-like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill">gill</a>s and are found in bodies of water. Some species are found in brooks and streams where there is fresh water running, while others thrive in swamps, ditches, and rice paddies. Most crayfish cannot tolerate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_pollution">polluted water</a>, although some species such as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Procambarus_clarkii">Procambarus clarkii</a></em> are hardier. Crayfish feed on living and dead animals and plants.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Christoph Needon, Johannes Petermann, Peter Scheffel &amp; Bernd Scheibe " data-publisher="Urania Verlag " data-location="Leipzig " data-year="1971">Plants and Animals (Pflanzen und Tiere) </span></ref>
<strong>Damselflies</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s of suborder <strong>Zygoptera</strong> in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odonata">Odonata</a>. They are similar to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dragonfly">dragonflies</a>, which constitute the other odonatan suborder, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anisoptera">Anisoptera</a>, but are smaller, have slimmer bodies, and most species fold the wings along the body when at rest. An ancient group, damselflies have existed since at least the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_Permian">Lower Permian</a>, and are found on every continent except <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>.
All damselflies are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>y; both nymphs and adults eat other insects. The nymphs are aquatic, with different species living in a variety of freshwater habitats including acid bogs, ponds, lakes and rivers. The nymphs moult repeatedly, at the last moult climbing out of the water to undergo <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/metamorphosis">metamorphosis</a>. The skin splits down the back, they emerge and inflate their wings and abdomen to gain their adult form. Their presence on a body of water indicates that it is relatively unpolluted, but their dependence on freshwater makes them vulnerable to damage to their wetland habitats.
Some species of damselfly have elaborate courtship behaviours. Many species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexually_dimorphic">sexually dimorphic</a>, the males often being more brightly coloured than the females. Like dragonflies, they reproduce using indirect <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insemination">insemination</a> and delayed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fertilisation">fertilisation</a>. A mating pair form a shape known as a &quot;heart&quot; or &quot;wheel&quot;, the male clasping the female at the back of the head, the female curling her abdomen down to pick up sperm from secondary genitalia at the base of the male&apos;s abdomen. The pair often remain together with the male still clasping the female while she lays eggs within the tissue of plants in or near water using a robust <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ovipositor">ovipositor</a>.
Fishing flies that mimic damselfly nymphs are sometimes used in wet-fly fishing. Damselflies sometimes provide the subject for personal jewellery such as brooches.
The <strong>horse</strong> (<em>Equus ferus caballus</em>)<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="International Commission on Zoological Nomenclature" data-year="2003" data-journal="Bull. Zool. Nomencl." data-volume="60" data-issue="1" data-pages="81&#x2013;84" data-url="http://www.nhm.ac.uk/hosted_sites/iczn/BZNMar2003opinions.htm" data-archiveurl="http://web.archive.org/web/20070821235959/http://www.nhm.ac.uk/hosted_sites/iczn/BZNMar2003opinions.htm" data-archivedate="2007-08-21">Usage of 17 specific names based on wild species which are pre-dated by or contemporary with those based on domestic animals (Lepidoptera, Osteichthyes, Mammalia): conserved. Opinion 2027 (Case 3010)</span></ref> is one of two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon">extant</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wild_horse"><em>Equus ferus</em></a>. It is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/odd-toed_ungulate">odd-toed ungulate</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a> belonging to the taxonomic family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equidae">Equidae</a>. The horse has <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/evolution_of_the_horse">evolved</a> over the past 45 to 55&#xA0;million years from a small multi-toed creature, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyracotherium">Hyracotherium</a>, into the large, single-toed animal of today. Humans began to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication">domesticate</a> horses around 4000 BC, and their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication_of_the_horse">domestication</a> is believed to have been widespread by 3000 BC. Horses in the subspecies <em>caballus</em> are domesticated, although some domesticated populations live in the wild as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/feral_horse">feral horse</a>s. These feral populations are not true <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wild_horse">wild horse</a>s, as this term is used to describe horses that have never been domesticated, such as the endangered <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Przewalski&apos;s_horse">Przewalski&apos;s horse</a>, a separate subspecies, and the only remaining true <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wild_horse">wild horse</a>. There is an extensive, specialized vocabulary used to describe equine-related concepts, covering everything from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anatomy">anatomy</a> to life stages, size, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/equine_coat_color">colors</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horse_markings">markings</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/horse_breed">breeds</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/animal_locomotion">locomotion</a>, and behavior.
Horses&apos; anatomy enables them to make use of speed to escape predators and they have a well-developed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equilibrioception">sense of balance</a> and a strong <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fight-or-flight_response">fight-or-flight response</a>. Related to this need to flee from predators in the wild is an unusual trait: horses are able to sleep both standing up and lying down. Female horses, called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mare">mare</a>s, carry their young for approximately 11&#xA0;months, and a young horse, called a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/foal">foal</a>, can stand and run shortly following birth. Most domesticated horses begin training under <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/saddle">saddle</a> or in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horse_harness">harness</a> between the ages of two and four. They reach full adult development by age five, and have an average lifespan of between 25 and 30&#xA0;years.
Horse breeds are loosely divided into three categories based on general temperament: spirited &quot;hot bloods&quot; with speed and endurance; &quot;cold bloods&quot;, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/draft_horse">draft horse</a>s and some <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pony">ponies</a>, suitable for slow, heavy work; and &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/warmblood">warmblood</a>s&quot;, developed from crosses between hot bloods and cold bloods, often focusing on creating breeds for specific riding purposes, particularly in Europe. There are more than 300&#xA0;breeds of horse in the world today, developed for many different uses.
Horses and humans interact in a wide variety of sport competitions and non-competitive recreational pursuits, as well as in working activities such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mounted_police">police work</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/working_animal">agriculture</a>, entertainment, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hippotherapy">therapy</a>. Horses were historically used in warfare, from which a wide variety of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/equestrianism">riding</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/driving_(horse)">driving</a> techniques developed, using many different styles of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horse_tack">equipment</a> and methods of control. Many products are derived from horses, including meat, milk, hide, hair, bone, and pharmaceuticals extracted from the urine of pregnant mares. Humans provide domesticated horses with food, water and shelter, as well as attention from specialists such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/veterinarian">veterinarian</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/farrier">farrier</a>s.
The <strong>guinea pig</strong> (<em>Cavia porcellus</em>), also called the <strong>cavy</strong> or <strong>domestic guinea pig</strong>, is a species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rodent">rodent</a> belonging to the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caviidae">Caviidae</a> and the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cavia">Cavia</a></em>. Despite their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a>, these animals are not in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/suidae">pig family</a>, nor are they from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea_(region)">Guinea</a>. They originated in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andes">Andes</a>, and earlier studies based on biochemistry and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybridization</a> suggested they are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domesticated</a> descendants of a closely related species of cavy such as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazilian_guinea_pig">Cavia aperea</a></em>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shiny_Guinea_Pig">C. fulgida</a></em>, or <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Montane_Guinea_Pig">C. tschudii</a></em> and, therefore, do not exist naturally in the wild.<ref name="Walker"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last=" Nowak" data-first=" Ronald M." data-title=" Walkers Mammals of the World, 6th edition" data-publisher=" Johns Hopkins University Press" data-year=" 1999" data-isbn=" 0-8018-5789-9"></span></ref> Recent studies applying molecular markers,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="Spotorno, A.E., Mar&#xED;n, J.C., Manr&#xED;quez, G., Valladares, J.P., Rico, E. and Rivas, C. " data-doi="10.1111/j.1469-7998.2006.00117.x" data-year="2006" data-journal="Journal of Zoology" data-pages="060606025751032&#x2013;&#x2013;">Ancient and modern steps during domestication of guinea pigs (Cavia porcellus<strong>L.)</strong></span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-doi="10.1111/j.1439-0469.2009.00561.x" data-year="2010" data-last1="Dunnum" data-first1="Jonathan L." data-last2="Salazar-Bravo" data-first2="Jorge" data-journal="Journal of Zoological Systematics and Evolutionary Research" data-volume="48" data-issue="4" data-pages="376&#x2013;388">Molecular systematics, taxonomy and biogeography of the genus Cavia (Rodentia: Caviidae)</span></ref> in addition to studying the skull and skeletal morphology of current and mummified animals,<ref>Spotorno, A.E., Manr&#xED;quez, G., Fern&#xE1;ndez L., A., Mar&#xED;n, J.C., Gonz&#xE1;lez, F. and Wheeler, J. 2007. <a href="http://books.google.com/books?id=PVIJr-aA-XMC&amp;pg=PA367">Domestication of guinea pigs from a southern Peru-northern Chile wild species and their middle pre-Columbian mummies</a>. pp. 367&#x2013;388 in: Kelt, D.A., Lessa, E.P., Salazar-Bravo, J. and Patton, J.L. (eds.). The Quintessential Naturalist: Honoring the Life and Legacy of Oliver P. Pearson. University of California Publications in Zoology 134:1&#x2013;981 ISBN 0-520-09859-5.</ref> revealed that the ancestor is most likely Cavia tschudii.
The domestic guinea pig plays an important role in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/folk_culture">folk culture</a> of many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas">Indigenous South American</a> groups, especially as a food source, but also in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/traditional_medicine">folk medicine</a> and in community religious ceremonies.<ref name="morales"><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#Morales">Morales</a></ref> Since the 1960s, efforts have been made to increase consumption of the animal outside South America.<ref name="cbs"><span class="reference" data-type="news" data-last=" Vecchio" data-first=" Rick" data-title=" Peru Pushes Guinea Pigs as Food" data-publisher=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" cbs_news"="">CBS News&quot; data-url =&quot; http://www.cbsnews.com/stories/2004/10/19/world/main650148.shtml&quot; data-date =&quot; 2004-10-19&quot; data-accessdate =&quot; 2007-03-12&quot;&gt;</span></ref>
In <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_culture">Western</a> societies, the domestic guinea pig has enjoyed widespread popularity as a household <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pet">pet</a> since its introduction by European traders in the 16th century. Their docile nature and responsiveness to handling and feeding, and the relative ease of caring for them, continue to make the guinea pig a popular pet. Organizations devoted to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_fancy">competitive breeding</a> of guinea pigs have been formed worldwide, and many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea_pig_breed">specialized breeds</a> of guinea pig, with varying coat colors and compositions, are cultivated by breeders.
Biological experimentation on guinea pigs has been carried out since the 17th century. The animals were frequently used as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/model_organism">model organism</a>s in the 19th and 20th centuries, resulting in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/epithet">epithet</a> &quot;guinea pig&quot; for a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human_subject_research">test subject</a>, but have since been largely replaced by other rodents such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_Mouse">mice</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_Rat">rats</a>. They are still used in research, primarily as models for human medical conditions such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/diabetes_mellitus_type_1">juvenile diabetes</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scurvy">scurvy</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pre-eclampsia">pregnancy complications</a>.
:<em>This is about the bird also called the ring dove; for other definitions, see <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ring_dove_(disambiguation)">Ring dove (disambiguation)</a>.</em>
The <strong>Barbary dove</strong>, <strong>ringed turtle dove</strong>, <strong>ringneck dove</strong>, <strong>ring-necked turtle dove</strong>, or <strong>ring dove</strong> (<em>Streptopelia risoria</em>) is a domestic member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dove">dove</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> (Columbidae).
Although the Barbary dove is normally assigned its own systematic name, as <em>Streptopelia risoria</em>, considerable doubt exists as to its appropriate classification. Some sources confidently assert that it is a domestic form of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_collared_dove">Eurasian collared dove</a>, <em>S. decaocto</em>, but the majority of evidence points to it being a domesticated form of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/African_collared_dove">African collared dove</a>, <em>S. roseogrisea</em>. It appears that it can <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybridise</a> freely with either species, and its status as a species must therefore be regarded as doubtful. However because of the wide use of both the common and systematic names, it is best to consider it separately from either of the putative parent species. Their time of domestication is also is uncertain. While Linnaeus described them in 1756, they may have been imported into Italy from North Africa in the late 16th century.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" author="Fisher, James " year="1953 " title="British Birds, number 5, Vol. XLVI"></span></ref>
Barbary doves are easily kept, and long-lived, in captivity, living for up to 12 years. However, There have been cases of doves living over 20 years,<ref><em>Documented Info by a hobbyist at <a href="">http://diamonddove.info/bird11%20Ringneck.htm</a></em></ref> and at one point a dove living up to 29 years<ref>.<em>Documented story at <a href="">http://diamonddove.info/bird11%20Ringneck.htm</a></em></ref> In recent years they have been used extensively in biological research, particularly into the hormonal bases of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reproduction">reproductive behaviour</a>, because their sequences of courtship, mating and parental behaviour have been accurately described and are highly consistent in form. Dove fanciers have bred them in a great variety of colours; the number of colours available has increased dramatically in the latter half of the twentieth century, and it is thought that this has been achieved by interbreeding with <em>S. roseogrisea</em>. Some of these doves carry a mutation that makes them completely white. These white Barbaries are most commonly used in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stage_magic">stage magic</a> acts.  White Barbary doves are traditionally released in large public ceremonies, since it is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/peace_symbol">peace symbol</a> in several cultures, and &quot;dove releases&quot; are also sometimes found at weddings and funerals. However, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/release_dove">release dove</a> is, in fact, usually a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/homing_pigeon">homing pigeon</a>, as Barbary doves lack the homing instinct.
The <em>coo</em> of the Barbary dove is created by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/muscle">muscle</a>s that vibrate air sent up from the doves <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lung">lung</a>s.  These muscles belong to the fastest known class of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vertebrate">vertebrate</a> muscles, contracting as much as ten times faster than muscles vertebrates use for running.  This class of muscles is usually found in high speed tissue such as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rattlesnake">rattlesnakes</a> tail. Barbary doves are the first bird species to have been found to have this class of muscle.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" author="Elemans CP, Spierts IL, Muller UK, Van Leeuwen JL, Goller F " title="Bird song: superfast muscles control dove&apos;s trill " journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" nature_(journal)"_data-nature"="">Nature (journal)&quot; data-Nature =&quot;undefined&quot; data- volume=&quot;431 &quot; data- issue=&quot;7005 &quot; data- year=&quot;2004 &quot; data- pages=&quot;146 &quot; data- doi=&quot;10.1038/431146a &quot; data- pmid=&quot;15356620&quot;&gt;</span></ref>
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feral">Feral</a> populations of Barbary doves establish themselves readily as a result of escapes or releases from captivity, but they will merge with local populations of Eurasian collared doves if they exist. There is a small feral population in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California">Los Angeles</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/California">California</a>, where neither <em>S. decaocto</em> nor <em>S. roseogrisea</em> is currently found.
It has been observed that at least the female can not only coo but emit a high pitched laughing sound.(Muns Baare, July 2010)
