There are many <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/breed">breed</a>s of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Guinea_pig">guinea pig or cavy</a></strong> which have been developed since its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domestication</a> ca. 5000 BC. Breeds vary widely in appearance and purpose, ranging from  show breeds with long, flowing hair to those in use as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/model_organisms">model organisms</a> by science. From ca. 1200 AD to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Inca_Empire">Spanish conquest of the Inca Empire</a> in 1532, selective breeding by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_in_South_America">indigenous South American people</a> resulted in many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/landrace">landrace</a> varieties of domestic guinea pigs, which form the basis for some of the modern, formal breeds.<ref name="walker"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" last=" Nowak " first=" Ronald M. " title=" Walker&apos;s Mammals of the World " edition=" 6th " year=" 1999 " pages=" 1667&#x2013;1669 " isbn=" 0-8018-5789-9"></span></ref>  Early Andean varieties were primarily kept as agricultural stock for food, and efforts at improving the guinea pig as a food source continue to the modern era.
With the export of guinea pigs to Europe in the 15th century, the goal in breeding shifted to focus on the development of appealing pets. To this end, various <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_fancy">competitive breeding</a> organizations were founded by fanciers. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Cavy_Breeders_Association">American Cavy Breeders Association</a>, an adjunct to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Rabbit_Breeders_Association">American Rabbit Breeders Association</a>, is the governing body in the United States and Canada.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" title=" Constitution " url=" http://www.acbaonline.com/constitution.html " publisher=" American Cavy Breeders Association " date=" 2006-09-29 " accessdate=" 2007-03-22"></span></ref> The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Cavy_Council">British Cavy Council</a> governs cavy clubs in the United Kingdom. Similar organizations exist in Australia (Australian National Cavy Council)<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" url=" http://cavycouncil.org.au/ " title=" Official Website " publisher=" Australian National Cavy Council " accessdate=" 2012-11-25"></span></ref> and New Zealand (Combined Cavy Clubs New Zealand). Each club publishes its own Standard of Perfection and determines which breeds are eligible for showing. New breeds continue to emerge in the 21st century.
Though there are many breeds of guinea pig, only a few breeds are commonly found on the show table as pets. Most guinea pigs found as pets were either found undesirable[undesirable for showing or as pets?date=March 2013] by breeders or were bred to be pleasant pets regardless of how well they meet the breed standard of perfection. The English/American Short-haired, the Abyssinian (rough-coated), the Peruvian (long-coated), and the Sheltie (a.k.a. Silkie, long-coated) breeds are those most frequently seen as pets, and the former three are the core breeds in the history of the competitive showing of guinea pigs. In addition to their standard form, nearly all breeds come in a Satin variant. Satins, due to their hollow hair shafts, possess coats of a special gloss and shine. However, there is growing evidence that the genes responsible for the satin coat also can cause severe bone problems, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/osteodystrophy">osteodystrophy</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pagets_disease_of_bone">Pagets disease</a>.<ref>Jordan, Julia.  <a href="http://www.diss.fu-berlin.de/diss/receive/FUDISS_thesis_000000003536">&quot;Untersuchungen zur Osteodystrophia fibrosa beim Hausmeerschweinchen&quot; (<em>Cavia aperea</em> f. <em>porcellus</em>) der Z&#xFC;chtung &#x201A;satin&#x2018;&#x200A;&quot;</a> Investigation of Osteodystrophia Fibrosa in Guinea Pigs (Cavia aperea<strong>f. </strong>porcellus<em>) the &quot;Satin&quot; phenotype</em>.  Dissertation, Freie Universit&#xE4;t Berlin, 2006.  ISBN 978-3-86664-349-9</ref> Showing satin variations is prohibited by some cavy breeders&apos; associations because of animal welfare reasons.
All cavy breeds have some shared general standards: the head profile should be rounded, with large eyes and large, smooth ears. The body should be strong and of compact build. Coat colour should in all variations be clearly defined and thorough from root to tip. These standards are best met by long established, commonly bred breeds, as their breeders have had enough time and animals to effectively breed for these qualities. The coat colour ideal of good definition and thoroughness is rarely met by other than the smooth-coated breeds, which have had well established, separate breeding lines for different colours.
The <strong>cheetah</strong> (<em>Acinonyx jubatus</em>) is a big cat in the subfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felinae">Felinae</a> that inhabits most of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a> and parts of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iran">Iran</a>. It is the only <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant member</a> of the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acinonyx">Acinonyx</a></em>. The cheetah can run as fast as [109.4to120.7km/hmphabbr=on], <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fastest_animals">faster</a> than any other land animal.(ref: Sharp1997)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Sharp" data-first="N. C. " data-year="1997 " data-url="" data-journal="Journal of Zoology, London " data-volume="241 " data-issue="3 " data-pages="493&#x2013;494" data-doi="10.1111/j.1469-7998.1997.tb04840.x">Timed running speed of a cheetah (<em>Acinonyx jubatus</em>) </span>(ref: cbn)<span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-author="Hildebrand, M. " data-year="1959 " data-journal="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" journal_of_mammalogy"="">Journal of Mammalogy &quot; data-jstor=&quot;1376265&quot; data-pages=&quot;481&#x2013;495 &quot; data-volume=&quot;40&quot; data-issue=&quot;4&quot; data-doi=&quot;10.2307/1376265 &quot;&gt;Motions of Cheetah and Horse </span> [date=September 2010bot=H3llBot] Although according to Cheetah, Luke Hunter and Dave Hamman, (Struik Publishers, 2003), pp. 37&#x2013;38, the cheetah&apos;s fastest recorded speed was [110km/habbr=on].<ref name="carwardine08"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last="Carwardine" data-first="M. " data-year="2008 " data-publisher="Sterling " data-location="New York" data-isbn="978-1-4027-5623-8" data-page="43">Animal Records </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last="Sears" data-first="E. S." data-year="2001" data-publisher="McFarland" data-location="Jefferson, North Carolina" data-isbn="978-0-7864-0971-6" data-page="5">Running Through the Ages</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="news" data-url="http://news.nationalgeographic.com/news/2012/08/120802-cheetah-sarah-cincinnati-zoo-fastest-record-science-usain-bolt-olympics/" data-newspaper="National Geographic Daily News" data-publisher="National Geographic Society" data-last="Smith" data-first="R. " data-year="2012">Cheetah Breaks Speed Record&#x2014;Beats Usain Bolt by Seconds</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.bbc.co.uk/nature/collections/p00hldcc#p00715gc" data-work="Nature Video Collections" data-publisher="BBC Nature">Speed sensation</span></ref> It covers distances up to [500mft0abbr=on] in short bursts, and can accelerate from [0to96km/hmphabbr=on] in three seconds.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.zsl.org/zsl-whipsnade-zoo/exhibits/cheetah-rock/cheetah-facts,858,ar.html" data-publisher="Zoological Society of London" data-accessdate="5 June 2013">Cheetah fast facts</span></ref>
The cheetah is a unique felid, with its closest living relatives being the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cougar">puma</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/jaguarundi">jaguarundi</a> of the Americas. This cat is notable for modifications in the species&apos; paws, being one of the few felids with only semi-<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/retractable_claw">retractable claw</a>s.(ref: factsheet)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://new.cheetah.org/site/wp-content/uploads/2014/02/FactCHEETAH_Sept2013_PT_final.pdf " data-publisher="Cheetah.org " data-date=" " data-accessdate="3 June 2013">Cheetah Fact Sheet </span>
Its main hunting strategy is to trip swift prey such as various <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antelope">antelope</a> species and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hare">hare</a>s with its dewclaw. Almost every facet of the cheetah&apos;s anatomy has evolved to maximise its success in the chase, the result of an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/evolutionary_arms_race">evolutionary arms race</a> with its prey. Due to this specialisation, however, the cheetah is poorly equipped to defend itself against other large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s, with speed being its main means of defence.
In the wild, the cheetah is a prolific breeder, with up to nine cubs in a litter. The majority of cubs do not survive to adulthood, mainly as a result of depredation from other predators. The rate of cub mortality varies from area to area, from 50% to 75%, and in extreme cases such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serengeti">Serengeti</a> ecosystem, up to 90%. Cheetahs are notoriously poor breeders in captivity, though several organizations, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/De_Wildt_Cheetah_and_Wildlife_Centre">De Wildt Cheetah and Wildlife Centre</a>, have succeeded in breeding high numbers of cubs.
The cheetah is listed as vulnerable, facing various threats including: loss of habitat and prey; conflict with humans; the illegal pet trade; competition with and predation by other carnivores; and a gene pool with very low variability. It is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/charismatic_species">charismatic species</a> and many captive cats are &quot;ambassadors&quot; for their species and wildlife conservation in general.
The <strong>domestic dog</strong> (<em>Canis lupus familiaris</em> or <em>Canis familiaris</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domestication">domesticated</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canidae">canid</a> which has been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/selective_breeding">selectively bred</a> for millennia for various behaviors, sensory capabilities, and physical attributes.(ref: ADW)
Although initially thought to have originated as a manmade variant of an extant canid species (variously supposed as being the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dhole">dhole</a>,<ref>Hodgson, B. H. (1833). Description and Characters of the Wild Dog of Nepal (<em>Canis prim&#xE6;vus</em>), <em>Asiatic Researches</em>, Vol. XVIII, Pt. 2, pp. 221&#x2013;37</ref> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/golden_jackal">golden jackal</a>,<ref>Lorenz, Konrad (2002). <em>Man meets dog.</em> Routledge, ISBN 0-415-26744-7</ref> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gray_wolf">gray wolf</a><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Wayne " first1=" Robert K. " year=" 1993 " title=" Molecular evolution of the dog family " journal=" Trends in Genetics " volume=" 9 " issue=" 6" pages=" 218&#x2013;224 " doi=" 10.1016/0168-9525(93)90122-X " pmid="8337763"></span></ref>), extensive genetic studies undertaken during the 2010s indicate that dogs diverged from an extinct <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canidae#Phylogenetic_relationships">wolf-like canid</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a> 40,000 years ago.(ref: skoglund2015)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Domestication_of_the_dog">the oldest domesticated animals</a>, their long association with people has allowed dogs to be uniquely attuned to human behavior, as well as thrive on a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/starch">starch</a>-rich diet which would be inadequate for other canid species.<ref name="axelssonetal2013"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last1=" Axelsson " first1=" E. " last2=" Ratnakumar " first2=" A. " last3=" Arendt " first3=" M. L. " last4=" Maqbool " first4=" K. " last5=" Webster " first5=" M. T. " last6=" Perloski " first6=" M. " last7=" Liberg " first7=" O. " last8=" Arnemo " first8=" J. M. " last9=" Hedhammar " first9=" &#xC5;. " last10=" Lindblad-Toh " first10=" K. " title=" The genomic signature of dog domestication reveals adaptation to a starch-rich diet " doi=" 10.1038/nature11837 " journal=" Nature " volume=" 495 " issue=" 7441 " pages=" 360&#x2013;364 " year=" 2013 " pmid="  23354050" pmc=" " data-bibcode=" 2013Natur.495..360A "></span></ref>
Dogs perform many roles for people, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hunting_dog">hunting</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/herding_dog">herding</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sled_dog">pulling loads</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/guard_dog">protection</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/police_dog">assisting police</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dogs_in_warfare">military</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pet">companionship</a>, and, more recently, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/service_dog">aiding handicapped individuals</a>. This impact on human society has given them the nickname &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mans_best_friend_(phrase)">mans best friend</a>&quot; in the Western world. In some cultures, however, dogs <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#As_a_food_source">are a source of meat</a>.(ref: Wingfield-Hayes)<span class="reference" data-type="news" data-="" url="http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/programmes/from_our_own_correspondent/2074073.stm " work="BBC News " first="Rupert " last="Wingfield-Hayes " title="Chinas taste for the exotic " date="29 June 2002"></span>(ref: Vietnams dog meat tradition)<span class="reference" data-type="news" data-="" url="http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/asia-pacific/1735647.stm " work="BBC News " title="Vietnam&apos;s dog meat tradition " date="31 December 2001"></span>
The <strong>barracuda</strong> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ray-finned_fish">ray-finned fish</a> known for its large size and fearsome appearance. Its body is long, fairly compressed, and covered with small, smooth <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scale_(zoology)">scales</a>. Some species can reach up to [2.1mftabbr=on] in length and [30cminabbr=on]  in width.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-authors="Strege, D." data-url="http://www.grindtv.com/outdoor/excursions/post/river-monster-barracuda-is-nearly-7-feet-long-102-pounds/" data-publisher="GrindTV" data-accessdate="2013-04-03">Monster barracuda is nearly 7-feet long, 102 pounds</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-="" authors="Humann, P. &amp; Deloach, N. " data-date="2002 " title="Reef Fish Identification, Florida, Caribbean, Bahamas, 3rd edition " page="64 " publisher="New World Publications, Inc. " location="Jacksonville, Florida, USA " isbn="1-878348-30-2"></span></ref> The barracuda is a saltwater <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> of the genus <strong><em>Sphyraena</em></strong>, the only genus in the family Sphyraenidae, and is found in tropical and subtropical oceans worldwide ranging from the Eastern border of the Atlantic Ocean to the Red Sea and Caribbean Sea. They are found near the top of the water and near coral reefs and sea grasses.(ref: pawnation1)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-authors="Millburn, N." data-url="http://animals.pawnation.com/great-barracudas-diet-3678.html " data-publisher="Animals - PawNation ">The Great Barracuda&apos;s Diet </span>
A <strong>dragonfly</strong> is an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a> belonging to the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odonata">Odonata</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/suborder">suborder</a> <strong>Anisoptera</strong> (from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_language">Greek</a> &#x3B1;&#x3BD;&#x3B9;&#x3C3;&#x3BF;&#x3C2; <em>anisos</em> &quot;uneven&quot; + &#x3C0;&#x3C4;&#x3B5;&#x3C1;&#x3BF;&#x3C2; <em>pteros</em>, &quot;wings&quot;, because the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hindwing">hindwing</a> is broader than the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forewing">forewing</a>). Adult dragonflies are characterized by large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/compound_eye">multifaceted eyes</a>, two pairs of strong transparent <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect_wing">wings</a>, sometimes with coloured patches and an elongated body. Dragonflies can be mistaken for the related group, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Damselfly">damselflies</a> (Zygoptera), which are similar in structure, though usually lighter in build; however, the wings of most dragonflies are held flat and away from the body, while damselflies hold the wings folded at rest, along or above the abdomen. Dragonflies are agile fliers, while damselflies have a weaker, fluttery flight. Many dragonflies have brilliant iridescent or metallic colours produced by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/structural_coloration">structural coloration</a>, making them conspicuous in flight. An adult dragonfly eye has nearly 24,000 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ommatidium">ommatidia</a>.
Fossils of very large dragonfly ancestors in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protodonata">Protodonata</a> are found from 325&#xA0;million years ago (Mya) in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Carboniferous">Upper Carboniferous</a> rocks; these had wingspans up to about [750mminabbr=on]. About [3000 species] of Anisoptera are in the world today. Most are tropical, with fewer species in temperate regions.
Dragonflies are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>s, both in their aquatic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">larva</a>l stage, when they are known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nymph_(biology)">nymphs</a> or naiads, and as adults. Several years of their lives are spent as nymphs living in fresh water; the adults may be on the wing for just a few days or weeks. They are fast, agile fliers, sometimes migrating across oceans, and are often found near water. They have a uniquely complex mode of reproduction involving indirect insemination, delayed fertilization, and sperm competition. During mating, the male grasps the female at the back of the head or on the prothorax, and the female curls her abdomen under her body to pick up sperm from the male&apos;s secondary genitalia at the front of his abdomen, forming the &quot;heart&quot; or &quot;wheel&quot; posture.
Loss of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wetland">wetland</a> habitat threatens dragonfly populations around the world. Dragonflies are represented in human culture on artefacts such as pottery, rock paintings, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Art_Nouveau">Art Nouveau</a> jewellery. They are used in traditional medicine in Japan and China, and caught for food in Indonesia. They are symbols of courage, strength, and happiness in Japan, but seen as sinister in European folklore. Their bright colours and agile flight are admired in the poetry of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson">Alfred, Lord Tennyson</a> and the prose of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._E._Bates">H. E. Bates</a>.
The <strong>Galliformes</strong> are an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> of heavy-bodied ground-feeding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/turkey_(bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grouse">grouse</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_quail">New World quail</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_quail">Old World quail</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagopus">ptarmigan</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/partridge">partridge</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pheasant">pheasant</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/junglefowl">junglefowl</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a>.  The name derives from &quot;gallus&quot;, Latin for &quot;cock&quot; or &quot;rooster&quot;. Common names are <strong>gamefowl</strong> or <strong>gamebirds</strong>, <strong>landfowl</strong>, <strong>gallinaceous birds</strong>, or <strong>galliforms</strong>. &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfowl">Wildfowl</a>&quot; or just &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fowl">fowl</a>&quot; are also often used for the Galliformes, but usually these terms also refer to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/waterfowl">waterfowl</a> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anseriformes">Anseriformes</a>), and occasionally to other commonly hunted birds.  This group has about 290 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, one or more of which are found in essentially every part of the world&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continent">continent</a>s (except for the innermost <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a>s and perpetual <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ice">ice</a>).  They are rarer on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/island">island</a>s, and in contrast to the closely related waterfowl, are essentially absent from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ocean">ocean</a>ic islands&#x2014;unless introduced there by humans.  Several species have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> during their long and extensive relationships with humans.
This order contains five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">families</a>: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a> (including chicken, quail, partridges, pheasants, turkeys, peafowl and grouse), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odontophoridae">Odontophoridae</a> (New World quails), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numididae">Numididae</a> (guineafowl), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a> (including chachalacas and curassows), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megapodiidae">Megapodiidae</a> (incubator birds like mallee fowl and brush-turkeys). They are important as seed dispersers and predators in the ecosystems they inhabit, and are often reared as game birds by humans for their meat and eggs and for recreational hunting.  Many gallinaceous species are skilled runners and escape predators by running rather than flying.  Males of most species are more colorful than the females.  Males often have elaborate courtship behaviors that include strutting, fluffing of tail or head feathers, and vocal sounds. They are mainly nonmigratory.
The <strong>Galliformes</strong> are an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> of heavy-bodied ground-feeding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s that includes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/turkey_(bird)">turkey</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grouse">grouse</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_quail">New World quail</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_quail">Old World quail</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagopus">ptarmigan</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/partridge">partridge</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pheasant">pheasant</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/junglefowl">junglefowl</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a>.  The name derives from &quot;gallus&quot;, Latin for &quot;cock&quot; or &quot;rooster&quot;. Common names are <strong>gamefowl</strong> or <strong>gamebirds</strong>, <strong>landfowl</strong>, <strong>gallinaceous birds</strong>, or <strong>galliforms</strong>. &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wildfowl">Wildfowl</a>&quot; or just &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fowl">fowl</a>&quot; are also often used for the Galliformes, but usually these terms also refer to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/waterfowl">waterfowl</a> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anseriformes">Anseriformes</a>), and occasionally to other commonly hunted birds.  This group has about 290 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, one or more of which are found in essentially every part of the world&apos;s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continent">continent</a>s (except for the innermost <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a>s and perpetual <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ice">ice</a>).  They are rarer on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/island">island</a>s, and in contrast to the closely related waterfowl, are essentially absent from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ocean">ocean</a>ic islands&#x2014;unless introduced there by humans.  Several species have been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/domesticated">domesticated</a> during their long and extensive relationships with humans.
This order contains five <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">families</a>: <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a> (including chicken, quail, partridges, pheasants, turkeys, peafowl and grouse), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odontophoridae">Odontophoridae</a> (New World quails), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numididae">Numididae</a> (guineafowl), <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cracidae">Cracidae</a> (including chachalacas and curassows), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Megapodiidae">Megapodiidae</a> (incubator birds like mallee fowl and brush-turkeys). They are important as seed dispersers and predators in the ecosystems they inhabit, and are often reared as game birds by humans for their meat and eggs and for recreational hunting.  Many gallinaceous species are skilled runners and escape predators by running rather than flying.  Males of most species are more colorful than the females.  Males often have elaborate courtship behaviors that include strutting, fluffing of tail or head feathers, and vocal sounds. They are mainly nonmigratory.
The <strong>guanaco</strong> (<em>Lama guanicoe</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camelid">camelid</a> native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a> that stands between [1.0and1.2mftinabbr=on] at the shoulder<ref name="stahl08"><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-="" publisher=" Springer" isbn=" 9780387752280" pages=" 121&#x2013;130" editor1-first=" Helaine" data-editor1-last=" Silverman" editor2-last=" Isbell" editor2-first=" William" first=" Peter W." data-last="Stahl" title=" Handbook of South American Archaeology" chapter=" Animal Domestication in South America" date=" 4 April 2008"></span></ref> and weighs about 90&#xA0;kg (200&#xA0;lb). The colour varies very little (unlike the domestic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/llama">llama</a>), ranging from a light brown to dark cinnamon and shading to white underneath. Guanacos have grey faces and small, straight ears. The name guanaco comes from the South American language <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quechua_language">Quechua</a> word <em>huanaco</em><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.americazoo.com/goto/index/mammals/358.htm " data-archiveurl="http://web.archive.org/web/20090428114046/http://www.americazoo.com/goto/index/mammals/358.htm" data-archivedate="28 April 2009" data-publisher="Lesley Fountain" data-work="America Zoo">Guanaco &#x2013; LAMA GUANICOE</span></ref> (modern spelling, wanaku [date=July 2015]). Young guanacos are called chulengos.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.conservacionpatagonica.org/blog/2011/12/22/species-profile-guanacos/" data-publisher="Concervaci&#xF3;n Patagonia">Species Profile: Guanaco</span></ref>
The <strong>American robin</strong> (<em>Turdus migratorius</em>) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_migration">migratory</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/songbird">songbird</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thrush_(bird)">thrush</a> family. It is named after the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_robin">European robin</a><ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" last1="McCrum " first1="Robert " data-last2="Cran " data-first2="William " data-last3="MacNeil " data-first3="Robert " data-publisher="Faber and Faber " data-page="123 " data-year="1992 " data-isbn="0-571-16443-9">The Story of English </span></ref> because of its reddish-orange breast, though the two species are not closely related, with the European robin belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_flycatcher">Old World flycatcher</a> family. The American robin is widely distributed throughout North America, wintering from southern Canada to central <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a> and along the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific</a> Coast. It is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_birds">state bird</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michigan">Michigan</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>.(ref: 50 States)<span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.50states.com/bird/statelst.htm " data-author="50 States" data-accessdate="25 July 2007">Official US State Birds</span> According to some sources, the American robin ranks behind only the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red-winged_blackbird">red-winged blackbird</a> (and just ahead of the introduced <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_starling">European starling</a> and the not-always-naturally occurring <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/house_finch">house finch</a>) as the most abundant extant land bird in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>.<ref>McWilliams, Gerald M. &amp; Brauning, Daniel W., <em>The Birds of Pennsylvania</em>. Comstock Publishing Associates (2000), ISBN 0801436435.</ref> It has seven subspecies, but only <em>T. m. confinis</em> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baja_California_Sur">Baja California Sur</a> is particularly distinctive, with pale gray-brown underparts.
The American robin is active mostly during the day and assembles in large flocks at night. Its diet consists of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/invertebrate">invertebrate</a>s (such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beetle">beetle</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/larva">grubs</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/earthworm">earthworm</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/caterpillar">caterpillar</a>s), fruits, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/berry">berries</a>. It is one of the earliest bird species to lay eggs, beginning to breed shortly after returning to its summer range from its winter range. Its nest consists of long coarse grass, twigs, paper, and feathers, and is smeared with mud and often cushioned with grass or other soft materials. It is among the first birds to sing at dawn, and its song consists of several discrete units that are repeated.
The adult robin is preyed upon by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hawk">hawk</a>s, cats, and larger snakes, but when feeding in flocks, it can be vigilant and watch other birds for reactions to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predation">predators</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown-headed_cowbird">Brown-headed cowbird</a>s lay eggs in robin nests (see <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/brood_parasite">brood parasite</a>), but robins usually reject the cowbird eggs.
<strong>Monitor lizard</strong> is the common name of several large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lizard">lizard</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>, comprising the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Varanus</em></strong>. They are native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceania">Oceania</a>, but are now found also in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a> as an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/invasive_species">invasive species</a>.
Monitor lizards have long necks, powerful <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tail">tail</a>s and claws, and well-developed limbs. The adult length of extant species ranges from [20cminabbr=on] in some species, to over [3mftabbr=on0] in the case of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Komodo_dragon">Komodo dragon</a>, though the extinct varanid known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/megalania">megalania</a> (<em>Varanus priscus</em>) may have been capable of reaching lengths of as much as [7mftabbr=on0]. Most monitor species are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cursorial">terrestrial</a>, but <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal">arboreal</a> and semiaquatic monitors are also known. While most monitor lizards are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivorous">carnivorous</a>, eating eggs, smaller reptiles, fish, birds and small mammals, some also eat fruit and vegetation, depending on where they live.<ref name="EoR"><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-editor="Cogger, H.G. &amp; Zweifel, R.G. " data-author=" Bauer, Aaron M. " data-year="1998 " data-publisher=" Academic Press " data-location="San Diego " data-pages=" 157&#x2013;159 " data-isbn=" 0-12-178560-2">Encyclopedia of Reptiles and Amphibians </span></ref>
A total of 78 species are currently recognized; however, given that several species-groups are in need of taxonomic review, this number is certain to be increased with future research.
<strong>Clownfish</strong> or <strong>anemonefish</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a>es from the subfamily <strong>Amphiprioninae</strong> in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pomacentridae">Pomacentridae</a>. Thirty <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> are recognized: one in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maroon_clownfish">Premnas</a></em>, while the remaining are in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#Taxonomy">Amphiprion</a></em>. In the wild, they all form <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/symbiosis">symbiotic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)">mutualism</a>s with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sea_anemone">sea anemone</a>s. Depending on species, anemonefish are overall yellow, orange, or a reddish or blackish color, and many show white bars or patches. The largest can reach a length of [18cmin], while the smallest barely achieve [10cmin].
&quot;<strong>Clam</strong>&quot; is an informal term used to refer to any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mollusca">mollusca</a>ns within <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/class_(biology)">Class</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bivalvia">Bivalvia</a>.
Remains first appeared in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cambrian">Cambrian</a> age rocks 510 million years ago.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://geology.about.com/od/fossilstimeevolution/ig/fossil-pictures/fossil-bivalve.htm" data-author="Andrew Alden" data-work="About.com Education">Bivalves</span></ref> They presently live in both freshwater and marine habitats, and range in adult size from nearly microscopic to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/giant_clam">giant clam</a>, which can weigh [200kglbabbr=on].  Some have life cycles of only one year, while at least one has been discovered that may be over 500 years old.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.cbsnews.com/8301-205_162-57612409/ming-the-clam-worlds-oldest-animal-was-actually-507-years-old/" data-publisher="CBS News" data-author="Danielle Elliot" data-date="November 14, 2013" data-accessdate="15 November 2013">Ming the Clam, World&apos;s Oldest Animal, Was Actually 507 Years Old</span></ref>  They lack heads but most can react to changes in light and some, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scallop">scallop</a>s, have rudimentary eyes.  Though a common food item, many are too small to be useful as food, and not all species are considered <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/palatability">palatable</a>.  All clams have two calcareous shells or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/valve_(mollusc)">valves</a> joined near a hinge structure with a flexible ligament, and all are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/filter_feeder">filter feeder</a>s.
 | accessdate = 2014-11-01 | doi=10.1007/bf01014110}} This <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/musk">musky odor</a> is used to attract females during <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mating_season">mating season</a>. Muskoxen primarily live in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northern_Canada">Canadian Arctic</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenland">Greenland</a>,<ref><a href="http://www.greenland-guide.gl/animal_life.htm">Animal Life in Greenland &#x2013; an introduction by the tourist board</a>. Greenland-guide.gl. Retrieved on 2011-09-15.</ref> with small introduced populations in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norway">Norway</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alaska">Alaska</a>.
<strong>A</strong> <strong>kite</strong> is a tethered object with surfaces that react with a fluid (water, air, gas, plasma) to create lift and drag; often the result is flying in air or water of the wing parts of a kite. A kite consists of wings, tethers, and anchors. A kite may be with fixed or moving anchors. There are natural and synthetic kites. Kites have a long and varied history and there are many types of kites that are flown individually and at festivals world wide.
The necessary <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lift_(force)">lift</a> that sustains the kite in flight is generated when air flows above the kites surface, producing low pressure above and high pressure below the wings.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.grc.nasa.gov/WWW/K-12/airplane/guided.htm " data-publisher="NASA " data-accessdate="2012-10-03">Beginners Guide to Aeronautics </span></ref> The interaction with the wind also generates horizontal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(physics)">drag</a> along the direction of the wind. The resultant force vector from the lift and drag force components is opposed by the tension of one or more of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rope">lines</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tether">tether</a>s to which the kite is attached.<ref><a href="http://www.kitelife.com/magazine/issue39/altitude39/content.php">Flying High, Down Under</a></ref> The anchor point of the kite line may be static or moving (e.g., the towing of a kite by a running person, boat, free-falling anchors as in paragliders and fugitive parakites<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Woglom, Gilbert Totten " data-url="http://openlibrary.org/books/OL6980132M/Parakites " data-publisher="Putnam" data-year="1896 " data-oclc="2273288 ">Parakites: A treatise on the making and flying of tailless kites for scientific purposes and for recreation </span></ref><ref><a href="http://cires.colorado.edu/science/field/kites/"><em>Science in the Field</em>: Ben Balsley, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CIRES">CIRES</a> Scientist in the Field Gathering atmospheric dynamics data using kites. Kites are anchored to boats on the Amazon River employed to sample levels of certain gases in the air.</a></ref> or vehicle).<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://uboat.net/technical/bachstelze.htm " data-publisher="Uboat.net " data-date=" " data-accessdate="2012-10-03">The Bachstelze Article describes the Fa-330 Rotary Wing Kite towed by its mooring to the submarine. The kite was a man-lifter modeled after the autogyro principle </span></ref><ref><a href="http://www.drachen.org/journals/journal22/Journal-22/TalStreet.pdf">Kite Fashions: Above, Below, Sideways. Expert kite fliers sometimes tie a flying kite to a tree to have the kite fly for days on end.</a> [url=http://www.drachen.org/journals/journal22/Journal-22/TalStreet.pdf date=20110723191633 ]</ref>
The same principles can be used in water<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://2lo.de/kitedive/index.htm " data-publisher="2lo.de " data-date=" " data-accessdate="2012-10-03">Underwater kiting </span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.google.com/patents?id" data-publisher="Google.com " data-date=" " data-accessdate="2012-10-03">Hydro kite angling device Jason C. Hubbart </span></ref> and experiments have also been made with lighter than air tethered balloons (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kytoon">kytoon</a>s)<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Streeter " data-first="Tal " data-url="http://drupal.drachen.org/sites/default/files/pdf/Journal%20Issue%2010.pdf " data-format="PDF " data-journal="Drachen Foundation Journal " data-date="Fall 2002 " data-issue="10 " data-accessdate="2012-10-03 " data-pages="41&#x2013;44">Domina Jalbert: Brother of the Wind </span></ref>
Kites may be flown for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/recreation">recreation</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/art">art</a> or other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#Practical_uses">practical</a> uses. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sport_kites">Sport kites</a> can be flown in aerial <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ballet">ballet</a>, sometimes as part of a competition. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_kite">Power kite</a>s are multi-line steerable kites designed to generate large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/force">force</a>s which can be used to power activities such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kite_surfing">kite surfing</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kite_landboarding">kite landboarding</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kite_fishing">kite fishing</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kite_buggying">kite buggying</a> and a new trend <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snow_kiting">snow kiting</a>.
<strong>Junglefowl</strong> are the four living <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a> from the genus <strong><em>Gallus</em></strong> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gallinaceous">Gallinaceous</a> bird order, which occur in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India">India</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.
These are large birds, with colourful male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feather">plumage</a>, but are nevertheless difficult to see in the dense vegetation they inhabit.
As with many birds in the pheasant family, the male takes no part in the incubation of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egg_(biology)">egg</a> or rearing of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/precocial">precocial</a> young. These duties are performed by the drab and well-<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/camouflage">camouflage</a>d female.
The junglefowl are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seed">seed</a>-eaters, but <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s are also taken, particularly by the young birds.
One of the species in this genus, the red junglefowl, is of historical importance as the likely ancestor of the domesticated <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/chicken">chicken</a>, although it has been suggested the grey junglefowl was also involved.<ref>Eriksson J, Larson G, Gunnarsson U, Bedhom B, Tixier-Boichard M, et al. (2008) Identification of the Yellow Skin Gene Reveals a Hybrid Origin of the Domestic Chicken. PLoS Genet January 23, 2008 <a href="">http://genetics.plosjournals.org/perlserv/?request=get-document&amp;doi=10.1371%2Fjournal.pgen.1000010.eor&amp;ct=1</a>.</ref>
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lankan_junglefowl">Sri Lankan junglefowl</a> is the national bird of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sri_Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>.
The <strong>Coccinellidae</strong> ([,k&#x252;ks&#x26A;n&#x25B;l&#x26A;da&#x26A;])(ref: OED)<span class="reference" data-type="encyclopedia " data-="" title="coccinellid " encyclopedia="The New Oxford Dictionary of English " publisher="Clarendon Press " accessdate="22 February 2015 " year="1998 " location="Oxford " isbn="019861263X " quote="coccinellid/,k&#x252;ks&#x26A;n&#x25B;l&#x26A;d/ &lt;strong&gt;noun&lt;/strong&gt; a beetle of a family (coccinelidae) that includes the ladybirds " page="351"></span> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> of small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beetle">beetle</a>s, ranging from 0.8 to 18&#xA0;mm (0.0315 to 0.708&#xA0;inches).(ref: Seago)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" title="Phylogeny, classification and evolution of ladybird beetles (Coleoptera: Coccinellidae) based on simultaneous analysis of molecular and morphological data " journal="Molecular Phylogenetics and Evolution " data-date="July 2011  " volume="60 " issue="1 " pages="137&#x2013;151 " doi="10.1016/j.ympev.2011.03.015 " last1="Seago " last2="Giorgi " last3="Li " last4="&#x15A;lipi&#x144;skia " first1="A. E. " first2="J. A. " first3="J. " first4="A."></span> They are commonly yellow, orange, or scarlet with small black spots on their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/elytra">wing covers</a>, with black legs, heads and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antenna_(biology)">antennae</a>. Such colour patterns vary greatly, however; for example, a minority of species, such as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vibidia_duodecimguttata">Vibidia duodecimguttata</a></em>, a twelve-spotted species, have whitish spots on a brown background. Coccinellids are found worldwide, with over 5,000 species described.<ref>Judy Allen &amp; Tudor Humphries (2000). <em>Are You A Ladybug?</em>, Kingfisher, p. 30</ref>
Coccinellidae are known colloquially as <strong>ladybirds</strong> (in Britain, Ireland, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations">Commonwealth</a>, and some parts of the southern United States), <strong>ladybugs</strong> (originating in North America) or <strong>lady cows</strong>, among other names.<ref><a href="http://dictionary.die.net/lady%20cow">Definition of lady cow</a>, Websters Revised Unabridged Dictionary (1913), provided by die.net. Retrieved 14 November 2008.</ref> When they need to use a common name, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entomology">entomologists</a> in the United States widely prefer the names ladybird beetlesor lady beetles as these insects are not <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hemiptera">true bugs</a>.
The Coccinellidae are generally considered useful insects, because many species feed on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aphid">aphid</a>s or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scale_insect">scale insect</a>s, which are pests in gardens, agricultural fields, orchards, and similar places. Within the colonies of such plant-eating pests, they will lay hundreds of eggs, and when these hatch, the larvae will commence feeding immediately. However, some species do have unwelcome effects; among these, the most prominent are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epilachninae">Epilachninae</a>, which are plant eaters. Usually, Epilachninae are only mild agricultural pests, eating the leaves of grain, potatoes, beans, and various other crops, but their numbers can increase explosively in years when their natural enemies, such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasitoid">parasitoid</a> wasps that attack their eggs, are few. In such situations, they can do major crop damage. They occur in practically all the major crop-producing regions of temperate and tropical countries.
<strong>Lampreys</strong> (sometimes also called <strong>lamprey eels</strong>) are any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Agnatha">jawless</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> Petromyzontiformes.  The adult lamprey may be characterized by a toothed, funnel-like sucking mouth. The common name &quot;lamprey&quot; is probably derived from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin">Latin</a> <em>lampetra</em>, which may mean &quot;stone licker&quot; (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:lambere#Latin">lambere</a></em> &quot;to lick&quot; + <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/:wikt:petra#Latin">petra</a></em> &quot;stone&quot;), though the etymology is uncertain.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" oxford_university_press"="">Oxford University Press&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;February 12, 2014&quot; data-url=&quot;http://www.oed.com/view/Entry/105386&quot;&gt;lamprey</span></ref>
Currently there are about 38 known extant species of lampreys.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last1="DOCKER" data-first1="Margaret F" data-journal="Guelph Ichthyology Reviews" data-date="2006" data-volume="7" data-url="http://gir.uoguelph.ca/article/view/61/127" data-accessdate="12 Jun 2014">Bill Beamish&#x2019;s Contributions to Lamprey Research and Recent Advances in the Field.</span></ref> Although they are well known for boring into the flesh of other fish to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hematophagy">suck their blood</a>, in fact only a minority do so;<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-last1="Hardisty " data-first1="M. W. " data-last2="Potter " data-first2="I. C. " data-editor1-last="Hardisty " data-editor1-first="M. W. " data-editor2-last="Potter " data-editor2-first="I. C." data-year="1971 " data-edition="1 " data-publisher="Academic Press " data-isbn="9780123248015">The Biology of Lampreys </span></ref> only 18 species of lampreys are actually parasitic.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last1="Gill" data-first1="Howard S." data-last2="Renaud" data-first2="Claude B." data-last3="Chapleau" data-first3="Fran&#xE7;ois" data-last4="Mayden" data-first4="Richard L." data-last5="Potter" data-first5="Ian C." data-last6="Douglas" data-first6="M. E." data-journal="Copeia" data-volume="2003" data-issue="4" data-pages="687&#x2013;703" data-doi="10.1643/IA02-085.1">Phylogeny of Living Parasitic Lampreys (Petromyzontiformes) Based on Morphological Data</span></ref> The lampreys are a very ancient lineage of vertebrates, though their exact relationship to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hagfish">hagfish</a>es and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gnathostomata">jawed vertebrate</a>s is still a matter of dispute.
<strong>Harrier</strong> can mean:
<strong>Gibbons</strong> ([&#x2C8;&#x261;&#x26A;b&#x259;nz]) are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ape">ape</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Hylobatidae</strong> [&#x2CC;ha&#x26A;l&#x275;&#x2C8;be&#x26A;t&#x268;di&#x2D0;]. The family historically contained one genus, but now is split into four genera and 17 species. Gibbons occur in tropical and subtropical rainforests from eastern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and northeast <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India">India</a> to southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China">China</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia">Indonesia</a> (including the islands of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Borneo">Borneo</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_(island)">Java</a>).
Also called the <strong>lesser apes</strong>, gibbons differ from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/great_ape">great ape</a>s (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bonobo">bonobo</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gorilla">gorilla</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/orangutan">orangutan</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>s) in being smaller, exhibiting low <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexual_dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, in not making nests, and in certain anatomical details in which they superficially more closely resemble <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monkey">monkey</a>s than great apes do, but like all apes, gibbons are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prehensile_tail">tail</a>less. Gibbons also display <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pair_bond">pair-bonding</a>, maintaining the same mate for life, unlike most of the great apes (this has been disputed by Palombit and others, who have found that gibbons might be socially monogamous, with occasional &quot;divorce&quot;, but not sexually monogamous<ref>Reichard, U. (1995) &#x201C;Extra-pair copulations in a monogamous gibbon (Hylobates lar).&#x201D; Ethology, vol. 100, no2, pp.&#xA0;99&#x2013;112</ref>
Depending on species and sex, gibbons&apos; fur coloration varies from dark to light brown shades, and any shade between black and white. Seeing a completely &quot;white&quot; gibbon is rare.
Gibbon species include the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/siamang">siamang</a>, the white-handed or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lar_gibbon">lar gibbon</a>, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hoolock_gibbon">hoolock gibbon</a>s.
<strong>Crayfish</strong>, also known as <strong>crawfish</strong>, <strong>crawdads</strong>, <strong>freshwater lobsters</strong>, or <strong>mudbugs</strong>, are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/freshwater">freshwater</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crustacean">crustacean</a>s resembling small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lobster">lobster</a>s, to which they are related; taxonomically, they are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/superfamilies">superfamilies</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astacoidea">Astacoidea</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parastacoidea">Parastacoidea</a>. They breathe through feather-like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill">gill</a>s and are found in bodies of water. Some species are found in brooks and streams where there is fresh water running, while others thrive in swamps, ditches, and rice paddies. Most crayfish cannot tolerate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_pollution">polluted water</a>, although some species such as <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Procambarus_clarkii">Procambarus clarkii</a></em> are hardier. Crayfish feed on living and dead animals and plants.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-author="Christoph Needon, Johannes Petermann, Peter Scheffel &amp; Bernd Scheibe " data-publisher="Urania Verlag " data-location="Leipzig " data-year="1971">Plants and Animals (Pflanzen und Tiere) </span></ref>
<strong>Hares</strong> and <strong>jackrabbits</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leporid">leporid</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Lepus</em></strong>. Hares are classified into the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rabbit">rabbit</a>s and are of similar size, form, and diet as rabbits. They are generally herbivorous, long-eared, and fast runners, and typically live <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solitary_animal">solitarily</a> or in pairs. Hare species are native to Africa, Eurasia, North America, and the Japanese archipelago.
Five leporid species with &quot;hare&quot; in their common names are not considered true hares: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hispid_hare">hispid hare</a> (<em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caprolagus_hispidus">Caprolagus hispidus</a></em>), and four species known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red_rock_hare">red rock hare</a>s (comprising <em>Pronolagus</em>). Meanwhile, jackrabbits are hares rather than rabbits.
A hare less than one year old is called a <strong>leveret</strong>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/collective_noun">collective noun</a> for a group of hares is a &quot;drove&quot;.
<strong>Anglerfishes</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish">fish</a> that are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleostei">teleost</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <strong>Lophiiformes</strong> [&#x2CC;l&#x252;fi&#x2D0;&#x259;&#x2C8;f&#x254;rmi&#x2D0;z].(ref: fishbase) They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteichthyes">bony fish</a> named for their characteristic mode of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predation">predation</a>, in which a fleshy growth from the fish&apos;s head (the esca or illicium) acts as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aggressive_mimicry#Food_as_an_attractant">lure</a>.
Anglerfish are also notable for extreme <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexual_dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> seen in the suborder Ceratioidei, and sexual <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasitism">parasitism</a> of male anglerfish. In these species, males may be several orders of magnitude smaller than females.<ref name="Pietsch"><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Pietsch" data-first="Theodore W." data-journal="Ichthyological Research" data-date="25 August 2005" data-volume="52" data-issue="3" data-pages="207&#x2013;236" data-doi="10.1007/s10228-005-0286-2">Dimorphism, parasitism, and sex revisited: modes of reproduction among deep-sea ceratioid anglerfishes (Teleostei: Lophiiformes)</span></ref> 
Anglerfish occur worldwide. Some are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pelagic_zone">pelagic</a>, while others are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benthic_zone">benthic</a>; some live in the deep sea (e.g., <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceratiidae">Ceratiidae</a>) while others on the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/continental_shelf">continental shelf</a> (e.g., the frogfishes <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antennariidae">Antennariidae</a> and the monkfish/goosefish <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lophiidae">Lophiidae</a>). Pelagic forms are most <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location">lateral</a>ly compressed, whereas the benthic forms are often extremely <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dorsum_(anatomy)">dorso</a><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ventral">ventral</a>ly compressed (depressed), often with large upward-pointing mouths.
<strong>Bed bugs</strong>, <strong>bed-bugs</strong>, or <strong>bedbugs</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasite">parasitic</a> insects of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cimicidae">cimicid</a> family that <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hematophagy">feed exclusively on blood</a>.  <strong><em>Cimex lectularius</em></strong>, the common bed bug, is the best known, as it prefers to feed on human blood. Other <em>Cimex</em> species specialize in other animals, e.g., <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bat_bug">bat bug</a>s, such as <em>Cimex pipistrelli</em> (Europe), <em>Cimex pilosellus</em> (western US), and <em>Cimex adjunctus</em> (entire eastern US).
The name &quot;bed bug&quot; derives from the preferred habitat of <em>Cimex lectularius</em>: warm houses and especially near or inside beds and bedding or other sleep areas. Bed bugs are mainly active at night, but are not exclusively <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nocturnal">nocturnal</a>.  They usually feed on their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Host_(biology)">host</a>s without being noticed.<ref name="NEJM2009"><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Goddard" data-first="J" data-author2="deShazo, R" data-journal="JAMA: the Journal of the American Medical Association" data-date="1 April 2009" data-volume="301" data-issue="13" data-pages="1358&#x2013;66" data-pmid="19336711" data-doi="10.1001/jama.2009.405">Bed bugs (Cimex lectularius) and clinical consequences of their bites</span></ref><ref name="bioofbedbug"><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-="" last1=" Reinhardt" first1=" Klaus" last2=" Siva-Jothy" first2=" Michael T." title=" Biology of the Bed Bugs (Cimicidae)" journal=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" annual_review_of_entomology"="">Annual Review of Entomology&quot; data- volume =&quot; 52&quot; data- pages =&quot; 351&#x2013;374&quot; data-date=&quot;Jan 2007&quot; data- pmid =&quot; 16968204&quot; data- url =&quot; http://www.falw.vu.nl/nl/Images/siva%202006_tcm19-30750.pdf &quot; data-doi =&quot; 10.1146/annurev.ento.52.040306.133913 &quot; data-accessdate =&quot; 26 May 2010 &quot; data- archiveurl=&quot; http://web.archive.org/web/20100705084911/http://www.falw.vu.nl/nl/Images/siva%202006_tcm19-30750.pdf&quot; data- archivedate=&quot; 5 July 2010 &quot; data- deadurl=&quot; no&quot;&gt;</span></ref>
A number of adverse health effects may result from bed bug bites, including <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skin_rashes">skin rashes</a>, psychological effects, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/allergy">allergic symptoms</a>. They are not known to transmit any <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pathogen">pathogen</a>s as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)">disease vectors</a>. Certain signs and symptoms suggest the presence of bed bugs; finding the insects confirms the diagnosis.
Bed bugs have been known as human parasites for thousands of years.  At a point in the early 1940s, they were mostly eradicated in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/developed_world">developed world</a>, but have increased in prevalence since 1995, likely due to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pesticide_resistance">pesticide resistance</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1=" Kilpenen " data-="" first1=" O. " data-last2=" Vagn Jensen " first2=" K-M. " data-last3=" Kristensen " first3=" M. " data-title=" Bed Bug Problems in Denmark, with a European Perspective " data-pages=" 395&#x2013;399 " data-url=" http://www.icup.org.uk/reports/ICUP858.pdf " data-accessdate=" 27 May 2010 "></span></ref>
The <strong>impala</strong> (<em>Aepyceros melampus</em>) is a medium-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>n <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/antelope">antelope</a>.
It is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/type_species">type species</a> of the genus <em>Aepyceros</em> and belongs to the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bovidae">Bovidae</a>. It was first described by German zoologist <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Hinrich_Carl_Lichtenstein">Martin Hinrich Carl Lichtenstein</a> in 1812. Two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subspecies">subspecies</a> of the impala have been recognised: the common impala (<em>A. m. melampus</em>) and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/black-faced_impala">black-faced</a> (<em>A. m. petersi</em>). They are typically between [120-160cminabbr=on] long. Males stand up to approximately [75-92cminabbr=on] at the shoulder and weigh [53-76kglbabbr=on], while females are [70-85cminabbr=on] and [40-53kglbabbr=on]. Both are characterised by a glossy, reddish brown coat. Only the males have the characteristic slender, lyre-shaped <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)">horns</a>, which can grow to be [45-92cminabbr=on] long.
The impala inhabits savanna grasslands and woodlands in close proximity to water sources. It is a mixed forager, whose diet consists of grasses, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forb">forb</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monocot">monocot</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dicot">dicot</a>s and foliage. It switches between grazing and browsing depending on the season and habitat. Water is an essential requirement. Impala are fast runners and are known for their leaping ability, reaching heights up to [3mftabbr=on]. They communicate using a variety of unique visual and vocal cues. There are three distinct social groups during the wet season: the female herds, the bachelor herds and the territorial males. The mating season is the three-week-long period toward the end of the wet season in May. A single fawn is born after a gestational period of about six to seven months. The fawn remains with its mother for four to six months, after which it joins juvenile groups.
The impala is native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Botswana">Botswana</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Namibia">Namibia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swaziland">Swaziland</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. Regionally extinct in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Burundi">Burundi</a>, it has been introduced in two protected areas of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gabon">Gabon</a>. The black-faced impala is confined to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kaokoland">Kaokoland</a> (Namibia) and southwestern Angola. The common impala has been widely introduced in southern Africa. Though there are no major threats to the survival of the species as a whole, poaching and natural calamities have significantly contributed to the decline of the black-faced subspecies. While the common impala has been listed as of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Least_Concern">Least Concern</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN), the black-faced has been rated as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulnerable_species">Vulnerable</a>.

<strong>Wombats</strong> are short-legged, muscular <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/quadruped">quadruped</a>al <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsupial">marsupial</a>s native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a> and are about [1min-1abbr=on] in length, with small, stubby tails.   All are members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Vombatidae</strong>.  They are adaptable and habitat tolerant, and are found in forested, mountainous, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heath">heathland</a> areas of south-eastern Australia, including Tasmania, as well as an isolated patch of about [300haabbr=on] in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epping_Forest_National_Park">Epping Forest National Park</a><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" url=" http://www.environment.gov.au/biodiversity/threatened/publications/northern-hairynosed.html " title=" Northern Hairy-nosed Wombat " data-work="Department of Sustainability, Environment, Water, Population and Communities" data-publisher="Australian Government" accessdate=" 2 July 2011"></span></ref> in central Queensland.
Ants form <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ant_colony">colonies</a> that range in size from a few dozen predatory individuals living in small natural cavities to highly organised colonies that may occupy large territories and consist of millions of individuals. Larger colonies consist mostly of sterile, wingless females forming castes of &quot;workers&quot;, &quot;soldiers&quot;, or other specialised groups. Nearly all ant colonies also have some fertile males called &quot;drones&quot; and one or more fertile females called &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queen_ant">queens</a>&quot;. The colonies are described as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/superorganism">superorganism</a>s because the ants appear to operate as a unified entity, collectively working together to support the colony.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book" data-author="Oster GF, Wilson EO" data-year="1978" data-publisher="Princeton University Press, Princeton" data-pages="21&#x2013;22" data-isbn="0-691-02361-1">Caste and ecology in the social insects</span></ref>(ref: Flannery2011)<span class="reference" data-type="book" data-last="Flannery" data-first="Tim" data-year="2011" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" grove="" atlantic,_inc."="">Grove/Atlantic, Inc.&quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-8021-9560-9&quot; data-page=&quot;79&quot;&gt;A Natural History of the Planet</span>
Ants have colonised almost every landmass on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth">Earth</a>. The only places lacking indigenous ants are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and a few remote or inhospitable islands. Ants thrive in most ecosystems and may form 15&#x2013;25% of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/terrestrial_animal">terrestrial animal</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)">biomass</a>.(ref: schultz)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/co-evolution">co-evolution</a> with other species has led to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mimicry">mimetic</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/commensalism">commensal</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasitism">parasitic</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)">mutualistic</a> relationships.
Ant societies have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/division_of_labour">division of labour</a>, communication between individuals, and an ability to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_solving">solve complex problems</a>.(ref: SANdisk)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civilization">human societies</a> have long been an inspiration and subject of study. Many human cultures make use of ants in cuisine, medication, and rituals. Some species are valued in their role as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biological_pest_control">biological pest control</a> agents.(ref: HolldoblerWilsonAnts3)<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/red_imported_fire_ant">red imported fire ant</a> (<em>Solenopsis invicta</em>), are regarded as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/invasive_species">invasive species</a>, establishing themselves in areas where they have been introduced accidentally.(ref: pests)
