The <strong>New World quails</strong> or <strong>Odontophoridae</strong> are small birds only distantly related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_quail">Old World quail</a>, but named for their similar appearance and habits. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">American</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> are in their own family <strong>Odontophoridae</strong>, whereas <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World_quail">Old World quail</a> are in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pheasant">pheasant</a> family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a>. The family ranges from Canada through to southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazil">Brazil</a>, and two species, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/California_quail">California quail</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bobwhite_quail">bobwhite quail</a>, have been successfully <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/introduced_species">introduced</a> to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Zealand">New Zealand</a>. A variety of habitats are used by the family from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical_rainforest">tropical rainforest</a> to deserts, although few species are capable of surviving at very low temperatures. Thirty-two species are placed in nine <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a>.
<strong>Locusts</strong> are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/swarm">swarm</a>ing phase of certain species of short-horned <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grasshopper">grasshopper</a>s in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acrididae">Acrididae</a>. In the solitary phase these grasshoppers are innocuous, their numbers are low and they cause little economic threat to agriculture. However, under suitable conditions of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/drought">drought</a> followed by rapid vegetation growth, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/serotonin">serotonin</a> in their brains triggers a dramatic set of changes: they start to breed abundantly, becoming <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gregarious">gregarious</a> and nomadic (loosely described as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insect_migration">migratory</a>) when their populations become dense enough. They form bands of wingless <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nymph_(biology)">nymphs</a> which later become swarms of winged adults. Both the bands and the swarms move around and rapidly strip fields and cause <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pest_(organism)">damage to crops</a>. The adults are powerful fliers; they can travel great distances, consuming most of the green vegetation wherever the swarm settles.
Locusts have formed plagues since prehistory. The Ancient Egyptians carved them on their tombs and the insects are mentioned in the Bible and the Quran. Swarms have devastated crops and been a contributory cause of famines and human migrations. More recently, changes in agricultural practices and better surveillance of locations where swarms tend to originate, have meant that control measures can be used at an early stage. The traditional means of control are based on the use of insecticides from the ground or the air, but other methods using biological control are proving effective. 
Swarming behaviour has decreased in the twentieth century, but despite modern surveillance and control methods, the potential for swarms to form is still present, and when suitable climatic conditions occur and vigilance lapses, plagues can still occur. Locusts are large insects and convenient for use in research and the study of zoology in the classroom. They are also <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entomophagy">edible insects</a>; they have been eaten throughout history and are considered a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/delicacy">delicacy</a> in many countries. The word &quot;locust&quot; is derived from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vulgar_Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> <em>locusta</em>, meaning locust or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lobster">lobster</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?allowed_in_frame" data-publisher="Etymonline.com " data-accessdate="12 October 2011">Online Etymology Dictionary </span></ref>
<strong>Lizards</strong> are a widespread group of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squamata">squamate</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptile">reptile</a>s, with approximately over 6,000 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>,<ref><a href="http://www.reptile-database.org">Reptile Database</a>. Retrieved on 2012-04-22</ref> ranging across all continents except <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, as well as most oceanic <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/island">island</a> chains. The group, traditionally recognized as the suborder <strong>Lacertilia</strong>, is defined as all extant members of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lepidosauria">Lepidosauria</a> (reptiles with overlapping scales) that are neither <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sphenodontia">sphenodonts</a> (i.e., <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tuatara">tuatara</a>) nor <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snake">snake</a>s &#x2013; they form an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/evolutionary_grade">evolutionary grade</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" last="Gibbons " first="J. Whitfield " last2="Gibbons " first2="Whit " title="Their Blood Runs Cold: Adventures With Reptiles and Amphibians " publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" university_of_alabama"="">University of Alabama Press &quot; data- year =&quot;1983 &quot; data- location =&quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alabama">Alabama</a> &quot; data- page =&quot;164 &quot; data-isbn=&quot;978-0-8173-0135-4 &quot;&gt;</span></ref> While the snakes are recognized as falling <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cladistics">phylogenetically</a> within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toxicofera">Toxicofera</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> from which they evolved, the sphenodonts are the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sister_group">sister group</a> to the squamates, the larger <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monophyletic">monophyletic</a> group, which includes both the lizards and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snake">snake</a>s.
<strong>Mackerel</strong> is a common name applied to a number of different species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pelagic_fish">pelagic fish</a>, mostly, but not exclusively, from the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scombridae">Scombridae</a>. They are found in both temperate and tropical seas, mostly living along the coast or offshore in the oceanic environment.
 
Mackerel typically have vertical stripes on their backs and deeply forked tails. Many species are restricted in their distribution ranges, and live in separate populations or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fish_stock">fish stock</a>s based on geography. Some stocks <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fish_migration">migrate</a> in large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling">schools</a> along the coast to suitable spawning grounds, where they spawn in fairly shallow waters. After spawning they return the way they came, in smaller schools, to suitable feeding grounds often near an area of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/upwelling">upwelling</a>. From there they may move offshore into deeper waters and spend the winter in relative inactivity. Other stocks migrate across oceans.
Smaller mackerel are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forage_fish">forage fish</a> for larger predators, including larger mackerel and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_cod">Atlantic cod</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-last="Daan" data-first="N." data-journal="Netherlands Journal of Sea Research" data-date="December 1973" data-volume="6" data-issue="4" data-pages="479&#x2013;517" data-doi="10.1016/0077-7579(73)90002-1">A quantitative analysis of the food intake of North Sea cod, Gadus Morhua</span></ref>  Flocks of seabirds, as well as whales, dolphins, sharks and schools of larger fish such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tuna">tuna</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marlin">marlin</a> follow mackerel schools and attack them in sophisticated and cooperative ways. Mackerel is high in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid">omega-3 oils</a> and is intensively harvested by humans. In 2009, over five million tons were landed by commercial fishermen (see graph on the right). Sport fishermen value the fighting abilities of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/king_mackerel">king mackerel</a>.<ref><em>Merriam-Webster</em> (2003) <a href="http://books.google.co.nz/books?id=TAnheeIPcAEC&amp;pg=PA688&amp;dq=%22king+mackerel%22++%22sport+fish%22&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=kdOAT8qUItHAmQW7g7DvBw&amp;ved=0CD4Q6AEwAQ#v=onepage&amp;q=%22king%20mackerel%22%20%20%22sport%20fish%22&amp;f=false">King mackerel</a> Page 688. ISBN 978-0-87779-809-5.</ref>
