A <strong>leopon</strong> [&#x2C8;l&#x25B;p&#x259;n] is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)">hybrid</a> resulting from the crossing of a male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a> with a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lion">lion</a>ess. The head of the animal is similar to that of a lion while the rest of the body carries similarities to leopards. These hybrids are produced in captivity and are unlikely to occur in the wild.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://messybeast.com/genetics/hyb-leopxlion.htm" data-accessdate="16 September 2014">HYBRIDS BETWEEN LEOPARDS AND LIONS</span></ref>
<strong>Sloths</strong> ([slo&#x28A;&#x3B8;] [SLOHTH], [sl&#x252;&#x3B8;] [SLOTH]) are medium-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">families</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/two-toed_sloth">Megalonychidae</a> (two-toed sloth) and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/three-toed_sloth">Bradypodidae</a> (three-toed sloth), classified into six <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a>.
They are part of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pilosa">Pilosa</a> and are therefore related to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anteater">anteater</a>s, which sport a similar set of specialized claws. Extant sloths are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/arboreal_locomotion">arboreal</a> (tree-dwelling) residents of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropical_rainforest">jungles</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America">Central</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_America">South America</a>, and are known for being slow-moving, and hence named &quot;sloths&quot;. Extinct sloth species include a few species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thalassocnus">aquatic sloths</a> and many <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ground_sloth">ground sloth</a>s, some of which attained the size of elephants.
Sloths make a good habitat for other organisms, and a single sloth may be home to moths, beetles, cockroaches, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ciliates">ciliates</a>, fungi, and algae.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-="" url="http://blogs.biomedcentral.com/bmcblog/2010/04/14/sloth-fur-has-symbiotic-relationship-with-green-algae/ " title="Sloth fur has symbiotic relationship with green algae " publisher="Springer Science+Business Media " work="BioMed Central " date="April 14, 2010 " accessdate="February 15, 2013 " author="Horne, Genevieve"></span></ref>
The <strong>sperm whale</strong> (<em>Physeter macrocephalus</em>), or <strong>cachalot</strong>, is the largest of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/toothed_whale">toothed whale</a>s and the largest toothed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a>. It is the only living member of genus <strong><em>Physeter</em></strong>, and one of three extant <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sperm_whale_family">sperm whale family</a>, along with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pygmy_sperm_whale">pygmy sperm whale</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dwarf_sperm_whale">dwarf sperm whale</a> of the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kogia">Kogia</a></em>.
The sperm whale can be found anywhere in the open ocean.  Females and young males live together in groups while mature males live solitary lives outside of the mating season. The females cooperate to protect and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lactation">nurse</a> their young. Females give birth every four to twenty years, and care for the calves for more than a decade. A mature sperm whale has few natural predators. Calves and weakened adults are taken by pods of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/orcas">orcas</a>.
From the early eighteenth century through the late 20th, the species was a prime target of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/whaling">whalers</a>. The head of the whale contains a liquid wax called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spermaceti">spermaceti</a>, from which the whale derives its name.  Spermaceti was used in lubricants, oil lamps, and candles. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ambergris">Ambergris</a>, a waste product from its digestive system, is still used as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fixative_(perfumery)">fixative</a> in perfumes. Occasionally the sperm whale&apos;s great size allowed it to defend itself effectively against whalers. The species is now protected by a whaling moratorium, and is currently listed as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/vulnerable_species">vulnerable</a> by the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature_and_Natural_Resources">IUCN</a>.
<strong><em>Tyrannosaurus</em></strong> ([t&#x268;&#x2CC;r&#xE6;n&#x259;&#x2C8;s&#x254;r&#x259;s] or [ta&#x26A;&#x2CC;r&#xE6;n&#x259;&#x2C8;s&#x254;r&#x259;s] (&quot;tyrant lizard&quot;, from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <em>tyrannos</em> (&#x3C4;&#x3CD;&#x3C1;&#x3B1;&#x3BD;&#x3BD;&#x3BF;&#x3C2;), &quot;tyrant&quot;, and <em>sauros</em> (&#x3C3;&#x3B1;&#x1FE6;&#x3C1;&#x3BF;&#x3C2;), &quot;lizard&quot;<ref name="OnlineEtDict"><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" online_etymology_dictionary"="">Online Etymology Dictionary &quot; data-url=&quot;http://www.etymonline.com/index.php?term&quot;&gt;Tyrannosaurus </span></ref>)) is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coelurosaur">coelurosaur</a>ian <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/theropoda">theropod</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dinosaur">dinosaur</a>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> <strong><em>Tyrannosaurus rex</em></strong> (<em>rex</em> meaning &quot;king&quot; in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin">Latin</a>), commonly abbreviated to <em>T.&#xA0;rex</em>, is one of the most well-represented of the large theropods. <em>Tyrannosaurus</em> lived throughout what is now western North America, on what was then an island continent known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laramidia">Laramidia</a>. <em>Tyrannosaurus</em> had a much wider range than other <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurids</a>. Fossils are found in a variety of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/geologic_formation">rock formations</a> dating to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maastrichtian">Maastrichtian</a> age of the upper <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Period_(geology)">Period</a>, 68 to 66&#xA0;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mya_(unit)">million years ago</a>.<ref name="hellcreekage"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Hicks " data-first1="J. F. " data-last2="Johnson " data-first2="K. R. " data-last3="Obradovich " data-first3="J. D. " data-last4="Tauxe " data-first4="L. " data-last5="Clark " data-first5="D. " data-date="2002 " data-journal="Geological Society of America Special Papers " data-doi="10.1130/0-8137-2361-2.35 " data-volume="361 " data-pages="35&#x2013;55 " data-url="http://specialpapers.gsapubs.org/content/361/35.full.pdf " data-format="PDF">Magnetostratigraphy and geochronology of the Hell Creek and basal Fort Union Formations of southwestern North Dakota and a recalibration of the Cretaceous&#x2013;Tertiary Boundary </span></ref> It was the last known member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tyrannosauridae">tyrannosaurids</a>,<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-last="DiChristina " data-first="Mariette " data-date="Apr 14, 2015 " data-url="http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/rise-of-the-tyrants " data-quote="...&#xA0;was merely the last survivor of a startling variety of tyrannosaurs that lived across the globe right up until the asteroid impact 66 million years ago&#xA0;...">Rise of the Tyrants </span></ref> and among the last non-<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/aves">avian</a> dinosaurs to exist before the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cretaceous&#x2013;Paleogene_extinction_event">Cretaceous&#x2013;Paleogene extinction event</a>.
Like other tyrannosaurids, <em>Tyrannosaurus</em> was a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biped">biped</a>al <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivore">carnivore</a> with a massive skull balanced by a long, heavy tail. Relative to its large and powerful hind limbs, <em>Tyrannosaurus</em> fore limbs were short but unusually powerful for their size and had two clawed digits. Although other theropods rivaled or exceeded <em>Tyrannosaurus rex</em> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dinosaur_size">size</a>, it was the largest known tyrannosaurid and one of the largest known land predators. In fact, the most complete specimen measures up to [12.3mftabbr=onsp=us] in length,(ref: Hutchinsonet.al.2011)<ref name="SueFMNH"><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-author=" " data-work="Sue at the Field Museum " data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" field_museum_of_natural_history"="">Field Museum of Natural History &quot; data-url=&quot;http://www.fieldmuseum.org/sue/about_vital.asp &quot; data-accessdate=&quot;September 15, 2007 &quot; data-archiveurl=&quot;http://web.archive.org/web/20070929090231/http://www.fieldmuseum.org/SUE/about_vital.asp  &quot; data-archivedate=&quot;September 29, 2007&quot;&gt;Sues vital statistics </span></ref> and up to [6.8MTST] in weight.<ref name="ericksonetal2004"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-last1="Erickson " data-first1="Gregory M. " data-last2="Makovicky " data-first2="Peter J. " data-last3="Currie " data-first3="Philip J. " data-authorlink3="Phil Currie " data-last4="Norell " data-first4="Mark A. " data-last5="Yerby " data-first5="Scott A. " data-last6="Brochu " data-first6="Christopher A. " data-date="2004 " data-journal="Nature " data-doi="10.1038/nature02699 " data-pmid="15306807 " data-volume="430 " data-issue="7001 " data-pages="772&#x2013;775">Gigantism and comparative life-history parameters of tyrannosaurid dinosaurs </span></ref> By far the largest carnivore in its environment, Tyrannosaurus rex<strong>may have been an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/apex_predator">apex predator</a>, preying upon <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hadrosaur">hadrosaur</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ceratopsia">ceratopsia</a>ns, and possibly sauropods,(ref: SwitekChompedSauropod)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-last="Switeck " data-first="Brian " data-date="April 13, 2012 " data-publisher="Smithsonian Media " data-url="http://blogs.smithsonianmag.com/dinosaur/2012/04/when-tyrannosaurus-chomped-sauropods/ " data-accessdate="August 24, 2013">When Tyrannosaurus Chomped Sauropods </span> although some experts have suggested the dinosaur was primarily a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scavenger">scavenger</a>. The question of whether </strong>Tyrannosaurus<strong>was an apex predator or a pure scavenger has been among the longest ongoing debates in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/paleontology">paleontology</a>; however, most scientists now agree that </strong>Tyrannosaurus rex<strong>was an opportunistic carnivore, acting as both a predator and a scavenger.(ref: HutchinsonTrexPredatorMediaHype)<span class="reference" data-type="web " data-last="Hutchinson " data-first="John " data-date="July 15, 2013 " data-publisher="What&lt;/strong&gt;s in John&apos;s Freezer? " data-url="http://whatsinjohnsfreezer.com/2013/07/15/trex_scavenger_stfu/ " data-accessdate="August 26, 2013">Tyrannosaurus rex: predator or media hype? </span> It is estimated to have one of the largest biting forces among all animals.(ref: SnivelyHendersonPhillips2006FusedVaultedNasals)(ref: MM03)</strong>
More than 50&#xA0;specimens of <em>Tyrannosaurus rex</em> have been identified, some of which are nearly complete skeletons. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_tissue">Soft tissue</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/protein">protein</a>s have been reported in at least one of these specimens. The abundance of fossil material has allowed significant research into many aspects of its biology, including its life history and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biomechanics">biomechanics</a>. The feeding habits, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/physiology">physiology</a> and potential speed of <em>Tyrannosaurus rex</em> are a few subjects of debate. Its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomy</a> is also controversial, as some scientists consider <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tarbosaurus">Tarbosaurus bataar</a></em> from Asia to be a second <em>Tyrannosaurus</em> species while others maintain <em>Tarbosaurus</em> is a separate genus. Several other genera of North American tyrannosaurids have also been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/synonym_(biology)">synonymized</a> with <em>Tyrannosaurus</em>.
<strong>Apes</strong> (<strong>Hominoidea</strong>) are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">branch</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a> tailless <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simian">anthropoid</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/catarrhine">catarrhine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/primate">primate</a>s native to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a>. They are distinguished from other primates by a wider degree of freedom of motion at the shoulder joint as evolved by the influence of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/brachiation">brachiation</a>. There are two extant branches of the superfamily <strong>Hominoidea</strong>: the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gibbon">gibbon</a>s, or lesser apes; and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hominid">hominid</a>s, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/great_apes">great apes</a>.
Members of the superfamily <strong>Hominoidae</strong> are called <strong>hominoids</strong>&#x2014;which term is not to be confused with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hominid">hominid</a>s, the family of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/great_ape">great ape</a>s; or with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hominin">hominin</a>s, the tribe of humans also known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/the_human_clade">the human clade</a>; or with other very similar terms of primate taxa. (Compare <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominidae#Terminology">terminology of primate names</a>.)
Recent evidence has changed our understanding of the relationships between the hominoids, especially regarding the human lineage; and the traditionally used terms have become somewhat confused. Competing approaches re methodology and terminology are found among current scientific sources. See below, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/#History_of_hominoid_taxonomy">History of hominoid taxonomy</a> and see <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate#Historical_and_modern_terminology">Primate: Historical and modern terminology</a> for discussions of the changes in scientific classification and terminology regarding hominoids.
Some and, recently, all, <strong>hominoids</strong> are also called &quot;<strong>apes</strong>&quot;, but the term is used broadly and has several different senses within both popular and scientific settings. &quot;Ape&quot; has been used as a synonym for &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monkey">monkey</a>&quot; or for naming any primate with a humanlike appearance, particularly those without a tail. Thus the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barbary_macaque">Barbary macaque</a>, a kind of monkey, is popularly called the &quot;Barbary ape&quot;. Biologists have traditionally used the term &quot;ape&quot; to mean a member of the superfamily Hominoidea <em>other than</em> humans, but more recently to mean <em>all</em> members of Hominoidea. So &quot;ape&quot;&#x2014;not to be confused with &quot;great ape&quot;&#x2014;now becomes another word for <strong>hominoid</strong> <em>including</em> humans.
Except for gorillas and humans, hominoids are agile climbers of trees. Their diet is best described as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/frugivorous">frugivorous</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/folivorous">folivorous</a>, consisting mainly of fruit, nuts, seeds, including grass seeds, leaves, and in some cases other animals, either hunted or scavenged, or (solely in the case of the humans) farmed&#x2014;along with anything else available and easily digested.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn14926-loving-bonobos-have-a-carnivorous-dark-side.html#.VHXsaclWqUU" data-last="Callaway" data-first="Ewen" data-date="13 October 2006" data-work="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" new_scientist"="">New Scientist&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;26 November 2014&quot;&gt;Loving bonobos have a carnivorous dark side</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.nature.com/news/humans-are-becoming-more-carnivorous-1.14282" data-last="Hoag" data-first="Hannah" data-date="2 December 2013" data-work="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" nature_(journal)"_data-nature"="">Nature (journal)&quot; data-Nature=&quot;undefined&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;26 November 2014&quot;&gt;Humans are becoming more carnivorous</span></ref>
Most non-human hominoids are rare or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endangered_species">endangered</a>. The chief threat to most of the endangered species is loss of tropical <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rainforest">rainforest</a> habitat, though some populations are further imperiled by hunting for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bushmeat">bushmeat</a>. The great apes of Africa are also facing threat from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebola_virus">Ebola virus</a>. Currently considered to be the greatest threat to survival of African apes, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebola">Ebola</a> is responsible for the death of at least one third of the species since 1990.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="news" data-url="http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/africa/ebola-virus-has-killed-a-third-of-the-worlds-gorillas-and-chimpanzees--and-could-pose-greatest-threat-to-their-survival-conservationists-warn-9998386.html" data-last="Rush" data-first="James" data-date="23 January 2015" data-work="The Independent" data-accessdate="26 March 2015">Ebola virus has killed a third of worlds gorillas and chimpanzees&apos; &#x2013; and could pose greatest threat to their survival, conservationists warn</span></ref>
An <strong>ox</strong> (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/English_plural#Irregular_-.28e.29n_plurals">plural</a> <strong>oxen</strong>), also known as a <strong>bullock</strong> in Australia and India, is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bovinae">bovine</a> trained as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/working_animal">draft animal</a>. Oxen are commonly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/castration">castrated</a> adult male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cattle">cattle</a>; castration makes the animals easier to control. Cows (adult females) or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bull">bull</a>s (intact males) may also be used in some areas.
Oxen are used for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plough">plow</a>ing, for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/transport">transport</a> (pulling <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cart">cart</a>s, hauling <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wagon">wagon</a>s and even riding), for <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/threshing">threshing</a> grain by trampling, and for powering machines that grind grain or supply <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/irrigation">irrigation</a> among other purposes. Oxen may be also used to skid logs in forests, particularly in low-impact, select-cut <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/logging">logging</a>.
Oxen are usually yoked in pairs. Light work such as carting household items on good roads might require just one pair, while for heavier work, further pairs would be added as necessary. A team used for a heavy load over difficult ground might exceed nine or ten pairs.
The <strong>jaguar</strong> ([&#x2C8;d&#x292;&#xE6;&#x261;ju&#x2D0;&#x259;r,_&#x2C8;d&#x292;&#xE6;&#x261;j&#x28A;&#x259;r,_&#x2C8;d&#x292;&#xE6;&#x261;ju&#x2D0;&#x251;r] or [&#x2C8;d&#x292;&#xE6;&#x261;w&#x251;r]), <strong><em>Panthera onca</em></strong>, is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/big_cat">big cat</a>, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/felidae">feline</a> in the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panthera">Panthera</a></em> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a>, and is the only <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/extant_taxon">extant</a> <em>Panthera</em> species native to the Americas. The jaguar is the third-largest feline after the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tiger">tiger</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lion">lion</a>, and the largest in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Americas">Americas</a>. The jaguar&apos;s present range extends from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southwestern_United_States">Southwestern United States</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico">Mexico</a> across much of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America">Central America</a> and south to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paraguay">Paraguay</a> and northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Argentina">Argentina</a>. Apart from a known and possibly breeding population in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arizona">Arizona</a> (southeast of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tucson">Tucson</a>) and the bootheel of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Mexico">New Mexico</a>, the cat has largely been <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_extinction">extirpated</a> from the United States since the early 20th century.
This spotted cat most closely resembles the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/leopard">leopard</a> physically, although it is usually larger and of sturdier build and its behavioral and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/habitat_(ecology)">habitat</a> characteristics are closer to those of the tiger. While dense <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests">rainforest</a> is its preferred habitat, the jaguar will range across a variety of forested and open terrains. It is strongly associated with the presence of water and is notable, along with the tiger, as a feline that enjoys swimming. The jaguar is largely a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wiktionary:solitary">solitary</a>, opportunistic, stalk-and-ambush <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/predator">predator</a> at the top of the food chain (an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/apex_predator">apex predator</a>). It is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/keystone_species">keystone species</a>, playing an important role in stabilizing <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecosystem">ecosystem</a>s and regulating the populations of the animals it hunts. The jaguar has an exceptionally powerful bite, even relative to the other big cats. This allows it to pierce the shells of armored reptiles and to employ an unusual killing method: it bites directly through the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/skull">skull</a> of prey between the ears to deliver a fatal bite to the brain.
The jaguar is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/near_threatened">near threatened</a> species and its numbers are declining. Threats include loss and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fragmentation_of_habitat">fragmentation of habitat</a>. While international trade in jaguars or their parts is prohibited, the cat is still frequently killed by humans, particularly in conflicts with ranchers and farmers in South America. Although reduced, its range remains large. Given its historical distribution, the jaguar has featured prominently in the mythology of numerous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas">indigenous American cultures</a>, including those of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maya_civilization">Maya</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aztec">Aztec</a>.
<strong>Flamingos</strong> (or <strong>flamingoes</strong><ref>Both forms of the plural are attested, according to the <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></em></ref>) are a type of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wading_bird">wading bird</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <strong>Phoenicopteridae</strong>. There are four flamingo species in the Americas and two species in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>. Often, they are pink in colour.
<strong>Sparrows</strong> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> of small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/passerine">passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s, <strong>Passeridae</strong>. They are also known as <strong>true sparrows</strong>, or <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a> sparrows</strong>, names also used for a particular <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> of the family, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Passer">Passer</a></em>. They are distinct from both the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_sparrow">American sparrow</a>s, in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emberizidae">Emberizidae</a>, and from a few other birds sharing their name, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_sparrow">Java sparrow</a> of the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Estrildidae">Estrildidae</a>. Many species nest on buildings, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_sparrow">house</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a>s in particular inhabit cities in large numbers, so sparrows may be the most familiar of all wild birds. They are primarily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seed">seed-eaters</a>, though they also consume small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s. Some species scavenge for food around cities and, like <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gull">gull</a>s or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rock_dove">rock dove</a>s, will happily eat virtually anything in small quantities.
<strong>Herring</strong> are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forage_fish">forage fish</a>, mostly belonging to the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clupeidae">Clupeidae</a>.
They often move in large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling">schools</a> around <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fishing_bank">fishing bank</a>s and near the coast. The most abundant and commercially important species belong to the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clupea">Clupea</a></em>, found particularly in shallow, temperate waters of the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ocean">Pacific</a> and the North <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean">Atlantic</a> oceans, including the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltic_Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, as well as off the west coast of South America. Three species of <em>Clupea</em> are recognised, and provide about 90% of all herrings captured in fisheries. Most abundant of all is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_herring">Atlantic herring</a>, providing over half of all herring capture. Fishes called herring are also found in India, in the Arabian Sea, Indian Ocean and Bay of Bengal.
Herring played a pivotal role in the history of marine fisheries in Europe,<ref>Cushing, David H (1975) <a href="http://books.google.co.nz/books?id=TgU4AAAAIAAJ&amp;printsec=frontcover&amp;dq=%22Marine+ecology+and+fisheries%22&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=BLeTT77ULOTSmAWBtoj0AQ&amp;ved=0CDgQ6AEwAA#v=onepage&amp;q=%22Marine%20ecology%20and%20fisheries%22&amp;f=false"><em>Marine ecology and fisheries</em></a> Cambridge University Press. ISBN 978-0-521-09911-0.</ref> and early in the twentieth century their study was fundamental to the evolution of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fisheries_science">fisheries science</a>.<ref>Went, AEJ (1972) &quot;The History of the International Council for the Exploration of the Sea&quot;. <em>Proceedings of the Royal Society of Edinburgh</em>. Section B. Biology, <strong>73</strong>: 351&#x2013;360.[10.1017/S0080455X0000240X]</ref> These <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/oily_fish">oily fish</a><ref><span class="reference" data-type="news" data-url="http://www.food.gov.uk/news/newsarchive/2004/jun/oilyfishdefinition" data-date="2004-06-24" data-publisher="&lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" food_standards_agency"="">Food Standards Agency&quot;&gt;What&apos;s an oily fish?</span></ref> also have a long history as an important <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/food_fish">food fish</a>, and are often <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/salted_fish">salted</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/smoked_fish">smoked</a>, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pickled_herring">pickled</a>.
<strong>Macaws</strong> are long-tailed, often colourful <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neotropical_parrot">New World parrots</a>.
The <strong>great blue heron</strong> (<em>Ardea herodias</em>) is a large wading <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/heron">heron</a> family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardeidae">Ardeidae</a>, common near the shores of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Body_of_water">open water</a> and in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wetland">wetland</a>s over most of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_America">Central America</a>, as well as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caribbean">Caribbean</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gal&#xE1;pagos_Islands">Gal&#xE1;pagos Islands</a>. It is a rare vagrant to Europe, with records from Spain, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Azores">Azores</a>, England, and the Netherlands. An all-white population found only in the Caribbean and southern Florida was once treated as a separate species and known as the <strong>great white heron</strong>.
<strong>Hookworm infection</strong>, also known as <strong>hookworm disease</strong>, is an infection by a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/parasitic">parasitic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hematophagy">bloodsucking</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nematode">roundworm</a>. Hookworm infections include <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ancylostomiasis">ancylostomiasis</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/necatoriasis">necatoriasis</a>. These worms live in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/small_intestine">small intestine</a> of their host, which may be a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a> or a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a> such as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog">dog</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cat">cat</a>, or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>. Hookworm infection in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pregnancy">pregnancy</a> can cause <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/intrauterine_growth_retardation">retarded growth</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fetus">fetus</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/preterm_birth">premature birth</a> and a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/low_birth_weight">low birth weight</a>. Hookworms in children can cause intellectual, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cognition">cognitive</a> and growth problems.
Two species of hookworms commonly infect humans: <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancylostoma_duodenale">Ancylostoma duodenale</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Necator_americanus">Necator americanus</a></em>. <em>A. duodenale</em> predominates in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_East">Middle East</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Africa">North Africa</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India">India</a> and (formerly) in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southern_Europe">southern Europe</a>, while <em>N. americanus</em> predominates in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/the_Americas">the Americas</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China">China</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancylostoma_tubaeforme">A. tubaeforme</a></em> infects cats, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancylostoma_caninum">A. caninum</a></em> infects dogs and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancylostoma_braziliense">A. braziliense</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uncinaria_stenocephala">Uncinaria stenocephala</a></em> infect both cats and dogs.  Hookworms are much smaller than the giant roundworms <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ascaris_lumbricoides">Ascaris lumbricoides</a></em> and so cause less tissue damage and obstruction. The most significant risk of hookworm infection is <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/anemia">anemia</a>, secondary to loss of iron (and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/protein">protein</a>) in the gut. The worms suck <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/blood">blood</a> voraciously and damage the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mucosa">mucosa</a>. However, the blood loss in the stools is not visibly apparent.
Hookworm infection affects over half a billion people globally.<ref name="Fen2012"><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" author=" Fenwick A " title=" The global burden of neglected tropical diseases. " journal=" Public health " volume=" 126 " issue=" 3 " pages=" 233&#x2013;6 " date=" March 2012 " pmid=" 22325616 " doi=" 10.1016/j.puhe.2011.11.015 "></span></ref> It is a leading cause of maternal and child morbidity in the developing countries of the tropics and subtropics. In developed countries, hookworm infection is rarely fatal, but anemia can be significant in a heavily infected individual. Hookworm infection is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/soil-transmitted_helminthiasis">soil-transmitted helminthiasis</a> and therefore classified as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/neglected_tropical_disease">neglected tropical disease</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.cdc.gov/globalhealth/ntd/diseases/index.html" data-website="cdc.gov" data-accessdate="28 November 2014" data-date="June 6, 2011">Neglected Tropical Diseases</span></ref> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancylostomiasis">Ancylostomiasis</a> is the disease caused when <em>Ancylostoma duodenale</em> hookworms, present in large numbers, produce an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/iron_deficiency_anemia">iron deficiency anemia</a> by sucking blood from the host&apos;s intestinal walls.
The <strong>herons</strong> are long-legged freshwater and coastal <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s in the family <strong>Ardeidae</strong>, with 64 recognised <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> (some are called <em>&quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/egret">egret</a>s&quot;</em> or <em>&quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bittern">bittern</a>s&quot;</em> instead of &quot;heron&quot;). Within Ardeidae, all members of the genera <em>Botaurus</em> and <em>Ixobrychus</em> are referred to as &quot;bitterns&quot;, and&#x2014;including the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/zigzag_heron">zigzag heron</a> or zigzag bittern&#x2014;are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monophyletic">monophyletic</a> group within the Ardeidae. However, egrets are not a biologically distinct group from the herons, and tend to be named differently because they are mainly white or have decorative plumes. Although egrets have the same build as herons, they tend to be smaller.
The classification of the individual heron/egret species is fraught with difficulty, and there is still no clear consensus about the correct placement of many species into either of the two major genera, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardea_(genus)">Ardea</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egretta">Egretta</a></em>. Similarly, the relationship of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> in the family is not completely resolved. However, one species formerly considered to constitute a separate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monotypic">monotypic</a> family <strong>Cochlearidae</strong>, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/boat-billed_heron">boat-billed heron</a>, is now regarded as a member of the Ardeidae.
Although herons resemble birds in some other families, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/stork">stork</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ibis">ibis</a>es, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spoonbill">spoonbill</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crane_(bird)">cranes</a>, they differ from these in flying with their necks retracted, not outstretched. They are also one of the bird groups that have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/powder_down">powder down</a>. Some members of this group nest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bird_colony">colonially</a> in trees, while others, notably the bitterns, use <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reed_bed">reed bed</a>s.
<strong>Irukandji jellyfish</strong> ([&#x2CC;&#x26A;r&#x259;&#x2C8;k&#xE6;nd&#x292;i] [IRR&#x259;KANjee]) are small and extremely <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/venom">venom</a>ous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/box_jellyfish">box jellyfish</a> that inhabit marine waters of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australia">Australia</a> and which are able to fire their stingers into their victim, causing symptoms collectively known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irukandji_syndrome">Irukandji syndrome</a>. Their size is roughly a cubic centimetre (1&#xA0;cm<sup>3</sup>). There are five known species of Irukandji: <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carukia_barnesi">Carukia barnesi</a></em>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malo_kingi">Malo kingi</a></em> and discovered in the 2000s and 2010s, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malo_maximus">Malo maximus</a></em>, <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malo_filipina">Malo filipina</a></em> and <em>Malo bella</em>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-="" title=" Malo kingi: A new species of Irukandji jellyfish (Cnidaria: Cubozoa: Carybdeida), possibly lethal to humans, from Queensland, Australia" last="Gershwin" first="Lisa-Ann" accessdate=" 2010-07-23" url=" http://eprints.jcu.edu.au/2670/" work=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" zootaxa"="">Zootaxa&quot; data- year =&quot; 2007&quot; data- issue=&quot;1659&quot; data- pages=&quot;55&#x2013;68&quot;&gt;</span></ref><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal" data-="" title=" The pharmacology of Malo maxima jellyfish venom extract in isolated cardiovascular tissues: A probable cause of the Irukandji syndrome in Western Australia" last="Li" first="R." work=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" tox_letters"="">Tox Letters&quot; data- year =&quot; 2011&quot; data- issue=&quot;201(3)&quot; data- pages=&quot;221-9&quot; data- pmid=&quot;21237252&quot; data- doi=&quot;10.1016/j.toxlet.2011.01.003&quot; data- volume=&quot;201&quot;&gt;</span></ref>
The symptoms of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irukandji_syndrome">Irukandji syndrome</a> were first documented by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hugo_Flecker">Hugo Flecker</a> in 1952.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="book " data-="" last="Pearn" first="J. H. " title=" Australian Dictionary of Biography, Volume 14" year=" 1990 " publisher=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" melbourne_university_press"="">Melbourne University Press &quot; data- pages=&quot;182&#x2013;184&quot; data-isbn =&quot; 0-522-84717-X &quot; data- url =&quot; http://www.adb.online.anu.edu.au/biogs/A140195b.htm&quot; data-accessdate=&quot;2013-10-01&quot;&gt;</span></ref> They were named after the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Irukandji_people">Irukandji people</a> whose country stretches along the coastal strip north of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairns,_Queensland">Cairns, Queensland</a>.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" issn=" 0025-729X " volume=" 2 " issue=" 3 " pages=" 89&#x2013;91 " last=" Flecker " first=" Hugo " title=" Irukandji sting to North Queensland bathers without production of weals but with severe general symptoms " journal=" &lt;a href=" http:="" en.wikipedia.org="" wiki="" the_medical_journal_of_australia"="">The Medical Journal of Australia &quot; data- date =&quot; 1952-07-19 &quot; data- pmid =&quot; 14956317 &quot;&gt;</span></ref> The first of these jellyfish, <em>Carukia barnesi</em>, was identified in 1964 by Jack Barnes; in order to prove it was the cause of Irukandji syndrome, he captured the tiny jelly and allowed it to sting him while his son and a lifeguard observed the effects.(ref: MJA1964-Barnes)<span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" last=" Barnes" first=" J. " title=" Cause and effect in Irukandji stingings " journal=" Med J Aust " volume=" 1 " issue=" " pages=" 897&#x2013;904 " year=" 1964 " pmid=" 14172390"></span><ref><span class="reference" data-type="journal " data-="" pmid=" 21237252 " doi="10.1016/j.toxlet.2011.01.003 " volume="201 " issue="3 " title="The pharmacology of Malo maxima jellyfish venom extract in isolated cardiovascular tissues: A probable cause of the Irukandji syndrome in Western Australia. " date="Mar 2011 " journal="Toxicol Lett " pages="221&#x2013;9"></span></ref>
A <strong>kangaroo mouse</strong> is either one of the two <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> of jumping mouse (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Microdipodops</em></strong>) native to the deserts of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/southwestern_United_States">southwestern United States</a>, predominantly found in the state of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nevada">Nevada</a>. The name &quot;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo">kangaroo</a> mouse&quot; refers to the species&apos; extraordinary jumping ability, as well as its habit of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/biped">biped</a>al locomotion.    The two species are:
Both species of kangaroo mouse live in sandy <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/desert">desert</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ecosystem">ecosystem</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/foraging">forage</a> for seeds and vegetation amongst the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scrub_brush">scrub brush</a> of their native habitat.  The dark kangaroo mouse is also known to feed occasionally on <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/insect">insect</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carrion">carrion</a>. The mouse never drinks <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/water">water</a>, instead deriving it <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cellular_respiration">metabolically</a> from the foods it eats.  The kangaroo mouse collects food and maintains large caches in their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/burrow">burrow</a>s, which are excavated to a length of between 3 and 8 feet (1 to 2.5 meters).  The burrow, the entrance to which the mouse covers during daylight hours, is also used to raise litters of between 2 and 7 young.  The pale kangaroo mouse burrows only in fine sand, while the dark kangaroo mouse prefers fine, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gravel">gravel</a>ly <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/soil">soil</a>s but may also burrow in sand or sandy soil.  Kangaroo mice are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nocturnal">nocturnal</a>, and are most active in the two hours following <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sunset">sunset</a>.  They are believed to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hibernation">hibernate</a> during cold weather.
The kangaroo mice are closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/kangaroo_rat">kangaroo rat</a>s, which belong to the same <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dipodomyinae">Dipodomyinae</a>.
The kangaroo mouse features significantly in the best-selling <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/science_fiction">science fiction</a> novel <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dune_(novel)_"> Dune</a>. The protagonist, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Atreides">Paul Atreides</a>, is called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muad&apos;Dib">Muad&apos;Dib</a>, a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fremen">fremen</a> word for kangaroo mouse.
A <strong>land snail</strong> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/common_name">common name</a> for any of the numerous species of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/snail">snail</a> that live on land, as opposed to those that live in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seawater">salt water</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/fresh_water">fresh water</a>. Land snails are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/terrestrial_animal">terrestrial</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gastropod">gastropod</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mollusk">mollusk</a>s that have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gastropod_shell">shells</a> (those without shells are known as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/slug">slug</a>s). In reality however, it is not always easy to say which species are terrestrial, because some are more or less <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amphibian">amphibious</a> between land and freshwater, and others are relatively amphibious between land and saltwater.
The majority of land snails are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pulmonate">pulmonate</a>s, i.e. they have a lung and breathe air. A minority however belong to much more ancient <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lineage_(evolution)">lineages</a> where their anatomy includes a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gill">gill</a> and an <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/operculum_(gastropod)">operculum</a>. Many of these operculate land snails live in habitats or microhabitats that are sometimes (or often) damp or wet, such as for example in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moss">moss</a>.
Land snails have a strong muscular foot; they use <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mucus">mucus</a> to enable them to crawl over rough surfaces, and in order to keep their soft bodies from drying out. Like other mollusks, land snails have a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mantle_(mollusc)">mantle</a>, and they have one or two pairs of tentacles on their head. Their internal anatomy includes a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/radula">radula</a> and a primitive brain.
In terms of reproduction, the majority of land snails are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hermaphrodite">hermaphrodite</a> (have a full set of organs of both sexes) and most lay clutches of eggs in the soil. Tiny snails hatch out of the egg with a small shell in place, and the shell grows spirally as the soft parts gradually increase in size. Most land snails have shells that are right-handed in their coiling.
A wide range of different vertebrate and invertebrate animals prey on land snails, and they are used as food by humans in various cultures worldwide, and are even raised on farms as food in some areas.
The <strong>martens</strong> constitute the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Martes</em></strong> within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelinae">Mustelinae</a>, in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mustelidae">Mustelidae</a>. Martens are slender, agile animals, adapted to living in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/taiga">taiga</a>s, and are found in coniferous and northern deciduous <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/forest">forest</a>s across the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_hemisphere">northern hemisphere</a>. They have bushy tails, and large paws with partially retractible claws. The fur varies from yellowish to dark brown, depending on the species, and, in many cases, is valued by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fur_trade">fur</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Animal_trapping">trappers</a>.
<strong>Marmots</strong> are large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/squirrel">squirrel</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> <strong><em>Marmota</em></strong>, of which there are 15 species. Some species live in mountainous areas, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alps">Alps</a>, northern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apennine_Mountains">Apennines</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains">Carpathians</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tatra_Mountains">Tatras</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyrenees">Pyrenees</a> in Europe and northwestern Asia; the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocky_Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_Hills">Black Hills</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cascade_Mountains">Cascades</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacific_Ranges">Pacific Ranges</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a>; and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deosai_National_Park">Deosai Plateau</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ladakh">Ladakh</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India">India</a>. Other species prefer rough grassland and can be found widely across <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasian_steppes">Eurasian steppes</a>. The similarly sized, but more social, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prairie_dog">prairie dog</a> is not classified in the genus <em>Marmota</em> but in the related genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cynomys">Cynomys</a></em>. 
Marmots typically live in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/burrow">burrow</a>s (often within rockpiles, particularly in the case of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/yellow-bellied_marmot">yellow-bellied marmot</a>), and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hibernation">hibernate</a> there through the winter.  Most marmots are highly social and use loud whistles to communicate with one another, especially when alarmed.
Marmots mainly eat greens and many types of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grass">grass</a>es, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/berries">berries</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lichen">lichen</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/moss">moss</a>es, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/root">root</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flower">flower</a>s.
<strong>Peafowl</strong> include two Asiatic species (the blue or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indian_peafowl">Indian peafowl</a> originally of India and Sri Lanka and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/green_peafowl">green peafowl</a> of Burma, Indochina, and Java) and one African species (the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_peafowl">Congo peafowl</a> native only to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Congo_Basin">Congo Basin</a>) of bird in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a> <em>Pavo</em> and <em>Afropavo</em> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a> family, the pheasants and their allies, known for the males piercing call and, among the Asiatic species, his extravagant eye-spotted tail <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/covert_feathers">covert feathers</a> which he displays as part of a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/courtship">courtship</a> ritual. The term peacock<strong> is properly reserved for the male; the female is known as a </strong>peahen, and the immature offspring are sometimes called peachicks.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web " data-url="http://www.britannica.com/eb/article-9058859/peacock " data-work="Britannica Online Encyclopedia">Peacock (bird) </span></ref> 
The functions of the elaborate <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/iridescent">iridescent</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/animal_coloration">coloration</a> and large &quot;train&quot; of peacocks have been the subject of extensive scientific debate. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin">Charles Darwin</a> suggested they served to attract females, and the showy features of the males had evolved by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sexual_selection">sexual selection</a>. More recently, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amotz_Zahavi">Amotz Zahavi</a> proposed in his <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/handicap_theory">handicap theory</a> that these features <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/signalling_theory">acted as honest signals</a> of the males&apos; fitness, since less fit males would be disadvantaged by the difficulty of surviving with such large and conspicuous structures.
<strong>Mockingbirds</strong> are a group of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World">New World</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/passerine">passerine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimid">Mimid</a>ae <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a>. They are best known for the habit of some species <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimicry">mimicking</a> the songs of other birds and the sounds of insects and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/amphibian">amphibian</a>s,<ref><a href="http://10000birds.com/mockingbirds.htm">10000birds.com</a></ref> often loudly and in rapid succession. There are about 17 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> in three <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genera</a>. These do not appear to form a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monophyletic">monophyletic</a> lineage: <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mimus">Mimus</a></em> and <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nesomimus">Nesomimus</a></em> are quite closely related; their closest living relatives appear to be some <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/thrasher">thrasher</a>s, such as the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/sage_thrasher">sage thrasher</a>. <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanotis">Melanotis</a></em> is more distinct; it seems to represent a very ancient <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)">basal</a> lineage of Mimidae.<ref name="Hunt">Hunt, Jeffrey S.; Bermingham, Eldredge; &amp; Ricklefs, Robert E. (2001): &quot;<a href="http://findarticles.com/p/articles/mi_qa3793/is_200101/ai_n8930531">Molecular systematics and biogeography of Antillean thrashers, tremblers, and mockingbirds (Aves: Mimidae)</a>.&quot; <strong><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Auk">Auk</a></strong> <strong>118</strong>(1): 35&#x2013;55. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_Object_Identifier">DOI</a>:10.1642/0004-8038(2001)1180035:MSABOA2.0.CO;2</ref><ref name="Barber">Barber, Brian R.; Mart&#xED;nez-G&#xF3;mez, Juan E. &amp; Peterson, A. Townsend (2004) &quot;Systematic position of the Socorro mockingbird <em>Mimodes graysoni</em>.&quot; <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Journal_of_Avian_Biology">J. Avian Biol.</a></em> <strong>35</strong>: 195&#x2013;198. [10.1111/j.0908-8857.2004.03233.x]</ref>
The only mockingbird commonly found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_America">North America</a> is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/northern_mockingbird">northern mockingbird</a> <em>(Mimus polyglottos)</em>. The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_language">Greek</a> word <em>polyglottos</em> means <em>multiple languages</em>.
The <strong>jackal</strong> is a small omnivorous mammal of the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canis">Canis</a></em>, which also includes the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gray_wolf">wolf</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dog">dog</a>. While the word &quot;jackal&quot; has historically been used for many small canids, in modern use it most commonly refers to three species: the closely related <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/black-backed_jackal">black-backed jackal</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/side-striped_jackal">side-striped jackal</a> of sub-Saharan Africa, and the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/golden_jackal">golden jackal</a> of south-central Eurasia.
Jackals and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coyote">coyote</a>s (sometimes called the &quot;American jackal&quot;<ref><a href="http://www.canids.org/species/coyote.pdf">Coyote (2004) by E.M. Gese &amp; M. Bekoff</a></ref>) are opportunistic omnivores, predators of small- to medium-sized animals and proficient <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/scavenger">scavenger</a>s.  Their long legs and curved <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/canine_teeth">canine teeth</a> are adapted for hunting small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mammal">mammal</a>s, birds, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/reptile">reptile</a>s, and their large feet and fused leg bones give them a physique well-suited for long-distance running, capable of maintaining speeds of [16km/hmphabbr=on] for extended periods of time. Jackals are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/crepuscular">crepuscular</a>, most active at dawn and dusk.
Their most common <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/social_unit">social unit</a> is a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monogamous">monogamous</a> pair, which defends its territory from other pairs by vigorously chasing intruding rivals and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/territorial_marking">marking landmarks</a> around the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/territory_(animal)">territory</a> with their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/urine">urine</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/feces">feces</a>. The territory may be large enough to hold some young adults, which stay with their parents until they establish their own territories. Jackals may occasionally assemble in small packs, for example, to scavenge a carcass, but they normally hunt either alone or in pairs.
  | id =}} Mastodons lived in herds and were predominantly forest dwelling animals that fed on a mixed diet of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Browsing_(herbivory)">browsing</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/grazing">grazing</a> with a seasonal preference for browsing, in contrast to living elephants that are mostly grazing animals.
<em>M. americanum</em>, the American mastodon, is the youngest and best-known species of the genus. They disappeared from North America as part of a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event">mass extinction</a> of most of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleistocene_megafauna">Pleistocene megafauna</a>, widely presumed to have been related to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/overexploitation">overexploitation</a> by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clovis_culture">Clovis hunters</a>, and possibly also to climate change.
The <strong>opossums</strong>, also known by their scientific name <strong>Didelphimorphia</strong> [da&#x26A;&#x2CC;d&#x25B;lf&#x268;&#x2C8;m&#x254;rfi&#x259;]), make up the largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/order_(biology)">order</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/marsupial">marsupial</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Western_Hemisphere">Western Hemisphere</a>, including 103 or more species in 19 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus">genera</a>. Of South American ancestry, they <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_American_Interchange">entered North America</a> following the connection of the two continents. Their unspecialized biology, flexible diet, and reproductive habits make them successful colonizers and survivors in diverse locations and conditions.[date=October 2010]
The <strong>meerkat</strong> or <strong>suricate</strong> (<em>Suricata suricatta</em>) is a small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivora">carnivora</a>n belonging to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mongoose">mongoose</a> family (Herpestidae). It is the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monotypic">only member</a> of the genus <strong><em>Suricata</em></strong>.<ref name="msw3genus">[ id=14000677  heading = <em>Suricata</em>]</ref> Meerkats live in all parts of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kalahari_Desert">Kalahari Desert</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Botswana">Botswana</a>, in much of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Namib_Desert">Namib Desert</a> in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Namibia">Namibia</a> and southwestern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angola">Angola</a>, and in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Africa">South Africa</a>. A group of meerkats is called a &quot;mob&quot;, &quot;gang&quot; or &quot;clan&quot;. A meerkat clan often contains about 20 meerkats, but some super-families have 50 or more members. In captivity, meerkats have an average life span of 12&#x2013;14 years, and about half this in the wild.
<strong>Parrotfishes</strong> are a group of about 95 species traditionally regarded as a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Family_(biology)">family</a> (Scaridae), but now often considered a subfamily (Scarinae) of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wrasse">wrasse</a>s. They are found in  relatively shallow tropical and subtropical oceans throughout the world, displaying their largest <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species_richness">species richness</a> in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a>. They are found in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/coral_reef">coral reef</a>s, rocky coasts, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seagrass">seagrass</a> beds, and play a significant role in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bioerosion">bioerosion</a>.
<strong>Pelicans</strong> are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genus">genus</a> of large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/water_bird">water bird</a>s that makes up the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> Pelecanidae. They are characterised by a long <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/beak">beak</a> and a large <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/throat_pouch">throat pouch</a> used for catching <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/prey">prey</a> and draining water from the scooped up contents before swallowing. They have predominantly pale plumage, the exceptions being the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brown_pelican">brown</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peruvian_pelican">Peruvian pelican</a>s. The bills, pouches and bare facial skin of all species become brightly coloured before the breeding season. The eight living pelican species have a patchy global distribution, ranging <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/latitude">latitudinally</a> from the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropics">tropics</a> to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/temperate_zone">temperate zone</a>, though they are absent from interior South America as well as from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/polar_region">polar region</a>s and the open ocean.
Long thought to be related to <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/frigatebird">frigatebird</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/cormorant">cormorant</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/tropicbird">tropicbird</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sulidae">gannets and boobies</a>, pelicans instead are now known to be most closely related to the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/shoebill">shoebill</a> and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hamerkop">hamerkop</a>, and are placed in the order <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pelecaniformes">Pelecaniformes</a>. <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ibis">Ibis</a>es, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/spoonbill">spoonbill</a>s and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/heron">heron</a>s are more distant relatives, and have been classified in the same order. Fossil evidence of pelicans dates back to at least 30 million years to the remains of a beak very similar to that of modern species recovered from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligocene">Oligocene</a> strata in France. They are thought to have evolved in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Old_World">Old World</a> and spread into the Americas; this is reflected in the relationships within the genus as the eight species divide into Old World and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World">New World</a> lineages.
Pelicans frequent inland and coastal waters where they feed principally on fish, catching them at or near the water surface. They are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/wikt:gregarious">gregarious</a> birds, travelling in flocks, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cooperative_hunting">hunting cooperatively</a> and breeding <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird_colony">colonially</a>. Four white-plumaged species tend to nest on the ground, and four brown or grey-plumaged species nest mainly in trees. The relationship between pelicans and people has often been contentious. The birds have been persecuted because of their perceived competition with commercial and recreational fishing. Their populations have fallen through habitat destruction, disturbance and environmental pollution, and three species are of conservation concern. They also have a long history of cultural significance in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/mythology">mythology</a>, and in Christian and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/heraldry">heraldic</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/iconography">iconography</a>.
<strong>Old World quail</strong> is a collective name for several <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a> of mid-sized <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/bird">bird</a>s in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/pheasant">pheasant</a> family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phasianidae">Phasianidae</a>.
<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_quail">New World quail</a> are also found in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galliformes">Galliformes</a>, but are not in the same family (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Odontophoridae">Odontophoridae</a>). <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buttonquail">Buttonquail</a>s are not closely related at all, but are named for their similar appearance.<ref><span class="reference" data-type="web" data-url="http://www.beautyofbirds.com/quailinfo.html" data-work="Beauty Of Birds">Quail Information, including listing of species and photos</span></ref>  They are presently found in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turnicidae">Turnicidae</a> family in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charadriiformes">Charadriiformes</a>, more closely related to shorebirds, gulls and auks.
The <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/collective_noun">collective noun</a> for a group of quail is <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/flock_(birds)">flock</a></em>, <em>bevy</em> or <em>covey</em>.<ref><a href="http://www.npwrc.usgs.gov/about/faqs/animals/names.htm">USGS &#x2013; Animal Congregations, or What Do You Call a Group</a></ref>
<strong>Mongoose</strong> is the popular English name for 29 of 34<ref>Vaughan, Terry A.; James M. Ryan; Nicholas J. Czaplewski (2010). <em>Mammalogy</em>. Jones &amp; Bartlett Learning. p. 300. ISBN 0-7637-6299-7</ref> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/species">species</a> in the 14 <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/genera">genera</a> of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/family_(biology)">family</a> Herpestidae, which are small <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/carnivora">carnivora</a>ns that are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indigenous_(ecology)">native</a> to southern <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and mainland <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Africa">Africa</a>. The other five species in the family are the four kusimanses in the genus <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crossarchus">Crossarchus</a></em>, and the only species in the genus <em>Suricata</em>: <em>Suricata suricatta</em>, commonly called <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/meerkat">meerkat</a> in English.
Six species in family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eupleridae">Eupleridae</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endemic">endemic</a> to the island of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geography_of_Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, are also called &quot;mongoose&quot; and were originally classified as a genus within the Herpestidae family, but genetic evidence has since shown that they are more closely related to other Madagascar carnivorans in the family <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eupleridae">Eupleridae</a>; they have, since 2006, been classified in the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/subfamily">subfamily</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galidiinae">Galidiinae</a> within Eupleridae.
Herpestidae is placed within the suborder <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Feliformia">Feliformia</a>, together with the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Felidae">cat</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hyena">hyena</a>, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/civet">civet</a> families.
<strong>Lemurs</strong> ([audio=En-us-Lemur.ogg&#x2C8;li&#x2D0;m&#x259;r] [LEEm&#x259;r]) are a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/strepsirrhini">strepsirrhine</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/primate">primate</a>s <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/endemism">endemic</a> to the island of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar">Madagascar</a>. The word &quot;lemur&quot; derives from the word <em><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/lemures">lemures</a></em> (ghosts or spirits) from <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roman_mythology">Roman mythology</a> and was first used to describe a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/slender_loris">slender loris</a> due to its <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nocturnality">nocturnal</a> habits and slow pace, but was later applied to the primates on Madagascar. Although lemurs often are confused with ancestral primates, the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simian">anthropoid</a> primates (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/monkey">monkey</a>s, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ape">ape</a>s, and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/human">human</a>s) did not evolve from them; instead, lemurs merely share morphological and behavioral traits with <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/basal_(phylogenetics)">basal</a> primates. Lemurs arrived in Madagascar around 62 to 65&#xA0;<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year#Symbols_y_and_yr">mya</a> by rafting on mats of vegetation at a time when ocean currents favored <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/oceanic_dispersal">oceanic dispersal</a> to the island. Since that time, lemurs have <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution">evolved</a> to cope with an extremely seasonal environment and their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/adaptation">adaptation</a>s give them a level of diversity that rivals that of all other primate groups. Until shortly after humans arrived on the island around 2,000&#xA0;years ago, there were lemurs as large as a male <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/gorilla">gorilla</a>. Today, there are <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_lemur_species">nearly 100 species of lemurs</a>, and most of those species have been discovered or promoted to full species status since the 1990s; however, lemur <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)">taxonomic classification</a> is controversial and depends on which <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Species_problem">species concept</a> is used.  Even the higher-level taxonomy is disputed, with some experts preferring to place most lemurs within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">infraorder</a> <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemuriformes">Lemuriformes</a>, while others prefer Lemuriformes to contain all living strepsirrhines, placing all lemurs in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">superfamily</a> <strong>Lemuroidea</strong> and all <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/loris">loris</a>es and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/galago">galago</a>s in superfamily <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorisoidea">Lorisoidea</a>.[name=LemuriformesAlthough the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monophyly">monophyletic relationship</a> between lemurs and lorisoids is widely accepted, their clade name is not. The term &quot;lemuriform&quot; is used here because it derives from one popular taxonomy that clumps the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/clade">clade</a> of toothcombed primates into one <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">infraorder</a> and the extinct, non-toothcombed <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adapiformes">adapiforms</a> into another, both within the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxonomic_rank">suborder</a> Strepsirrhini.{{SfnSzalayDelson1980p=149][Cartmill2010p=15] However, another popular alternative taxonomy places the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lorisoidea">lorisoids</a> in their own infraorder, Lorisiformes.[Hartwig2011pp=20&#x2013;21]}}
Ranging in weight from 30&#xA0;g (1.1&#xA0;oz) to 9&#xA0;kg (20&#xA0;lb), lemurs share many common, basal primate traits, such as divergent <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/digit_(anatomy)">digits</a> on their hands and feet and <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/nail_(anatomy)">nails</a> instead of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/claw">claw</a>s (in most species). However, their <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain-to-body_mass_ratio">brain-to-body size ratio</a> is smaller than that of anthropoid primates, and among many other traits they share with other strepsirrhine primates, they have a &quot;wet nose&quot; (<a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/rhinarium">rhinarium</a>). Lemurs are generally the most social of the strepsirrhine primates and communicate more with scents and vocalizations than with visual signals. Many lemur adaptations are in response to Madagascar&apos;s highly seasonal environment. Lemurs have relatively low <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/basal_metabolic_rate">basal metabolic rate</a>s and may exhibit <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/seasonal_breeder">seasonal breeding</a>, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dormancy">dormancy</a> (such as <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/hibernation">hibernation</a> or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/torpor">torpor</a>), or <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/dominance_(ethology)">female social dominance</a>. Most eat a wide variety of fruits and leaves, while some are specialists.  Although many share similar diets, different species of lemur share the same forests by <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/niche_differentiation">differentiating niches</a>.
